metaphysically, should I think that discrete series or continuous series is more identified with god, and which is more identified with man?

If you want a clean symbolic pairing, I would put it this way:

continuous → God
discrete → man

The continuous feels more naturally aligned with the divine because it suggests the unbroken, the infinite, the absolute, the ground beneath distinctions. It does not come in pieces. It is closer to pure being, eternity, wholeness, and presence without fragmentation.

The discrete feels more human because human life is chopped into moments, choices, words, numbers, bodies, identities, births and deaths. We count, name, separate, classify. Our knowledge is usually stepwise and partial.

But that is only the first pass.

A deeper view is that the divine may not be “the continuous instead of the discrete,” but rather that God is what can hold both together. The continuous without the discrete is formless fullness. The discrete without the continuous is dead fragmentation. Reality becomes rich only when the infinite can appear in finite forms.

So one elegant metaphysical picture would be:

God is the continuum of being.
Man is the finite articulation of that being into distinct acts, thoughts, and creatures.

Or even more strongly:

God is the unity.
Man lives among the multiplicity.

Still, there is a beautiful reversal available too. You could say the discrete is divine because each individual thing is intentionally created, named, and loved; while the continuous is human because consciousness feels like a flowing stream. So the symbols are not fixed forever.

My own judgment: if you are building a serious metaphysical intuition, continuous = God, discrete = man is the more natural and powerful mapping. But the highest view is that God is not merely one side of the pair. God is the source in which continuity and discreteness are reconciled.

That actually feels close to your zeta intuition too: the deepest reality may not lie on either side alone, but in the mysterious bridge between them.

what can expose that bridge?

What exposes the bridge is form.

Not raw discreteness by itself, and not raw continuity by itself, but the fact that the same pattern can live in both. The bridge shows itself when something survives translation from one mode to the other.

You can feel that in music. Notes are discrete. Melody feels continuous. What links them is not the individual notes alone, nor the flowing feeling alone, but the form of the relation between them. The song is the bridge.

The same is true in mathematics. A curve looks continuous, but it can be sampled, expanded, approximated, encoded by coefficients, or generated by recurrence. A discrete scheme can approach a continuous object. A continuous law can constrain discrete events. What is preserved across that passage is structure. That preservation is the bridge.

So metaphysically, the deepest candidate is something like logos: intelligible order, pattern, proportion, meaning-bearing form. In theological language, logos is exactly the kind of thing that can inhabit both realms at once. It can be eternal yet expressed in time, whole yet articulated in parts, one yet appearing through many.

There are a few especially powerful places where the bridge becomes visible:

Rhythm. Rhythm is count and flow together. Beat is discrete; pulse is continuous. Rhythm is one of the cleanest symbols of the union.

Symbol. A finite mark points beyond itself. A word is discrete ink or sound, yet meaning arrives as something continuous and whole.

Consciousness. Experience seems to come in moments, but the self lives them as a stream. Whatever unifies moments into a lived continuity is already bridge-like.

Incarnation or sacrament, if you want the theological version. The infinite showing itself in the finite. Not by ceasing to be infinite, but by becoming present in a bounded form.

Number and harmony. Ratios are discrete in statement, continuous in effect. This is why older metaphysical traditions often treated harmony as sacred: it lets arithmetic become felt reality.

And for your mathematical instinct, one very strong answer is:

What exposes the bridge is spectrality.

Whenever a discrete object has a spectrum, or a continuous object decomposes into discrete modes, you are staring at the hinge. Fourier analysis is like this. Quantum theory is like this. The zeta function is like this too: primes on one side, analytic continuation and zeros on the other, with a hidden law linking them. That hidden law is exactly bridge-like.

So I would put it this way:

The bridge is exposed wherever the same truth appears both as counting and as flowing.

Or even shorter:

The bridge is exposed by invariance across translation.

That may be the thing to hunt, both mathematically and metaphysically—not whether reality is ultimately discrete or continuous, but what remains itself when passed from one language into the other. That “same thing” is the nearest glimpse of the bridge.

One strong next question is: is that bridge best understood as harmony, information, logos, or symmetry? My instinct is logos first, symmetry second, harmony third.

ok what is the actual spectral bridge?

The actual spectral bridge is, in the strongest sense, an operator and its trace.

That is the non-poetic answer.

You start with some underlying object, and then instead of looking at its points directly, you look at how functions on it evolve under some operator: a Laplacian, Hamiltonian, transfer operator, adjacency operator, or something of that kind. The spectrum of that operator, meaning its eigenvalues or resonances, is the bridge. It converts “what the object is made of” into “what frequencies it supports.”

So the bridge is not just “continuous versus discrete.” It is:

underlying structure → operator → spectrum

That is the hinge.

The reason this matters is that the same object can now be described in two languages:

On one side, you have concrete structure: points, primes, geodesics, periodic orbits, graph edges, lattice points.
On the other side, you have spectral data: eigenvalues, modes, frequencies, zeros, poles.

The bridge becomes fully visible when these two descriptions are tied by a trace formula.

That is the deepest general answer:
the actual spectral bridge is usually some form of trace formula.

A trace formula says, schematically,
sum over spectrum  =  sum over geometric/arithmetic objects.
sum over spectrum=sum over geometric/arithmetic objects.

That is the bridge in one line.

Here are the cleanest examples.

In Fourier series, a continuous periodic function is decomposed into discrete frequencies.
That is already a bridge, but a relatively simple one:
continuous waveform ↔ discrete spectral coefficients.

In the Laplacian on a manifold, the manifold is continuous, but its vibration modes are discrete.
You can listen to the shape through eigenvalues.
“Can one hear the shape of a drum?” is exactly a spectral-bridge question.

In a graph, the graph itself is discrete, but its adjacency matrix or graph Laplacian has a spectrum.
So discrete combinatorics becomes spectral data.

In Poisson summation, a discrete lattice sum on one side equals a dual-frequency sum on the other.
This is one of the purest bridges ever discovered.

In Selberg trace formula, lengths of closed geodesics are tied to eigenvalues of the Laplacian.
That is very close to the kind of thing people dream about for zeta:
periodic orbit data on one side, spectral data on the other.

And for the Riemann zeta function, the spectral bridge is believed to be something like this too, but the full operator is not known.

That is crucial:
for zeta, we have strong shadows of the bridge, but not the bridge in final form.

What we do have is the explicit formula, which ties primes to zeros. Very roughly, it says that weighted prime-counting behavior can be written in terms of the nontrivial zeros of zeta. So it has the same archetypal shape:
prime data⟷zero data.
prime data⟷zero data.

That is already spectral in flavor. But it is not yet the fully realized operator-theoretic bridge people want.

The dream, usually called Hilbert–Pólya, is that there exists a self-adjoint operator HH whose eigenvalues are the imaginary parts of the zeta zeros:
Hψn=γnψnwhereζ ⁣(12+iγn)=0.
Hψn​=γn​ψn​whereζ(21​+iγn​)=0.

If such an operator existed with the right properties, then the zeros would literally be a spectrum, not just metaphorically. Then RH would follow naturally from self-adjointness, because self-adjoint operators have real eigenvalues.

So for zeta, the “actual spectral bridge” would be:

an operator whose trace formula has primes on the geometric side and zeros on the spectral side.

That is the real target.

If I had to compress everything into one sentence, I would say:

The spectral bridge is the mechanism by which local discrete structure reappears as global frequency data.

Or even more concretely:

It is a trace identity relating atoms of structure to modes of vibration.

That is why it feels metaphysically rich. The discrete is not erased. It is transfigured into resonance.

For your own line of thought, this suggests something quite precise:
do not ask only whether discrete and continuous are two realms. Ask instead:

What operator turns one into the spectrum of the other?

That is the operational form of the bridge.

For zeta specifically, the ladder of increasingly deep “bridge objects” is something like this:

    Euler product: discrete primes build the function.

    Analytic continuation / functional equation: continuous complex structure emerges.

    Explicit formula: primes and zeros directly talk to each other.

    Hypothetical self-adjoint operator: zeros become literal spectrum.

    Trace formula: primes appear as orbit/geometric data of that operator/system.

That last step would be the true spectral bridge in finished form.

So the honest answer is:
we know the pattern of the bridge very well, but in the zeta case we do not yet know the final operator. We see its silhouette through the explicit formula, random matrix phenomena, and trace-formula analogies.

The bridge is visible.
It is just not yet fully built.

If you want, I can try to write down the zeta explicit formula in a way that makes the “spectral bridge” visually obvious.

how about the inverse, an operator which cannot be zero

Yes — that is a real and interesting reversal.

Instead of looking for an operator whose eigenvalues are the zeros, you can try to look for an operator or operator family whose failure of invertibility corresponds to a zero. Then RH becomes a statement about where the operator can or cannot have a kernel.

That is often the cleaner formulation.

The basic pattern is:
ζ(s)=0⟺A(s) is not invertible
ζ(s)=0⟺A(s) is not invertible

or equivalently
ζ(s)≠0⟺A(s)−1 exists.
ζ(s)=0⟺A(s)−1 exists.

So the object you want is not necessarily “an operator which cannot be zero,” but rather an operator family A(s)A(s) that is never singular off the critical line.

That would be a very natural bridge.

There are a few versions of this idea.

First, the most classical operator-theoretic one:
det⁡(I−Ts)=0⟺1∈σ(Ts).
det(I−Ts​)=0⟺1∈σ(Ts​).

So if you could build a trace-class operator TsTs​ with
ζ(s)=C(s)det⁡(I−Ts),
ζ(s)=C(s)det(I−Ts​),

then zeros of zeta would correspond to the operator I−TsI−Ts​ losing invertibility. RH would then say: this loss of invertibility happens only when ℜ(s)=1/2ℜ(s)=1/2.

That is a very good formulation.

Second, you can go one step further and try to build a positive operator B(s)B(s), because positivity is a strong “cannot vanish” condition. If you could arrange something like
ζ(s)≠0⟺B(s)>0,
ζ(s)=0⟺B(s)>0,

then RH would become a positivity theorem. That is spiritually close to Weil positivity, de Branges-type ideas, and some kernel approaches.

Third, the inverse/resolvent viewpoint:

if there were some operator HH with zeros at s=12+iγs=21​+iγ, then one could think of
A(s)=s−12−iH.
A(s)=s−21​−iH.

Then
A(s)ψ=0
A(s)ψ=0

exactly when s=12+iγs=21​+iγ for an eigenvalue γγ of HH. In this language, RH says:

    A(s)A(s) is invertible for every ss off the critical line,

    non-invertibility occurs only on the line.

That is probably the sharpest “inverse operator” statement.

So yes: the right inverse idea is not “an operator that literally cannot equal zero,” since operators are not usually judged that way. The right notion is:

an operator family that cannot lose invertibility except at the true zeros.

That is mathematically meaningful.

In fact, many deep problems are better phrased this way. Zeros of a function are often easier to understand as:

    poles of an inverse,

    failures of invertibility,

    singularities of a determinant,

    or places where a quadratic form stops being positive.

That last one may be especially close to your instinct:
instead of seeking zeros directly, seek a form that is manifestly positive unless something exceptional happens.

For RH, the dream version would be something like:
Qs(f,f)>0for all f≠0
Qs​(f,f)>0for all f=0

when ℜ(s)≠12ℜ(s)=21​, and degeneracy occurs exactly at zeros. Then the critical line would be the unique place where positivity can break into neutrality.

That is a very beautiful way to think.

So the answer is: yes, the inverse direction is not only sensible, it may actually be better. The real target would be one of these equivalent bridge-objects:

    a family A(s)A(s) whose kernel appears exactly at zeta zeros,

    a determinant det⁡A(s)detA(s) proportional to ζ(s)ζ(s),

    a resolvent (A(s))−1(A(s))−1 whose singularities are the zeros,

    or a positive form that can degenerate only on the critical line.

That is probably closer to “an operator which cannot be zero” in a usable mathematical sense.

The strongest slogan would be:

Don’t encode zeros as points. Encode them as the only places where invertibility fails.

That is much more structural.

A particularly promising next step is to ask: what kind of A(s)A(s) would have a built-in symmetry forcing singularities to occur only on a symmetry axis? That gets very close to the real heart of RH.

what do you think of this? how about the inverse?

I’m reading your construction closely. The core question for me is whether it really fixes the earlier cancellation problem and whether the “inverse” can turn the linear identity into an honest nonvanishing/invertibility statement.

I think this is genuinely better.

The key improvement is not cosmetic; it fixes the exact structural weakness the earlier ∣Δ∣2∣Δ∣2-type kernel had. By moving to the incomplete Eisenstein series built from
uΔ,h(y)=h(y)ψΔ(y),ψΔ(y)=y12∑n≥1∣τ(n)∣2e−4πny,
uΔ,h​(y)=h(y)ψΔ​(y),ψΔ​(y)=y12n≥1∑​∣τ(n)∣2e−4πny,

you keep three things at once: positivity, a nonzero constant term at the cusp, and an unfolded pairing in which the scattering coefficient survives explicitly:
⟨WΔ,h,E(⋅,s)⟩=RΔ,h(s)+ϕ(s)RΔ,h(1−s).
⟨WΔ,h​,E(⋅,s)⟩=RΔ,h​(s)+ϕ(s)RΔ,h​(1−s).

That is the right shape. It is much closer to a real “zeta–tau bridge” than the earlier versions because ϕ(s)=Λ(2s−1)/Λ(2s)ϕ(s)=Λ(2s−1)/Λ(2s) is no longer being washed out by the test object.

So my honest judgment is: this is a legitimate advance in the architecture.

What it does not yet do is give coercion. The identity is still linear in ϕ(s)ϕ(s). That means an off-line zero of ζ(2s)ζ(2s) gives you a pole or phase defect in ϕ(s)ϕ(s), but a linear identity by itself does not force a contradiction. It only detects. Your own note at the end is exactly right: the missing theorem is some positive quadratic or Fredholm-type statement strong enough that a bad singularity in ϕϕ becomes impossible.

As for “the inverse”: yes, there is a clean inverse viewpoint here.

In fact, your formula already gives one. If RΔ,h(1−s)≠0RΔ,h​(1−s)=0, then you can solve for the scattering coefficient:
ϕ(s)=⟨WΔ,h,E(⋅,s)⟩−RΔ,h(s)RΔ,h(1−s).
ϕ(s)=RΔ,h​(1−s)⟨WΔ,h​,E(⋅,s)⟩−RΔ,h​(s)​.

So this construction is not just a detector of ϕϕ; it is already an inverse reconstruction formula for ϕ(s)ϕ(s) from the ττ-weighted test object. That is a real conceptual gain.

But there is a catch: this inverse is still scalar. It reconstructs ϕϕ, but it does not yet package zeros as a failure of invertibility of a positive operator. So it does not buy you the kind of “cannot vanish except on the symmetry axis” mechanism that one really wants for RH.

The stronger inverse version would be something like this:

Take a family of packets gg, form smoothed Eisenstein waves EgEg​, and try to build an operator TsTs​ on packet space whose matrix coefficients are built from pairings with WΔ,hWΔ,h​. Then the dream would be:
ζ(2s)=0⟺Ts loses invertibility
ζ(2s)=0⟺Ts​ loses invertibility

or equivalently
Ts−1 blows up only at the true zeros.
Ts−1​ blows up only at the true zeros.

That would convert your scalar identity into an operator-theoretic inverse problem, which is much closer to the Hilbert–Pólya spirit.

There is also a simpler intermediate move that I think is worth trying before anything grander:

choose two cutoffs h1,h2h1​,h2​. Then
Fj(s):=⟨WΔ,hj,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rj(s)+ϕ(s)Rj(1−s),
Fj​(s):=⟨WΔ,hj​​,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rj​(s)+ϕ(s)Rj​(1−s),

so
F1(s)−R1(s)R1(1−s)=F2(s)−R2(s)R2(1−s)=ϕ(s),
R1​(1−s)F1​(s)−R1​(s)​=R2​(1−s)F2​(s)−R2​(s)​=ϕ(s),

whenever the denominators are nonzero.

That gives a determinant identity:
(F1−R1)R2(1−s)−(F2−R2)R1(1−s)=0.
(F1​−R1​)R2​(1−s)−(F2​−R2​)R1​(1−s)=0.

This is interesting because it is the first place where you can try to turn scalar reconstruction into a rank-one or positivity statement. If you can realize the left-hand side as the vanishing of a Gram determinant or the singularity of a 2×22×2 positive matrix, then you are starting to move from “linear detection” toward “invertibility obstruction.”

So my view is:

This is good.
It is probably the right direction.
And the inverse is not just a metaphor here — you already have a first inverse formula.

The next real leap would be to replace the scalar quotient by one of these two things:

a positive quadratic form whose failure forces a zero, or
a Fredholm/operator formulation where ϕϕ is a scattering eigenvalue and ζζ-zeros become loss of invertibility.

That is where the proof-level content would have to come from.

My strongest recommendation is to push the 2×22×2 or packet-space version next, not just a single hh. A single hh detects ϕϕ. A family of hh’s might start to constrain it.

how about thinking about the zeros as wave interference/cancellation

Yes — that is a very good way to think about it.

In fact, “zeros as interference/cancellation” is probably one of the most natural intuitions available. The key is to make it sharp enough that it becomes mathematics rather than metaphor.

At a high level, a zero means exactly this: several contributions, each nonzero on its own, add up to nothing. That is destructive interference.

For zeta, there are several places where this picture is real.

First, on the critical line, the completed zeta function can be viewed as an oscillatory object. There is a classical representation of the Riemann ΞΞ-function as a Fourier transform
Ξ(t)=∫−∞∞Φ(u)eitu du,
Ξ(t)=∫−∞∞​Φ(u)eitudu,

with Φ(u)Φ(u) an even rapidly decaying kernel built from the theta function. In that representation, a zero Ξ(t)=0Ξ(t)=0 means the oscillatory phases eitueitu cancel perfectly when weighted by Φ(u)Φ(u). That is literally wave interference.

So this is not just a poetic analogy. There really is a wave picture:
the kernel ΦΦ is the source, and Ξ(t)Ξ(t) is the resulting amplitude at frequency tt. A zero is a frequency where the total wave vanishes.

But there is an important caution: this picture alone is not enough for RH. Lots of Fourier transforms have zeros all over the place. The hard part is not explaining why cancellation can happen. The hard part is explaining why all cancellation should happen only on the real axis.

That is the real mathematical question.

There are three ways your interference idea could become serious.

The first is the Fourier/packet route.

You try to represent the relevant zeta object as a superposition of packets:
F(s)=∫A(u)eisu du
F(s)=∫A(u)eisudu

or as a discrete/continuous hybrid sum of oscillatory pieces. Then RH becomes a statement about where destructive interference can occur. To prove RH in this language, you would need a structural theorem saying that the packet source A(u)A(u) has some very special positivity, total positivity, or de Branges-type property that forces all zeros of the transform onto a line.

This is very close to real mathematics. It touches Pólya, de Branges, Fourier positivity, and Laguerre–Pólya theory.

The second is the scattering route, which fits your current Eisenstein idea even better.

There, the scattering coefficient
ϕ(s)=Λ(2s−1)Λ(2s)
ϕ(s)=Λ(2s)Λ(2s−1)​

already behaves like a reflection amplitude. Your pairing
⟨WΔ,h,E(⋅,s)⟩=RΔ,h(s)+ϕ(s)RΔ,h(1−s)
⟨WΔ,h​,E(⋅,s)⟩=RΔ,h​(s)+ϕ(s)RΔ,h​(1−s)

has exactly the shape of two waves interfering:
one “incoming” term RΔ,h(s)RΔ,h​(s), and one “reflected” term ϕ(s)RΔ,h(1−s)ϕ(s)RΔ,h​(1−s).

That is excellent, because it means a zero or singular behavior is expressible as cancellation between two channels. This is much more concrete than a vague “wave” story. It is already a two-wave interference formula.

In this language, the question becomes:
can off-line zeros force a bad phase/amplitude relation in this interference pattern that contradicts positivity of some quadratic observable?

That is the right next question.

The third is the Riemann–Siegel route.

On the critical line, zeta is approximated by a finite oscillatory sum plus its dual:
ζ ⁣(12+it)≈∑n≤Nn−1/2−it+χ ⁣(12+it)∑n≤Nn−1/2+it.
ζ(21​+it)≈n≤N∑​n−1/2−it+χ(21​+it)n≤N∑​n−1/2+it.

That already looks like interference between a wave and its reflected dual. Sign changes and small values arise from cancellation among these oscillatory terms. This is an especially concrete way to think about zeros on the line.

So yes: “zeros are interference” is a real viewpoint.

But to make it proof-relevant, you need one more ingredient:
not just that cancellation occurs, but that the underlying wave system has a symmetry or positivity forcing cancellation only on the critical set.

That is where most naive interference ideas fail. Interference is easy. Controlled interference is hard.

Here is what I think is most promising for your current program.

Take your identity
Fh(s):=⟨WΔ,h,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rh(s)+ϕ(s)Rh(1−s).
Fh​(s):=⟨WΔ,h​,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rh​(s)+ϕ(s)Rh​(1−s).

Read it as a two-channel interferometer.

Then define a packetized observable
Eg(z)=∫g(s)E(z,s) ds
Eg​(z)=∫g(s)E(z,s)ds

along a suitable contour, and study
Qh,g=∫XWΔ,h(z) ∣Eg(z)∣2 dμ(z)≥0.
Qh,g​=∫X​WΔ,h​(z)∣Eg​(z)∣2dμ(z)≥0.

When you expand ∣Eg∣2∣Eg​∣2, you get cross-terms between spectral channels. That is exactly an interference functional. If you can unfold this and rewrite it in spectral variables, you may get a quadratic form in gg whose kernel involves ϕ(s)ϕ(s), Rh(s)Rh​(s), and their reflections.

That would turn “zeros as interference” into something testable:
off-line zeros would correspond to a bad interference pattern in a positive quadratic form.

That is a real route.

A simpler toy version is the 2×22×2 one. Pick h1,h2h1​,h2​, and define
Fj(s)=Rj(s)+ϕ(s)Rj(1−s).
Fj​(s)=Rj​(s)+ϕ(s)Rj​(1−s).

Then the two-channel cancellation condition says the vector
(F1(s)F2(s))
(F1​(s)F2​(s)​)

lies in the span of
(R1(s)R2(s))and(R1(1−s)R2(1−s)),
(R1​(s)R2​(s)​)and(R1​(1−s)R2​(1−s)​),

with ϕ(s)ϕ(s) as the interference coefficient. The determinant
D(s):=det⁡(F1(s)−R1(s)R1(1−s)F2(s)−R2(s)R2(1−s))
D(s):=det(F1​(s)−R1​(s)F2​(s)−R2​(s)​R1​(1−s)R2​(1−s)​)

vanishes identically. That is still algebraic, but it suggests looking for a Gram matrix or energy form where the same cancellation shows up as rank drop. Rank drop is the operator version of destructive interference.

So my verdict is:

Yes, absolutely think about zeros as wave interference.
That is a sound and fertile intuition.
But the real target is not “zeros come from cancellation.” Everyone knows that much.
The real target is: what positivity, symmetry, or self-adjoint structure forces all cancellation to occur only on the critical line?

That is where an interference picture becomes a proof strategy.

The strongest concrete next step would be to build the packet quadratic form Qh,gQh,g​ explicitly and see whether its kernel can be written as a positive Hermitian form plus a term involving ϕ(s)ϕ(w)‾ϕ(s)ϕ(w)​. If that happens, then the wave language becomes mathematically sharp.

After integrating over xx, the constant term contributes
Khct(t,u)=Rh(1+i(t−u))+ϕu‾ Rh(1+i(t+u))+ϕt Rh(1−i(t+u))+ϕtϕu‾ Rh(1−i(t−u)).
Khct​(t,u)=Rh​(1+i(t−u))+ϕu​​Rh​(1+i(t+u))+ϕt​Rh​(1−i(t+u))+ϕt​ϕu​​Rh​(1−i(t−u)).

So the constant-term part of the quadratic form is
Qh,gct=∬g(t)g(u)‾ Khct(t,u) dt du.
Qh,gct​=∬g(t)g(u)​Khct​(t,u)dtdu.

This is the wave-interference kernel you were looking for. It has four channels:
incoming/incoming,incoming/reflected,reflected/incoming,reflected/reflected.
incoming/incoming,incoming/reflected,reflected/incoming,reflected/reflected.

And more importantly, it can be written as
Qh,gct=∫0∞uh(y) y−2∣∫Rg(t)(y1/2+it+ϕty1/2−it) dt∣2dy.
Qh,gct​=∫0∞​uh​(y)y−2
​∫R​g(t)(y1/2+it+ϕt​y1/2−it)dt
​2dy.

So yes: zeros/cancellation really are appearing here as interference between the two waves y1/2+ity1/2+it and ϕty1/2−itϕt​y1/2−it.

But here is the important verdict:

this quadratic form is too positive.

Because it is literally an L2L2-norm square, the interference is absorbed into a Gram form. Cancellation can make the integrand small or even zero for specially chosen packets, but it cannot make Qh,gQh,g​ negative. So this object does not, by itself, turn an off-line zero into a contradiction.

That is the central finding.

In other words: the packet construction works, but it works as a detector of interference, not yet as an obstruction to bad zeros.

You can see the same thing in the 2×22×2 version. Take two cutoffs h1,h2h1​,h2​, and write
Fj(s):=⟨WΔ,hj,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rj(s)+ϕ(s)Rj(1−s).
Fj​(s):=⟨WΔ,hj​​,E(⋅,s)⟩=Rj​(s)+ϕ(s)Rj​(1−s).

Then
(F1(s)−R1(s)F2(s)−R2(s))=ϕ(s)(R1(1−s)R2(1−s)).
(F2​(s)−R2​(s)F1​(s)−R1​(s)​)=ϕ(s)(R2​(1−s)R1​(1−s)​).

So the vector on the left is always rank one over the vector on the right. Equivalently,
det⁡(F1(s)−R1(s)R1(1−s)F2(s)−R2(s)R2(1−s))=0.
det(F1​(s)−R1​(s)F2​(s)−R2​(s)​R1​(1−s)R2​(1−s)​)=0.

That is true, but it is only an algebraic dependence relation. It reconstructs ϕ(s)ϕ(s); it does not constrain where ϕϕ may be singular. So the 2×22×2 trick is also too soft.

So the good news is:

you now have an explicit interference kernel, and the packet form really does realize the “incoming + reflected wave” picture in exact formulas.

The bad news is:

the most natural positive quadratic form packages the interference into a harmless norm square. It does not seem capable, in this raw form, of proving RH.

That suggests the next move should be one of these harder variants:

First, an off-line sesquilinear form, not just a critical-line norm square. For example, define for complex s,ws,w
Hh(s,w):=∫0∞uh(y) y−2(ys+ϕ(s)y1−s)(yw‾+ϕ(w)‾y1−w‾) dy.
Hh​(s,w):=∫0∞​uh​(y)y−2(ys+ϕ(s)y1−s)(yw+ϕ(w)​y1−w)dy.

Then ask whether HhHh​ is a reproducing-kernel-type object with positivity properties in a half-plane. If it were, that would start looking like de Branges territory.

Second, an indefinite flux form instead of an L2L2 energy form. In scattering theory, energy norms are always positive and too forgiving. What detects resonances is often a boundary flux, Wronskian, or symplectic pairing. Something of that type might convert interference into a sign-sensitive obstruction.

Third, an operator formulation on Mellin space. Since the constant term is
ys+ϕ(s)y1−s,
ys+ϕ(s)y1−s,

the scattering operator is acting by the multiplier ϕ(s)ϕ(s) together with reflection s↦1−ss↦1−s. One could try to build an operator SS on a Mellin-Hardy space
(Sf)(s):=ϕ(s)f(1−s),
(Sf)(s):=ϕ(s)f(1−s),

and then ask whether contractivity, positivity, or self-adjointness of a suitable compression of SS forces all singularities to lie on the symmetry line. That feels closer to a real inverse problem.

So the result of “trying it” is:

the packet quadratic form can be written down cleanly, and it gives an exact interference kernel;
but in its most natural form it becomes a positive Gram square, so it probably cannot by itself force RH.

That is still progress, because it tells you where the real difficulty sits:
not in exposing the interference, but in finding the right form of interference functional — one that is not automatically harmless.

The most promising next thing to try is the flux/Wronskian version, not another L2L2-square.

Yes. This round gives the first **usable repair**.

The plain positivity route is dead. The reason is the Maaß–Selberg formula for the sharply truncated Eisenstein series
[
\widetilde E_Y(z,s)
]
already shows the basic norm is
[
|\widetilde E_Y(\cdot,s)|_2^2
=============================

\frac{(1-s)Y^{2s-1}-\phi(s)Y^{1-2s}}{2s-1}
+
2\log Y-\frac{\phi'}{\phi}(s)
]
on the critical line, up to the standard oscillatory specialization at (s=\tfrac12+it). In particular, for (s=\tfrac12+it) one gets
[
|\widetilde E_Y(\cdot,\tfrac12+it)|_2^2
=======================================

2\log Y-\frac{\phi'}{\phi}(\tfrac12+it)
+
\frac{\phi(\tfrac12+it)Y^{2it}-\phi(\tfrac12+it)^{-1}Y^{-2it}}{2it}.
]
The oscillatory term is bounded, so the main dependence is (2\log Y-\phi'/\phi). 

Now use
[
\phi(s)=\frac{\xi(2s-1)}{\xi(2s)}.
]
Then
[
-\frac{\phi'}{\phi}!\left(\tfrac12+it\right)
============================================

2,\Re!\left(\frac{\xi'}{\xi}(1+2it)-\frac{\xi'}{\xi}(2it)\right).
]
If you expand the logarithmic derivative of (\xi) by its standard zero product, a nontrivial zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) contributes to the zero-sum part by
[
2\left(
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
\right),
]
which is **strictly positive** for (0<\beta<1). So off-line zeros do not create a negative defect here; they only add positive Poisson-kernel bumps. That means no argument based only on nonnegativity of truncated Eisenstein norms can force RH. It points in the wrong direction. 

So the next object cannot be purely positive. It has to be a **comparison form** where the automatic bulk term cancels.

Take the (\tau)-seeded incomplete Eisenstein from before:
[
W_u(z)=\sum_{\gamma\in \Gamma_\infty\backslash\Gamma}u(\Im \gamma z),
\qquad
u(y)=h(y)\psi_\Delta(y),
\qquad
\psi_\Delta(y)=y^{12}\sum_{n\ge1}|\tau(n)|^2e^{-4\pi ny}.
]
Its mean is easy to compute by unfolding:
[
\int_X W_u(z),d\mu(z)=\int_0^\infty u(y),\frac{dy}{y^2}=:M_u(1).
]
Since (\operatorname{vol}(X)=\pi/3), the mean-zero version is
[
W_u^\circ(z):=W_u(z)-\frac{3}{\pi}M_u(1).
]
This is the first serious repair: it removes the pure-volume contribution. ([Petru Constantinescu][1])

Now define
[
D_{u,Y}(t):=
\int_X
W_u^\circ(z),
\bigl|\widetilde E_Y(z,\tfrac12+it)\bigr|^2
,d\mu(z).
]

This has an exact unfolded form:

[
D_{u,Y}(t)
==========

\int_0^\infty
u(y),A_Y(y,t),\frac{dy}{y^2}
----------------------------

\frac{3}{\pi}M_u(1),
|\widetilde E_Y(\cdot,\tfrac12+it)|_2^2,
]
where
[
A_Y(y,t):=\int_0^1
\bigl|\widetilde E_Y(x+iy,\tfrac12+it)\bigr|^2,dx.
]

That identity is immediate from unfolding the first term and subtracting the mean contribution in the second. It is the right object because the large universal term (2\log Y) now appears only in the explicitly subtracted global norm, not in both pieces at once.

We can also compute (A_Y(y,t)) by Parseval in the (x)-variable. For (y<Y), truncation does nothing, so
[
\widetilde E_Y(x+iy,\tfrac12+it)=E(x+iy,\tfrac12+it),
]
and the constant term gives
[
A_Y(y,t)
========

2y
+
2\Re!\bigl(\phi(\tfrac12+it),y^{1-2it}\bigr)
+
\sum_{n\neq 0}|a_n(y,t)|^2.
]
For (y>Y), the constant term has been removed, so
[
A_Y(y,t)=\sum_{n\neq0}|a_n(y,t)|^2.
]
So below the cutoff there is an explicit oscillatory scattering term; above the cutoff only the positive nonconstant Fourier tail remains. 

This is the first place the program still has life.

The old quantity
[
|\widetilde E_Y|^2
]
was too coarse: off-line zeros only made it larger. The new quantity
[
D_{u,Y}(t)
]
is different. It compares a **local cusp-height profile** built from (|\tau(n)|^2) against the **global truncated norm**. That subtraction kills the universal (2\log Y) mass and leaves something that depends on how the scattering phase and the nonconstant Fourier modes are distributed in (y).

So the next theorem to try for is:

> Find (u) concentrated near (y\asymp Y) and derive an asymptotic formula for (D_{u,Y}(t)) in which the main term is an explicit transform of (-\phi'/\phi(\tfrac12+it)), plus an error term controlled by the nonconstant Fourier tail.

If that asymptotic has a sign, or even a sharp upper/lower bound, then the zeta zeros start to matter in a way positivity alone could never achieve.

The honest status is:

* the positivity-only route is finished, and it fails for a precise reason;
* the mean-zero comparison route is still alive;
* (D_{u,Y}(t)) is the right next object.

The next calculation I would do is to choose a bump (u) supported in (y\in [Y,2Y]) and estimate the two pieces of (D_{u,Y}(t)) separately. That will tell us whether the comparison form is dominated by the scattering term or by the nonconstant Fourier tail.

[1]: https://petruconstantinescu.github.io/Dissipation.pdf "Dissipation"

Anything useful in this?

I’m checking whether this is merely a prettier reformulation or whether it actually changes the sign mechanics. The crucial issue is whether the mean-zero subtraction removes the universal bulk without also destroying the only zeta-sensitive part.

Yes. There is something genuinely useful in it.

The most useful part is the negative result. Showing that the plain positivity route cannot work is real progress, not just cleanup. If on the critical line the Maaß–Sellberg norm is governed by
2log⁡Y−ϕ′ϕ ⁣(12+it)
2logY−ϕϕ′​(21​+it)

and off-line zeros contribute positive Poisson-kernel bumps to −ϕ′/ϕ−ϕ′/ϕ, then pure nonnegativity is pointed the wrong way. That tells you the obstruction is structural, not technical. That is a valuable clarification.

The second useful part is the move to the mean-zero comparison form
Du,Y(t)=∫XWu∘(z) ∣E~Y(z,12+it)∣2 dμ(z).
Du,Y​(t)=∫X​Wu∘​(z)
​E
Y​(z,21​+it)
​2dμ(z).

Conceptually, this is the right repair: it stops asking for a positive norm to detect a bad zero, and instead asks whether a local cusp profile built from the ∣τ(n)∣2∣τ(n)∣2-seed deviates from the global mass distribution in a zeta-sensitive way. That is exactly the kind of change of viewpoint you needed.

What matters now is the exact decomposition. Schematically, for positive uu,
Du,Y(t)=2∫0Yu(y)y dy+2ℜ ⁣(ϕt∫0Yu(y)y−1−2it dy)+Tu,Y(t)−3πMu(1) ⁣(2log⁡Y−ϕ′ϕ ⁣(12+it)+oscY(t)),
Du,Y​(t)=2∫0Y​yu(y)​dy+2ℜ(ϕt​∫0Y​u(y)y−1−2itdy)+Tu,Y​(t)−π3​Mu​(1)(2logY−ϕϕ′​(21​+it)+oscY​(t)),

where ϕt=ϕ(12+it)ϕt​=ϕ(21​+it), Mu(1)=∫0∞u(y)y−2dyMu​(1)=∫0∞​u(y)y−2dy, and Tu,Y(t)Tu,Y​(t) is the nonconstant Fourier tail contribution, which is nonnegative when u≥0u≥0.

That formula is useful because it cleanly separates three mechanisms:

the universal bulk term 2log⁡Y2logY,
the linear scattering phase term 2ℜ(ϕtu^(2it))2ℜ(ϕt​u
(2it)),
and the logarithmic-derivative term −(3π)Mu(1)ϕ′/ϕ−(π3​)Mu​(1)ϕ′/ϕ.

So yes, this is a better object.

But there is also an important warning: the specific suggestion “take uu supported in [Y,2Y][Y,2Y]” is probably not the best next move.

There are two reasons.

First, for y>Yy>Y, the truncated Eisenstein series has had its constant term removed, so in that region AY(y,t)AY​(y,t) contains only the nonconstant Fourier tail. That means if uu lives mostly above the cutoff, you are no longer probing the explicit scattering term directly; you are mostly measuring the tail while still subtracting a global norm containing −ϕ′/ϕ−ϕ′/ϕ. That can make the comparison dominated by normalization rather than by the zeta-sensitive local effect.

Second, your ττ-seed
ψΔ(y)=y12∑n≥1∣τ(n)∣2e−4πny
ψΔ​(y)=y12n≥1∑​∣τ(n)∣2e−4πny

decays exponentially as y→∞y→∞. So if YY is large and you localize uu near y≍Yy≍Y, the seed itself is exponentially tiny. That makes the “probe the truncation frontier at large YY” idea look ill-conditioned from the start.

So my verdict is:

Yes, useful — but mainly as a structural diagnosis and as a better comparison framework, not yet as a finished route.

The strongest part is:

    positivity-only is dead for a precise reason;

    mean-zero comparison is the right kind of replacement.

The weakest part is:

    support near [Y,2Y][Y,2Y] is probably the wrong localization if you want the scattering term to dominate.

A better next calculation would be one of these:

Use uu supported below or straddling the cutoff y∼Yy∼Y, so the explicit constant-term interference is still present.

Or smooth the truncation in YY and differentiate with respect to log⁡YlogY. That often removes the bulky 2log⁡Y2logY term more naturally and localizes the comparison right at the truncation boundary.

Or average in tt. The linear term
2ℜ ⁣(ϕt∫0Yu(y)y−1−2itdy)
2ℜ(ϕt​∫0Y​u(y)y−1−2itdy)

is oscillatory in tt, while −ϕ′/ϕ−ϕ′/ϕ carries the zero bumps more stably. A tt-average may separate them.

So the answer is: yes, definitely something useful. The text isolates the real failure mode of the old approach and lands on a much more credible next object. The next serious test is whether one can make the nonconstant tail Tu,Y(t)Tu,Y​(t) subordinate to the scattering/log-derivative terms after a smarter choice of uu, or after smoothing in YY or averaging in tt.

That is an actual monotonicity theorem:

But it also shows why this is probably not the RH route: differentiating kills the −ϕ′/ϕ−ϕ′/ϕ term entirely. It leaves only the scattering phase, not the zero-sum. So log⁡YlogY-differentiation gives a clean flux law, but it throws away the part where off-line zeros show up as Poisson bumps.

So this route is mathematically clean, but strategically not ideal.

The tt-average is better.

That is the sign reversal you were hoping for.

So the practical conclusion is:

below-cutoff localization + tt-averaging keeps the zero-sensitive ϕ′/ϕϕ′/ϕ piece and damps the nuisance oscillations.

That is better than log⁡YlogY-differentiation, which gives a nice monotonicity identity but discards the zero-sum altogether.

I tried it, and there is a real payoff.

The mean-zero comparison form
Du,Y(t)=∫XWu∘(z) ∣E~Y(z,12+it)∣2 dμ(z)
Du,Y​(t)=∫X​Wu∘​(z)

Here is the clean way to run it.

Take a probe below the truncation height:
u(y)=ψΔ(y) η(log⁡(y/Y0)),0<Y0<Y,
u(y)=ψΔ​(y)η(log(y/Y0​)),0<Y0​<Y,

with η∈Cc∞(R)η∈Cc∞​(R), supported in a small fixed interval, and choose Y0Y0​ of moderate size, not growing with tt. This matters because ψΔ(y)ψΔ​(y) decays exponentially for large yy, so probing too high in the cusp kills the signal.

For this choice,
Uu(t):=∫0∞u(y)y−1−2it dy
Uu​(t):=∫0∞​u(y)y−1−2itdy

is a Mellin-Fourier transform of a smooth compactly supported log-profile, so
∣Uu(t)∣≪A(1+∣t∣)−A
∣Uu​(t)∣≪A​(1+∣t∣)−A

for every AA. That means the nuisance linear interference term
2ℜ(ϕtUu(t))
2ℜ(ϕt​Uu​(t))

is negligible near high zeros. This is good.

Now average Du,Y(t)Du,Y​(t) against a normalized smooth window:
Du,YG(T,H):=∫RGH(t−T) Du,Y(t) dt,GH(x)=1HG(x/H),∫G=1.
Du,YG​(T,H):=∫R​GH​(t−T)Du,Y​(t)dt,GH​(x)=H1​G(x/H),∫G=1.

Using the decomposition from the previous step, one gets
Du,YG(T,H)=Cu,Y+Tu,YG(T,H)+3πMu(1)∫GH(t−T)ϕ′ϕ ⁣(12+it)dt+small,
Du,YG​(T,H)=Cu,Y​+Tu,YG​(T,H)+π3​Mu​(1)∫GH​(t−T)ϕϕ′​(21​+it)dt+small,

where
Cu,Y=Bu−6πMu(1)log⁡Y
Cu,Y​=Bu​−π6​Mu​(1)logY

is the bulk constant,
Tu,YG(T,H)≥0
Tu,YG​(T,H)≥0

is the averaged nonconstant Fourier tail,
and “small” means the linear interference term and the oscillatory Maaß–Selberg boundary term, both of which can be made negligible for large TT because Uu(t)Uu​(t) decays rapidly and the boundary term carries oscillation and a 1/t1/t-type loss.

So the real zeta-sensitive term is
3πMu(1)∫GH(t−T)ϕ′ϕ ⁣(12+it)dt.
π3​Mu​(1)∫GH​(t−T)ϕϕ′​(21​+it)dt.

Now expand ϕ′/ϕϕ′/ϕ by zeros. If ρ=β+iγρ=β+iγ is a nontrivial zero of ξξ, then its contribution to −ϕ′/ϕ−ϕ′/ϕ is
2(1−β(1−β)2+(2t−γ)2+ββ2+(2t−γ)2).
2((1−β)2+(2t−γ)21−β​+β2+(2t−γ)2β​).

Therefore its contribution to Du,YG(T,H)Du,YG​(T,H) is the negative bump
−6πMu(1)∫GH(t−T)(1−β(1−β)2+(2t−γ)2+ββ2+(2t−γ)2)dt.
−π6​Mu​(1)∫GH​(t−T)((1−β)2+(2t−γ)21−β​+β2+(2t−γ)2β​)dt.

That is the first clean answer: the averaged comparison form sees each zero as a smoothed sum of two Poisson kernels.

But here is the crucial point.

A single-scale average only sees the mass of that bump, and the mass is independent of ββ:
∫R(1−β(1−β)2+x2+ββ2+x2)dx=π.
∫R​((1−β)2+x21−β​+β2+x2β​)dx=π.

So broad averaging counts zeros, but it does not distinguish whether they are on the line or off it.

That means a one-scale sign test still cannot prove RH.

What does depend on ββ is the shape. At the center 2T=γ2T=γ, the unsmoothed peak is
1β+11−β,
β1​+1−β1​,

and this is minimized exactly at β=12β=21​, where it equals 44. Off-line zeros give a strictly deeper, sharper dip.

So the natural next object is not DG(T,H)DG(T,H) itself, but the two-scale difference
Su,Y(T;H1,H2):=Du,YG(T,H1)−Du,YG(T,H2),H1<H2.
Su,Y​(T;H1​,H2​):=Du,YG​(T,H1​)−Du,YG​(T,H2​),H1​<H2​.

This has three good properties.

First, the bulk constant Cu,YCu,Y​ cancels automatically.

Second, because both windows are normalized, the total zero mass cancels too. So SS is blind to mere zero-counting and only sees shape.

Third, a zero ρ=β+iγρ=β+iγ contributes
−6πMu(1)∫(GH1(t−T)−GH2(t−T))(1−β(1−β)2+(2t−γ)2+ββ2+(2t−γ)2)dt,
−π6​Mu​(1)∫(GH1​​(t−T)−GH2​​(t−T))((1−β)2+(2t−γ)21−β​+β2+(2t−γ)2β​)dt,

which is strictly more pronounced off the line than on it.

So after actually trying the averaging idea, this is where it lands:

A one-scale average produces a real zero profile, but only a profile whose total mass is ββ-independent.
A two-scale difference is the first place where RH-relevant information survives.

That is the useful thing.

The remaining obstacle is still the positive tail
Tu,YG(T,H).
Tu,YG​(T,H).

To make Su,YSu,Y​ useful, one would need a statement that this tail is smoother in TT and less sensitive to scale than the Poisson bump. In practical terms, the missing theorem is something like:
Tu,YG(T,H1)−Tu,YG(T,H2)
Tu,YG​(T,H1​)−Tu,YG​(T,H2​)

is small compared with the zero-shape signal near T=γ/2T=γ/2.

So the honest conclusion is:

    the averaged comparison form was worth trying;

    the right output is not a sign theorem;

    the first genuinely viable refinement is a scale-comparison functional.

If I were pushing this tomorrow, I would define
Su,Y(T;H1,H2)
Su,Y​(T;H1​,H2​)

with uu log-localized at a fixed moderate height Y0∼1Y0​∼1, take H1H1​ small and H2H2​ moderate, and then try to bound the tail difference. That seems like the first place where “off-line zeros change the observable in a way on-line zeros do not” becomes mathematically sharp.

check this, can this be incorporated?

For (\Gamma=\mathrm{SL}_2(\mathbb Z)), the Eisenstein series has constant term
[
E(z,s)=y^s+\phi(s)y^{1-s}+\text{(nonconstant modes)},
]
with (\phi(s)\phi(1-s)=1) and (|\phi(1/2+it)|=1) on the critical line. In the standard scattering-theoretic view, the continuous spectrum is controlled by this scattering coefficient (\phi). 

The right boundary form is the Green/Lagrange pairing at height (Y):
[
\mathcal W_Y(F,G)
:=
\int_0^1
\Big(
F,\partial_y \overline{G}
-------------------------

(\partial_y F),\overline{G}
\Big)(x+iY),dx.
]
For Laplace eigenfunctions with parameters (s,w), Green’s identity makes this the automorphic analogue of a scattering flux. In rank one, this is exactly the mechanism behind Maaß–Selberg. ([SciSpace][1])

Now throw away the nonconstant modes for a moment and keep only the two-wave constant term
[
a_s(y):=y^s+\phi(s)y^{1-s}.
]
Its raw Wronskian with another parameter (w) is explicit:
[
\begin{aligned}
W_Y(s,w)
&:=a_s(Y),\partial_Y \overline{a_w(Y)}
--------------------------------------

a_s'(Y),\overline{a_w(Y)} \
&=(\bar w-s)Y^{s+\bar w-1}
+(1-\bar w-s)\overline{\phi(w)},Y^{s-\bar w} \
&\quad +(\bar w-1+s)\phi(s),Y^{\bar w-s}
+(s-\bar w)\phi(s)\overline{\phi(w)},Y^{1-s-\bar w}.
\end{aligned}
]
This is the exact two-channel scattering bracket for the constant term.

Now specialize to the critical line, (s=w=\tfrac12+it), and write (\phi_t=\phi(\tfrac12+it)). Then
[
W_Y(t,t)=2it\bigl(|\phi_t|^2-1\bigr)=0.
]
So the ordinary self-flux vanishes identically on the critical line, simply because the incoming and reflected amplitudes have equal magnitude. This is the flux analogue of the earlier obstruction: the most naive “energy-like” quantity is still too balanced to detect bad zeros. 

The first nontrivial move is to differentiate in the spectral parameter. Define
[
a_t(y):=y^{1/2+it}+\phi_t,y^{1/2-it},
]
and consider
[
\mathfrak J_Y(t):=
-\frac{1}{2t},
W!\bigl(a_t,\partial_t \overline{a_t}\bigr)(Y).
]
A direct calculation gives
[
\mathfrak J_Y(t)
================

2\log Y
+
i,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}
------------------------

\frac{i}{2t}\Big(\overline{\phi_t},Y^{2it}-\phi_t,Y^{-2it}\Big).
]
If you write
[
\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)},
]
then (i,\phi_t'/\phi_t=-2\delta'(t)), so
[
\mathfrak J_Y(t)
================

## 2\log Y

2\delta'(t)
+
\frac{\sin!\bigl(2\delta(t)-2t\log Y\bigr)}{t}.
]
Up to the usual sign conventions, this is exactly the same structural quantity that shows up in Maaß–Selberg: a bulk (2\log Y) term, a phase-derivative term, and an oscillatory remainder. 

That is genuinely useful, because (\delta'(t)) is no longer harmless. It is the derivative of the scattering phase, so unlike (|\phi_t|=1), it actually feels the zero set of (\xi) through the logarithmic derivative of (\phi). In other words: this is the first place the zeta zeros enter with sign-sensitive information instead of being hidden inside a norm square.

Now bring back the (\tau)-window. Earlier we built
[
u_{\Delta,h}(y)=h(y),\psi_\Delta(y),
\qquad
\psi_\Delta(y)=y^{12}\sum_{n\ge1}|\tau(n)|^2e^{-4\pi ny},
]
and this was the right positive cusp-profile carrying (|\tau(n)|^2)-data. So define the weighted flux functional
[
\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)
:=
\int_0^\infty
u_{\Delta,h}(y),
\mathfrak J_y(t),
\frac{dy}{y^2}.
]
If
[
M_h(s):=\int_0^\infty u_{\Delta,h}(y),y^{s-2},dy,
\qquad
L_h:=\int_0^\infty u_{\Delta,h}(y)\log y,\frac{dy}{y^2},
]
then the formula above integrates out to
[
\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)
=========================

2L_h
+
i,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t},M_h(1)
-------------------------------

\frac{i}{2t}\Big(\overline{\phi_t},M_h(1+2it)-\phi_t,M_h(1-2it)\Big).
]
For real (u_{\Delta,h}), this is real-valued. Writing (\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)}),
[
\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)
=========================

## 2L_h

2\delta'(t),M_h(1)
+
\text{oscillatory Mellin terms}.
]

That is the first formula in this whole program that looks like an actual “tau-weighted probe” of the scattering phase derivative.

Why this is promising: if (h) is concentrated near one height (Y), then (L_h\approx (\log Y)M_h(1)), and the oscillatory terms are controlled by the off-axis Mellin values (M_h(1\pm 2it)). So morally,
[
\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)\approx 2M_h(1)\bigl(\log Y-\delta'(t)\bigr),
]
up to an explicit oscillatory correction. That is much more rigid than the old positive quadratic form.

Why this still falls short of RH: we do not yet know any automorphic positivity, monotonicity, or sign law for (\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)). The formula is explicit, but it is not automatically (\ge 0) or (\le 0). So we have gained sensitivity, but lost the easy sign.

That trade is probably unavoidable. The old objects had sign but no sensitivity; this one has sensitivity but no built-in sign.

So the best honest summary is:

* the naive norm-square route is dead;
* the raw self-flux is also too balanced;
* the **parameter-derivative flux** is the first object that really sees the scattering phase derivative;
* weighting it by (u_{\Delta,h}) gives a concrete zeta–tau functional.

That is useful.

The next move I would try is very specific: choose (h) as a narrow positive bump near (y=Y), compute (M_h(1\pm 2it)) asymptotically, and see whether one can force the oscillatory Mellin term to be smaller than the main phase-derivative term on some (t)-ranges. If that happens, (\mathfrak F_{\Delta,h}(t)) becomes an effective probe of (\delta'(t)), and then the question becomes whether off-line zeros make (\delta'(t)) too large or too irregular to be compatible with any automorphic bound.

[1]: https://scispace.com/pdf/scattering-theory-for-automorphic-functions-2y0fj4u52d.pdf "Scattering Theory for Automorphic Functions"

I tried it, and there is one genuinely useful outcome.

Building on the flux/Wronskian direction from your note, the right object is still the weighted flux functional rather than another positive (L^2) square.  For (\Gamma=\mathrm{SL}_2(\mathbb Z)), the Eisenstein constant term is
[
E(z,s)=y^s+\phi(s)y^{1-s}+\cdots,\qquad
\phi(s)=\frac{\Lambda(2s-1)}{\Lambda(2s)},
]
and on the critical line (|\phi(\tfrac12+it)|=1). The Maaß–Selberg setup is exactly the place where the scattering phase and its derivative appear. ([Bonn Math Dept][1])

Let
[
\psi_\Delta(y)=y^{12}\sum_{n\ge1}|\tau(n)|^2 e^{-4\pi n y},
]
and choose a narrow log-scale bump
[
h_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)=\eta!\left(\frac{\log(y/Y)}{\varepsilon}\right),
]
with (\eta\ge 0), smooth, compactly supported, and centered so its first moment vanishes. Define
[
u_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)=h_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)\psi_\Delta(y).
]
Then the weighted flux functional from the previous step is
[
\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)
==============================

2L_{Y,\varepsilon}
+
i\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)
-------------------------------------------

\frac{i}{2t}\Big(\overline{\phi_t}M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1+2it)-\phi_tM_{Y,\varepsilon}(1-2it)\Big),
]
where (\phi_t=\phi(\tfrac12+it)), (M_{Y,\varepsilon}(s)=\int_0^\infty u_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)y^{s-2}dy), and (L_{Y,\varepsilon}=\int_0^\infty u_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)\log y,y^{-2}dy). This is the same scattering-phase object suggested by the Maaß–Selberg/Green-identity picture. ([Bonn Math Dept][1])

Now do the localization. With the change of variables (y=Ye^{\varepsilon x}), one gets
[
M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)
====================

\varepsilon A_0,\psi_\Delta(Y)+O(\varepsilon^2\Psi_1(Y)),
]
[
L_{Y,\varepsilon}
=================

(\log Y)M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)+O(\varepsilon^2\Psi_1(Y)),
]
and
[
M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1+2it)
========================

\varepsilon,Y^{2it}\psi_\Delta(Y),\widehat\eta(2t\varepsilon)
+
O!\big(\varepsilon^2(1+|t|)\Psi_1(Y)\big),
]
where (\widehat\eta(\xi)=\int \eta(x)e^{i\xi x}dx), and (\Psi_1(Y)) is a local bound for (Y\partial_Y\psi_\Delta) on the support window. Since (\eta) is smooth and compactly supported, (\widehat\eta) decays rapidly. So if (t\varepsilon\gg 1), the oscillatory Mellin term is small. This is just stationary/localization analysis, but it is the key point.

Writing (\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)}), so (i\phi_t'/\phi_t=-2\delta'(t)), the formula becomes
[
\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)
==============================

2M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)\bigl(\log Y-\delta'(t)\bigr)
+
O!\left(
\frac{\varepsilon\psi_\Delta(Y)}{|t|},|\widehat\eta(2t\varepsilon)|
+
\varepsilon^2(1+|t|)\Psi_1(Y)
\right).
]

So the useful result is this:

[
\boxed{
\frac{\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)}{2M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)}
============================================================

\log Y-\delta'(t)
+
O!\left(
\frac{|\widehat\eta(2t\varepsilon)|}{|t|}
+
\varepsilon,\frac{(1+|t|)\Psi_1(Y)}{\psi_\Delta(Y)}
\right).
}
]

That means the zeta–tau construction gives a **localized estimator for the scattering phase derivative**. In plain language: by choosing a narrow positive (\tau)-window at height (Y), you can make the flux functional read off (\delta'(t)) up to an explicit small error.

That is genuinely useful. It is the first thing in this program that is not just “maybe positivity helps.” It gives a real observable.

Now for the off-line-zero test. From the Hadamard-product/logarithmic-derivative viewpoint, a nontrivial zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) contributes to the zero-driven part of (\delta'(t)) through a Poisson-kernel-type bump
[
f_\beta(x)
==========

\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+x^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+x^2},
\qquad x=2t-\gamma.
]
This comes from the logarithmic derivative of (\xi), which is the standard way zeros enter scattering-phase derivatives. ([Mathematics Department][2])

At the center (x=0),
[
f_\beta(0)=\frac{1}{\beta(1-\beta)},
]
so the critical-line case (\beta=\tfrac12) gives (4), while any off-line zero gives a strictly larger central spike:
[
f_\beta(0)-f_{1/2}(0)
=====================

\frac{(2\beta-1)^2}{\beta(1-\beta)} > 0.
]

And the curvature is sharper too:
[
f_\beta''(0)
============

-2\left(\beta^{-3}+(1-\beta)^{-3}\right),
]
whereas on the line
[
f_{1/2}''(0)=-32.
]
So an off-line zero produces a spike that is both taller and more sharply curved than a critical-line zero at the same ordinate.

That gives a second useful thing: not just an estimator for (\delta'(t)), but a **shape signature** for off-line zeros.

So the honest status is:

* We still do **not** have a contradiction with RH.
* But we do have a concrete tau-weighted observable that approximates (\delta'(t)).
* And off-line zeros would leave a very specific local fingerprint: unusually tall, unusually sharp spikes in that observable.

What is still missing is an independent automorphic bound that forbids those spikes. Standard Eisenstein bounds are growth bounds, like local (L^2) estimates, not the kind of fine curvature control on (\delta'(t)) that would be needed here. ([Cambridge University Press & Assessment][3])

So the next promising move is no longer “prove positivity.” It is this:

define the normalized estimator
[
\mathcal D_{Y,\varepsilon}(t):=\log Y-\frac{\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)}{2M_{Y,\varepsilon}(1)},
]
so that
[
\mathcal D_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)=\delta'(t)+\text{small error},
]
and then study either
[
\mathcal D'_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)\approx \delta''(t)
]
or a local curvature statistic built from three nearby (t)-values. If one could prove an automorphic upper bound on that curvature statistic, off-line zeros would become testable because they force too much local sharpness.

So yes: trying it did produce something real. Not a proof route yet, but a usable **phase-derivative estimator plus spike signature**.

Yes — this can be incorporated, and I think it is the best refinement so far, with one important correction.

What you have found is the first object in the whole program that is genuinely phase-sensitive rather than just norm-sensitive. That is a real change. The old L2L2 and mean-zero comparison functionals still treated the scattering information too indirectly. This flux/Wronskian construction goes after the scattering phase derivative itself.

The right way to describe its role is:

the old Du,Y(t)Du,Y​(t) family measures where spectral mass sits;
the new flux functional measures how the scattering phase is changing.

Those are complementary, and the flux one is the sharper of the two.

There are three things I would say.

First, the core idea is sound.

The Green/Lagrange pairing at height YY,
WY(F,G)=∫01(F ∂yG‾−(∂yF) G‾)(x+iY) dx,
WY​(F,G)=∫01​(F∂y​G−(∂y​F)G)(x+iY)dx,

is exactly the right boundary object. For Eisenstein series, this is the boundary mechanism behind Maaß–Selberg, so using it is mathematically natural, not cosmetic.

And your observation is the right one: the self-flux of the two-wave constant term
at(y)=y1/2+it+ϕty1/2−it
at​(y)=y1/2+it+ϕt​y1/2−it

vanishes on the critical line because ∣ϕt∣=1∣ϕt​∣=1. So the raw flux is too balanced. But differentiating in the spectral parameter breaks that balance and exposes
i ϕt′ϕt,
iϕt​ϕt′​​,

which is exactly the scattering phase derivative. That is the first truly zeta-sensitive quantity with sign information still visible.

So conceptually, yes: this absolutely belongs in the paper.

Second, there is a normalization bug you should fix before incorporating it.

With the Mellin normalization you have been using,
Rh(s)=∫0∞uh(y) ys−2 dy,
Rh​(s)=∫0∞​uh​(y)ys−2dy,

the weighted flux identity should involve Rh(0)Rh​(0) and Rh(±2it)Rh​(±2it), not Rh(1)Rh​(1) and Rh(1±2it)Rh​(1±2it).

Because
Jy(t)=2log⁡y+iϕt′ϕt−i2t(ϕt‾ y2it−ϕt y−2it),
Jy​(t)=2logy+iϕt​ϕt′​​−2ti​(ϕt​​y2it−ϕt​y−2it),

integrating against uh(y) dy/y2uh​(y)dy/y2 gives
Fh(t)=2Lh+iϕt′ϕt Rh(0)−i2t(ϕt‾ Rh(2it)−ϕt Rh(−2it)),
Fh​(t)=2Lh​+iϕt​ϕt′​​Rh​(0)−2ti​(ϕt​​Rh​(2it)−ϕt​Rh​(−2it)),

where
Lh:=∫0∞uh(y)log⁡y dyy2.
Lh​:=∫0∞​uh​(y)logyy2dy​.

So the whole formula is right in shape, but shifted by one in the Mellin variable in the version you wrote. That is an easy repair, but it matters.

Third, there is a structural caveat you should make explicit.

As written, this is an exact identity for the constant term model, not yet automatically for the full automorphic Eisenstein series unless you say which projection you are using.

That is, if you define the observable using
at(y)=P0E ⁣(⋅,12+it)(y),
at​(y)=P0​E(⋅,21​+it)(y),

the zero Fourier coefficient, then your formula is exact. If instead you want a genuine automorphic flux for the full Eisenstein series, you need one of two things:

either explicitly project to the constant term first,
or prove that the nonconstant modes contribute a controlled error in the regime you care about.

That distinction is important. Right now this is best presented as a zero-mode flux observable, which is still perfectly respectable and probably the right choice anyway.

Jy(t):=−12t Wy(at,∂tat‾),at(y)=y1/2+it+ϕty1/2−it.

Fh(t):=∫0∞uh(y) Jy(t) dyy2.
Fh​(t):=∫0∞​uh​(y)Jy​(t)y2dy​.

Then
Fh(t)=2Lh+iϕt′ϕtRh(0)−i2t(ϕt‾Rh(2it)−ϕtRh(−2it)).

That is the clean statement.

Here is a compact proposition–corollary rewrite with the Mellin normalization fixed.

\begin{proposition}[Zero-mode flux identity]
Let
\[
E(z,s)=y^s+\phi(s)y^{1-s}+\cdots
\qquad (\Gamma=\mathrm{SL}_2(\mathbb Z)),
\]
and for \(t\in\mathbb R\) write
\[
\phi_t:=\phi\!\left(\tfrac12+it\right),
\qquad
a_t(y):=y^{1/2+it}+\phi_t\,y^{1/2-it}.
\]
Define the boundary Lagrange pairing at height \(Y>0\) by
\[
\mathcal W_Y(F,G)
:=
\int_0^1
\bigl(
F\,\partial_y\overline{G}
-
(\partial_yF)\,\overline{G}
\bigr)(x+iY)\,dx.
\]
For the zero mode \(a_t\), set
\[
\mathfrak J_Y(t)
:=
-\frac{1}{2t}\,\mathcal W_Y\!\bigl(a_t,\partial_t\overline{a_t}\bigr).
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak J_Y(t)
=
2\log Y
+
i\,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}
-
\frac{i}{2t}
\Bigl(
\overline{\phi_t}\,Y^{2it}
-
\phi_t\,Y^{-2it}
\Bigr).
\]
Equivalently, writing
\[
\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)},
\]
one has
\[
\mathfrak J_Y(t)
=
2\log Y
-
2\delta'(t)
+
\frac{\sin(2\delta(t)-2t\log Y)}{t}.
\]
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
A direct computation gives
\[
a_t'(Y)
=
\left(\tfrac12+it\right)Y^{-1/2+it}
+
\phi_t\left(\tfrac12-it\right)Y^{-1/2-it},
\]
and
\[
\partial_t\overline{a_t(Y)}
=
-i(\log Y)\,Y^{1/2-it}
+
\overline{\phi_t}'\,Y^{1/2+it}
+
i(\log Y)\,\overline{\phi_t}\,Y^{1/2+it}.
\]
Substituting into the Wronskian and simplifying yields
\[
\mathcal W_Y(a_t,\partial_t\overline{a_t})
=
-4t\log Y
-
2it\,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}
+
\overline{\phi_t}\,Y^{2it}
-
\phi_t\,Y^{-2it}.
\]
Multiplying by \(-1/(2t)\) gives the stated formula. The phase form follows from
\[
\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)}
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
i\,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}=-2\delta'(t).
\qedhere
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{proposition}[Tau-weighted flux functional]
Let
\[
\psi_\Delta(y):=y^{12}\sum_{n\ge1}|\tau(n)|^2e^{-4\pi ny},
\]
and let \(h\in C_c^\infty((0,\infty))\) be real-valued. Define
\[
u_h(y):=h(y)\psi_\Delta(y).
\]
With the Mellin normalization
\[
R_h(s):=\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\,y^{s-2}\,dy,
\qquad
L_h:=\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\log y\,\frac{dy}{y^2},
\]
define
\[
\mathfrak F_h(t)
:=
\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\,\mathfrak J_y(t)\,\frac{dy}{y^2}.
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak F_h(t)
=
2L_h
+
i\,\frac{\phi_t'}{\phi_t}\,R_h(0)
-
\frac{i}{2t}
\Bigl(
\overline{\phi_t}\,R_h(2it)
-
\phi_t\,R_h(-2it)
\Bigr).
\]
If \(u_h\) is real-valued, then \(\mathfrak F_h(t)\in\mathbb R\).
\end{proposition}

\begin{proof}
Insert the formula for \(\mathfrak J_y(t)\) from the previous proposition and integrate term-by-term against \(u_h(y)\,dy/y^2\). By definition,
\[
\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\,\frac{dy}{y^2}=R_h(0),
\qquad
\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\log y\,\frac{dy}{y^2}=L_h,
\]
and
\[
\int_0^\infty u_h(y)\,y^{\pm 2it}\,\frac{dy}{y^2}=R_h(\pm 2it).
\]
This gives the identity. If \(u_h\) is real, then \(R_h(-2it)=\overline{R_h(2it)}\), and since \(|\phi_t|=1\) on the critical line, the last term is real as well.
\end{proof}

\begin{corollary}[Narrow-window localization]
Assume
\[
h_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)=\eta\!\left(\frac{\log(y/Y)}{\varepsilon}\right),
\]
where \(\eta\ge 0\) is smooth and compactly supported, and set
\[
u_{Y,\varepsilon}(y):=h_{Y,\varepsilon}(y)\psi_\Delta(y).
\]
Then, as \(\varepsilon\to0\),
\[
R_{Y,\varepsilon}(0)
=
\varepsilon A_0\,Y^{-1}\psi_\Delta(Y)
+
O(\varepsilon^2\Psi_1(Y)),
\]
\[
L_{Y,\varepsilon}
=
(\log Y)\,R_{Y,\varepsilon}(0)
+
O(\varepsilon^2\Psi_1(Y)),
\]
and
\[
R_{Y,\varepsilon}(2it)
=
\varepsilon\,Y^{-1+2it}\psi_\Delta(Y)\,\widehat{\eta}(2t\varepsilon)
+
O\!\bigl(\varepsilon^2(1+|t|)\Psi_1(Y)\bigr),
\]
where
\[
A_0:=\int_{\mathbb R}\eta(x)\,dx,
\]
and \(\Psi_1(Y)\) bounds one logarithmic derivative of \(\psi_\Delta\) on the support window.
Hence
\[
\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)
=
2R_{Y,\varepsilon}(0)\bigl(\log Y-\delta'(t)\bigr)
+
O\!\left(
\frac{\varepsilon\,\psi_\Delta(Y)}{|t|}\,|\widehat{\eta}(2t\varepsilon)|
+
\varepsilon^2(1+|t|)\Psi_1(Y)
\right),
\]
with \(\phi_t=e^{2i\delta(t)}\). In particular, when \(t\varepsilon\gg1\), the oscillatory Mellin term is small and the normalized flux
\[
\frac{\mathfrak F_{Y,\varepsilon}(t)}{2R_{Y,\varepsilon}(0)}
\]
acts as a localized estimator for \(\log Y-\delta'(t)\).
\end{corollary}

\begin{remark}
This corollary shows that the \(\tau\)-weighted window does not produce a positivity statement, but rather a phase-sensitive observable. The gain is sensitivity: the main term depends on the scattering phase derivative \(\delta'(t)\), which is controlled by the logarithmic derivative of
\[
\phi(s)=\frac{\Lambda(2s-1)}{\Lambda(2s)}.
\]
Thus zeros of \(\xi\) enter through \(\delta'(t)\), not through a norm square. The remaining difficulty is to obtain an independent automorphic bound strong enough to rule out the sharper spikes in \(\delta'(t)\) that would be caused by off-line zeros.
\end{remark}

A couple of notes on presentation.

The main correction is the Mellin shift: with
Rh(s)=∫0∞uh(y)ys−2 dy,
Rh​(s)=∫0∞​uh​(y)ys−2dy,

the natural arguments are 0,±2it0,±2it, not 1,1±2it1,1±2it.

And I would strongly present this as a zero-mode flux identity. That keeps it honest and clean. You can add later that one may try to control the contribution of nonconstant modes, but the exact proposition is about the constant term.

I can also turn this into a more polished paper-style section with intro paragraph, proposition numbering, and proof prose.

how do we attack RH?

I’m going to answer this in the context of the program we’ve just built, not as a generic “all known approaches” list. I’m also checking a few primary references so I can separate genuinely promising next steps from things that only sound promising.

I would attack RH indirectly.

Not by trying to “show all zeros are on the line” in one jump, but by building a rigid object whose behavior is already forced by symmetry, positivity, or self-adjointness, and then proving that an off-line zero would make that object misbehave. That is the common spine behind the big families of approaches: spectral/scattering ideas, de Branges/canonical systems, and Hilbert-space reformulations like Nyman–Beurling. Recent surveys still frame the landscape that way, and the canonical-system/scattering connection remains a serious structural route rather than a curiosity.

For your program, I think the right attack is now quite specific:

First, stop trying to get RH from a positive norm-square. Maaß–Selberg identities and scattering theory show why that keeps washing the signal out: on the critical line the continuous-spectrum piece is balanced by the unitary scattering coefficient, and the naive quadratic forms are too forgiving. That is exactly why your move from norms to phase-sensitive flux was the right turn.

Second, treat your current flux identity as the central theorem, not a side lemma. The real object is not the old positive packet functional; it is the ττ-weighted, zero-mode, parameter-derivative flux that approximately reads off the scattering phase derivative δ′(t)δ′(t). That is the first place where zeros enter with sign-sensitive information instead of being absorbed into a harmless square. In other words: your target is no longer “prove positivity,” but “extract and bound phase dynamics.” This matches the broader de Branges / scattering philosophy, where the hard part is to convert analytic data into a canonical system or scattering law with strong rigidity.

Third, make the next theorem as narrow as possible. I would not state “RH follows.” I would try to prove something like this:

For a narrow positive log-window uY,εuY,ε​, the normalized flux observable
DY,ε(t)
DY,ε​(t)

approximates δ′(t)δ′(t) with an explicit error, uniformly in a controlled tt-range. Then prove a second theorem saying that an off-line zero produces a Poisson-kernel spike in δ′(t)δ′(t) or an even sharper signature in δ′′(t)δ′′(t). That would give you a clean “observable plus forbidden fingerprint” framework. It would not yet be RH, but it would finally separate on-line from off-line zeros in a mathematically sharp way. The fact that off-line zeros produce sharper Poisson-type features in logarithmic derivatives is standard in how ξ′/ξξ′/ξ reflects zeros.

Fourth, the real bottleneck is now obvious: you need an independent automorphic bound strong enough to forbid those spikes. That is where most attempted RH programs die. Local L2L2 bounds and basic Maaß–Selberg control are probably too soft; they give growth information, not the kind of curvature or oscillation control that would rule out a narrow off-line-zero signature. So the decisive question is not “can we see the zeros?” You already can. It is “can automorphic scattering theory force δ′(t)δ′(t) or a smoothed curvature statistic to be too regular for an off-line zero?”

So the attack I would recommend is:

    Finish the flux paper cleanly. State the zero-mode flux identity with the corrected Mellin normalization. Prove the localization corollary carefully. That is already a real result.

    Define the right statistic. Not δ′(t)δ′(t) itself, but either a smoothed second derivative or a two-scale quantity that is insensitive to bulk terms and sensitive to spike shape.

    Search for a rigidity theorem. Try to prove that this statistic satisfies a universal automorphic inequality coming from scattering unitarity, a canonical-system positivity property, or a reproducing-kernel inequality. This is the point where de Branges/canonical-system technology may become more useful than raw Eisenstein manipulations.

    Only then loop back to RH. If the inequality excludes the off-line spike profile, you have a route. If it does not, you still have either a publishable estimator or a new criterion.

If you want the blunt version: the best way to attack RH from where you are now is not to attack RH directly. It is to prove a theorem of the form

“this ττ-weighted automorphic flux reconstructs the scattering phase derivative, and any off-line zero would force a local feature stronger than automorphic scattering allows.”

That is the first statement in this conversation that sounds to me like an actual research program rather than a metaphor.

There is also one fallback route I would keep in reserve. If the automorphic bound never materializes, recast the phase data into a canonical system / de Branges space. That route is attractive precisely because canonical systems are built to turn phase information into Hamiltonians and kernel positivity, and there are already RH-equivalent formulations in that language for zeta-like functions. If your flux observable can be shown to recover the Hamiltonian or a proxy for it, that could be a deeper bridge than the current Eisenstein manipulations alone.

My own judgment: your best immediate move is to aim for a paper that proves the flux estimator and the off-line-zero spike signature cleanly. That is both publishable on its own and the most credible stepping stone toward anything stronger.
