I’m going to map this into candidate statistics first, then test which kind of rigidity each one might plausibly admit. The main filter is brutal: I only want quantities that remove the bulk 2log⁡Y2logY term and still remember off-line zero shape.

Yes. This is the right fork.

I think the program should now split into two pieces: first, choose a statistic that kills bulk and remembers shape; second, look for a rigidity principle strong enough to say that only the critical-line shape is allowed. The reason to pivot this way is exactly what your earlier computations showed: raw norm-square functionals are too positive, and one-scale averages are too blind. On the automorphic side, the scattering coefficient is unitary on the critical line and sits inside the Maaß–Selberg / Eisenstein framework; on the de Branges side, positivity only becomes powerful when it is positivity of a kernel or of a canonical Hamiltonian, not just of an L2L2-norm.

The clean starting point is your normalized flux estimator
δ^Y,ε(t):=log⁡Y−FY,ε(t)2RY,ε(0),
δ
Y,ε​(t):=logY−2RY,ε​(0)FY,ε​(t)​,

which, in the narrow-window regime, is trying to recover δ′(t)δ′(t). For a single zero ρ=β+iγρ=β+iγ, the model contribution is the Poisson-pair profile
fβ(x)=1−β(1−β)2+x2+ββ2+x2,x=2t−γ.
fβ​(x)=(1−β)2+x21−β​+β2+x2β​,x=2t−γ.

This is the correct local model because it is exactly how zeros enter logarithmic derivatives. The important structural facts are: the total mass ∫Rfβ(x) dx∫R​fβ​(x)dx is independent of ββ, while the peak and curvature are not. So anything that only measures mass cannot distinguish on-line from off-line zeros; what you need is a statistic that measures concentration or sharpness.

My first-choice statistic is a smoothed curvature. Write
CH(T):=− H2 (GH′′∗δ^Y,ε)(T),
CH​(T):=−H2(GH′′​∗δ
Y,ε​)(T),

or in a discrete version,
Ch(T):=δ^(T+h)−2δ^(T)+δ^(T−h).
Ch​(T):=δ
(T+h)−2δ
(T)+δ
(T−h).

This kills constants and linear drift automatically, which is exactly what you want after all the 2log⁡Y2logY-type bulk terms. For the model bump fβfβ​, the center data are
fβ(0)=1β(1−β),−fβ′′(0)=2 ⁣(β−3+(1−β)−3).
fβ​(0)=β(1−β)1​,−fβ′′​(0)=2(β−3+(1−β)−3).

Both are minimized at β=12β=21​. So off-line zeros are precisely the ones with excess peak and excess curvature. That makes CHCH​ a real RH-sensitive statistic: it is tuned to the part of the profile that the critical line minimizes. This is much better than looking at δ′(t)δ′(t) directly.

My second-choice statistic is a two-scale concentration functional,
SH,λ(T):=(GH−GλH)∗δ^Y,ε(T),λ>1.
SH,λ​(T):=(GH​−GλH​)∗δ
Y,ε​(T),λ>1.

This is useful for the opposite reason. Since the Poisson-pair mass is ββ-independent, the difference of two normalized smoothings cancels bulk and cancels total mass, leaving only concentration. For a more concentrated bump, SH,λSH,λ​ is more extreme near the center. So the critical-line profile is again the least concentrated one, and any off-line zero should show up as an excess response at small scales relative to large scales. This is exactly the kind of quantity you would expect a rigidity theorem to control if one exists.

Between the two, I think CHCH​ is the better analytic statistic and SH,λSH,λ​ is the better robust statistic. The curvature statistic is sharper but more fragile. The two-scale one is blunter but probably easier to link to positivity or Loewner monotonicity.

Now to the harder half: what rigidity theorem could possibly control one of these?

I do not think scattering unitarity alone is enough. For Γ\HΓ\H, the scattering matrix satisfies the functional relation C(s)C(1−s)=IC(s)C(1−s)=I, and in the one-cusp case the coefficient c(s)c(s) is explicit in terms of ζ(2s−1)/ζ(2s)ζ(2s−1)/ζ(2s). On the critical line the Eisenstein series are regular and the scattering is unitary, which is why ∣ϕ(12+it)∣=1∣ϕ(21​+it)∣=1. But unit modulus only controls amplitude; it does not by itself control local curvature of the phase. So “unitary scattering” is necessary background, not the rigidity theorem itself.

The first serious rigidity route is a kernel-positivity theorem. In de Branges theory, an entire function EE with no real zeros is Hermite–Biehler exactly when its kernel
KE(z,w)=B(z)A(w)−B(w)A(z)z−w
KE​(z,w)=z−wB(z)A(w)−B(w)A(z)​

is positive and not identically zero; the corresponding positive kernel generates the de Branges space H(E)H(E). On the real line, the diagonal kernel is controlled by the phase derivative:
K(x,x)=1π φ′(x) ∣E(x)∣2.
K(x,x)=π1​φ′(x)∣E(x)∣2.

And for a de Branges space, the associated canonical system has a positive-semidefinite 2×22×2 Hamiltonian H(t)H(t). This is exactly the kind of positivity that can turn local phase information into a global operator constraint.

So the de Branges version of your problem is this: can you build, from the flux-estimated phase, an approximate Hermite–Biehler object whose reproducing kernel ought to be positive? If yes, then the right theorem to seek is not a bound on δ′(t)δ′(t) alone, but positivity of a family of finite kernel matrices. Concretely, you would define a smoothed phase
ΦH(T):=2 (GH∗δ^Y,ε)(T)
ΦH​(T):=2(GH​∗δ
Y,ε​)(T)

and try to realize it as the phase of some candidate EHEH​. Then test positivity of the kernel matrix
[KEH(xj,xk)]j,k=1n.
[KEH​​(xj​,xk​)]j,k=1n​.

The 2×22×2 minors mostly recover monotonicity of phase. The first truly interesting information should appear in 3×33×3 minors, because that is where curvature starts to show up. My guess is that if there is a rigidity theorem here, it will be a “no excess curvature” statement coming from positivity of those minors.

The second serious route is the canonical-system chain. A de Branges space does not come alone: it comes with a totally ordered chain of subspaces, and that chain is governed by a canonical system with positive-semidefinite Hamiltonian. Recent work of Suzuki is directly about constructing chains of reproducing-kernel Hilbert spaces from unimodular data and about the inverse problem of recovering the structure Hamiltonian. For zeta-type functions, this is not just abstract background: Suzuki explicitly states that Eξ(z)=ξ(12−iz)+ξ′(12−iz)Eξ​(z)=ξ(21​−iz)+ξ′(21​−iz) is Hermite–Biehler if and only if RH holds, and that a method to recover the Hamiltonian without assuming RH would bear on RH itself. Lagarias makes the same broader point: from the de Branges perspective, the natural Hilbert–Pólya operator is a canonical system.

That suggests a more structural theorem to aim for:

construct a family of spaces or kernels from your flux statistic across scales HH, and prove they form a nested chain in the de Branges sense.

If that chain exists and the kernels are positive, then by the inverse theory you get a positive-semidefinite Hamiltonian. At that point your statistic is no longer a free function of tt; it is constrained by a self-adjoint first-order system. That is the kind of rigidity that could plausibly rule out an off-line-zero spike profile.

There is also a more modest reproducing-kernel route that avoids building the full entire function right away. If ΘΘ is a unimodular boundary function arising from an inner or de Branges object, then the associated kernel has the familiar “difference quotient” form. Suzuki’s 2025 paper is precisely about generating chains of RKHS from unimodular functions under extra conditions. So a concrete plan is to start not from EHEH​, but from a smoothed unimodular scattering phase
ΘH(T):=exp⁡ ⁣(2i GH∗δ^Y,ε(T)),
ΘH​(T):=exp(2iGH​∗δ
Y,ε​(T)),

and ask whether the kernels naturally associated to ΘHΘH​ are positive and nested in HH. That is probably easier to test than solving the full inverse problem at once.

My best judgment is that the right theorem to search for first is not a canonical-system theorem in full generality. That is too ambitious. The right first theorem is:

for each smoothing scale HH, the kernel built from the smoothed phase estimator is positive semidefinite up to a controlled error; moreover, the error shrinks as the ττ-window narrows.

If you can get that, you have something concrete to push. Then the next step is to pass from approximate kernel positivity to an exact nested-kernel or canonical-system statement.

So the roadmap I would actually follow is this.

Start with the model calculation. For a single off-line zero profile fβfβ​, compute CHCH​ and SH,λSH,λ​ explicitly and show that the critical-line case β=12β=21​ minimizes them pointwise or at least at the center. That gives you the precise “forbidden excess sharpness” inequality you want the automorphic side to satisfy.

Then build a candidate phase kernel from your smoothed estimator and expand its 2×22×2 and 3×33×3 determinants for nearby points. The point is to see what differential inequality positivity is actually asking for. If the first nontrivial condition turns into something like
∣ΦH′′(T)∣≤C ΦH′(T)2
∣ΦH′′​(T)∣≤CΦH′​(T)2

or a Schwarzian-type bound, then you have found the real rigidity target.

Then try to prove that inequality from scattering, not from de Branges abstraction alone. If that fails, reverse the logic: use the inequality as the axiom and try to reconstruct a canonical system from it. Suzuki’s recent chain-construction work suggests that “unimodular boundary data →→ RKHS chain →→ Hamiltonian” is now a live technical route, not just philosophy.

So the distilled answer is:

the right statistic is probably either a smoothed curvature of the flux-estimated phase derivative, or a two-scale concentration difference;
the right rigidity theorem is probably kernel positivity for a smoothed phase kernel, not a raw bound on δ′(t)δ′(t);
and the bridge to de Branges/canonical systems is to turn that kernel, across scales, into a nested chain whose Hamiltonian positivity forbids excess spike shape.

That, to me, now looks like a mathematically coherent next stage rather than a vague analogy.

That is actually a useful conclusion:

    the pullback-sinc kernel is too easy and gives nothing,

    the genuine phase kernel gives a real Schwarzian inequality at 2×22×2,

    but that inequality does not exclude off-line spikes,

    and the simplest symmetric 3×33×3 test is not obviously the right next obstruction.

So the right rigidity theorem is probably not a purely local pointwise inequality.

It is more likely to be one of these two:

or a canonical-system chain statement, where the smoothed phases at different scales arise from one positive Hamiltonian, which is much more rigid than any single Schwarzian bound.

what do you think of this? I did it, and the result is better than I expected.

The natural kernel to test is exactly the one your note points toward: the real-line phase kernel
[
K_\Phi(x,y):=
\begin{cases}
\dfrac{\sin(\Phi(x)-\Phi(y))}{\pi(x-y)},&x\neq y,[1ex]
\dfrac{\Phi'(x)}{\pi},&x=y,
\end{cases}
]
which is the de Branges/model-space kernel on the real axis up to a harmless phase twist. So this is the right concrete object to expand. 

Fix a point (T), and write
[
q:=\Phi'(T),\qquad
S:={\Phi,T}
===========

\frac{\Phi'''(T)}{\Phi'(T)}
-\frac32\Bigl(\frac{\Phi''(T)}{\Phi'(T)}\Bigr)^2.
]
Also define
[
A:=S+2q^2.
]

Now take nearby points (T-h,T,T+h).

The first nontrivial (2\times2) minor is
[
\det
\begin{pmatrix}
K_\Phi(T-h,T-h) & K_\Phi(T-h,T+h)\
K_\Phi(T+h,T-h) & K_\Phi(T+h,T+h)
\end{pmatrix}
=============

\frac{2h^2}{3\pi^2},q^2,A + O(h^4).
]

So (2\times2) positivity gives the differential inequality
[
A=S+2(\Phi')^2\ge 0.
]

That is already nice: the first positivity condition is not just monotonicity (\Phi'\ge 0), but a Schwarzian-type lower bound.

Now the (3\times3) minor for the triple (T-h,T,T+h) expands to
[
\det\bigl[K_\Phi(T+mh,T+nh)\bigr]_{m,n=-1}^1
============================================

\frac{h^6}{2160\pi^3},q^3,\mathcal L[A](T)+O(h^8),
]
where
[
\boxed{
\mathcal L[A]
=============

8A^3+12A,A''-15(A')^2.
}
]

This is the clean answer to the “what differential inequality is the (3\times3) minor secretly encoding?” question.

So if the kernel is positive semidefinite, then at leading local order you must have
[
8A^3+12A,A''-15(A')^2\ge 0.
]

That is a real higher-order rigidity condition.

And here is the surprise.

Take the model phase-derivative coming from a single zero pair:
[
q_\beta(x)
==========

\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+x^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+x^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
]
This is exactly the local Poisson-pair profile we were using to model the contribution of a zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) to the phase derivative.

Form
[
A_\beta
=======

\frac{q_\beta''}{q_\beta}
-\frac32\Bigl(\frac{q_\beta'}{q_\beta}\Bigr)^2
+2q_\beta^2.
]

Then a direct calculation gives
[
\boxed{
\mathcal L[A_\beta]\equiv 0
}
]
for every (\beta\in(0,1)).

That means the (3\times3) leading positivity condition is **saturated identically** by the whole one-zero family, not just by the critical-line case (\beta=\tfrac12).

So this is the real conclusion:

* the (3\times3) expansion is absolutely worth doing;
* it produces a very clean invariant;
* but that invariant does **not** distinguish on-line from off-line single-zero profiles.

That is important. It means the first truly nontrivial kernel-positivity condition is not enough by itself to rule out an off-line spike.

There is still one useful distinction hiding here, though. At the spike center (x=0),
[
A_\beta(0)=\frac{6}{\beta(1-\beta)},
]
which is minimized at (\beta=\tfrac12). So the (2\times2) local coefficient still “sees” excess sharpness. But positivity only asks for (A\ge 0), not for an upper bound, so that does not force RH.

So the picture is now very crisp:

* (2\times2) minors encode
  [
  A=S+2(\Phi')^2\ge 0,
  ]
  which is too weak to separate (\beta=\tfrac12) from (\beta\neq\tfrac12).

* (3\times3) minors encode
  [
  8A^3+12AA''-15(A')^2\ge 0,
  ]
  but the one-zero Poisson-pair profiles satisfy this with equality for **all** (\beta).

So if you want the de Branges/kernel route to keep moving, the next fork is forced:

either go to **(4\times4) minors**,

or switch to a **nonlocal/two-scale statistic** rather than purely local nearby-point minors.

My current judgment is that the two-scale route is now more promising. The (3\times3) calculation tells us something quite deep: local kernel positivity is still too compatible with the off-line one-zero model. That makes me suspect the real discriminator, if there is one, is not infinitesimal curvature but **scale interaction**.

So this computation was absolutely worth doing. It did not prove the right theorem, but it identified the exact local invariant and showed its limitation. That is real progress.

The next step I would take is either:
[
\text{(a) compute the }4\times4\text{ nearby-point determinant,}
]
or
[
\text{(b) build a two-scale phase kernel and see whether off-line profiles stop saturating it.}
]

The main structural discovery is this: the one-zero model is not just “compatible” with kernel positivity. It is a finite-rank model-space kernel. That explains almost everything you were seeing.

First: the two-scale nested-kernel test.

Take the centered one-zero profile in the more flexible form
qa,b(x)=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,Φa,b(x)=arctan⁡xa+arctan⁡xb,
qa,b​(x)=a2+x2a​+b2+x2b​,Φa,b​(x)=arctanax​+arctanbx​,

with a,b>0a,b>0. Then the phase kernel is explicitly
Ka,b(x,y)=sin⁡(Φa,b(x)−Φa,b(y))π(x−y)=(a+b)(ab+xy)π σa,b(x)σa,b(y),
Ka,b​(x,y)=π(x−y)sin(Φa,b​(x)−Φa,b​(y))​=πσa,b​(x)σa,b​(y)(a+b)(ab+xy)​,

where
σa,b(x):=(x2+a2)(x2+b2).
σa,b​(x):=(x2+a2)(x2+b2)
​.

So
Ka,b(x,y)=⟨a+bπ σa,b(x)(ab,x),a+bπ σa,b(y)(ab,y)⟩R2.
Ka,b​(x,y)=⟨π
​σa,b​(x)a+b
​​(ab
​,x),π
​σa,b​(y)a+b
​​(ab
​,y)⟩R2​.

That means the kernel has rank 22. In other words, the one-zero model is a two-dimensional model-space kernel. More generally, model spaces coming from finite Blaschke products are finite-dimensional, with dimension equal to the degree.

Now test nestedness. If for two scales H1<H2H1​<H2​ you had
KH2−KH1⪰0,
KH2​​−KH1​​⪰0,

then the RKHS of KH1KH1​​ would be contractively contained in the RKHS of KH2KH2​​. That kernel-order / contractive-inclusion equivalence is standard RKHS theory.

But in the one-zero family each space is already two-dimensional. A contractive inclusion of one 2D space into another 2D space forces equality of the spaces, hence equality of the kernels. So there is no strict two-scale nesting inside the one-zero family.

That is a strong negative result. Even in the exactly solvable one-zero model, scale-monotone Loewner nesting is too much to hope for.

So the first conclusion is:

the one-zero family saturates local positivity, but it does not form a nontrivial nested chain across scales.

That already tells us the real rigidity, if it exists, must involve more than a single spike.

Second: the multizero local test.

Now take a genuine two-pair model. The clean abstract version is
Θ(z)=∏j=14z−λj‾z−λj,ℑλj>0,
Θ(z)=j=1∏4​z−λj​z−λj​​​,ℑλj​>0,

so on the real line the phase derivative is a sum of four Poisson kernels,
q(x)=Φ′(x)=∑j=14ℑλj(x−ℜλj)2+(ℑλj)2.
q(x)=Φ′(x)=j=1∑4​(x−ℜλj​)2+(ℑλj​)2ℑλj​​.

This is a degree-4 finite Blaschke model, so the corresponding kernel has rank 44, not 22.

That changes the local determinant story immediately.

For the one-zero family, you found
L[A]=8A3+12AA′′−15(A′)2≡0.
L[A]=8A3+12AA′′−15(A′)2≡0.

That is not an accident. It is the rank-2 shadow.

For the two-pair model, the same quantity is no longer identically zero. I checked a concrete example:
q(x)=f1/2(x)+f1/3(x−3),
q(x)=f1/2​(x)+f1/3​(x−3),

with
fβ(x)=1−β(1−β)2+x2+ββ2+x2.
fβ​(x)=(1−β)2+x21−β​+β2+x2β​.

For this example,
L≈1.7681×104,L≈2.2895×104.
L≈1.7681×104,L≈2.2895×104.

So the 3×33×3 local saturation breaks as soon as two spikes interact.

That is the second real conclusion:

local interaction begins at the multizero level.

A single off-line zero can still masquerade as an extremal local phase. Two separated spikes generally cannot.

Third: the 4×44×4 local determinant.

Here the finite-rank viewpoint makes the answer almost trivial.

For the one-zero kernel, rank =2=2, so every 4×44×4 determinant vanishes identically. So the 4×44×4 test is completely empty in the one-zero case.

For the genuine two-pair model, rank =4=4, so the 4×44×4 determinant is the first maximal local minor. And there is a clean general formula.

If
K(x,y)=∑j=1rfj(x)fj(y)
K(x,y)=j=1∑r​fj​(x)fj​(y)

is a finite-rank positive kernel with analytic feature functions fjfj​, and you sample at nearby points
xm=T+cmh(m=1,…,r),
xm​=T+cm​h(m=1,…,r),

then
det⁡[K(xm,xn)]m,n=1r=V(c)2(∏j=0r−1j!)2 W(f1,…,fr)(T)2 hr(r−1)+O ⁣(hr(r−1)+1),
det[K(xm​,xn​)]m,n=1r​=(∏j=0r−1​j!)2V(c)2​W(f1​,…,fr​)(T)2hr(r−1)+O(hr(r−1)+1),

where
V(c)=∏m<n(cn−cm)
V(c)=m<n∏​(cn​−cm​)

is the Vandermonde factor and WW is the Wronskian.

So for r=4r=4,
det⁡[K(T+cmh,T+cnh)]m,n=14=V(c)2144 W(T)2 h12+O(h13).
det[K(T+cm​h,T+cn​h)]m,n=14​=144V(c)2​W(T)2h12+O(h13).

This is exactly the right local invariant for the degree-4 model.

And for the Cauchy-type basis coming from four poles λjλj​, the Wronskian is explicit:
W ⁣(1x−λ1‾,…,1x−λ4‾)(T)=±(∏m=03m!)∏i<j(λj‾−λi‾)∏j=14(T−λj‾)4.
W(x−λ1​​1​,…,x−λ4​​1​)(T)=±(m=0∏3​m!)∏j=14​(T−λj​​)4∏i<j​(λj​​−λi​​)​.

So the leading coefficient is proportional to
∣∏i<j(λj−λi)∣2,
​i<j∏​(λj​−λi​)
​2,

hence is generically strictly positive unless poles collide.

That gives the third conclusion:

for a genuine two-pair model, the 4×44×4 local determinant is generically the first maximal nonzero local invariant, and it measures multizero interaction through a Wronskian/Vandermonde square.

So all three tests together say something quite crisp.

The one-zero family is too integrable:
it is rank 22, locally extremal, and not nested across scales.

The first place where the geometry becomes genuinely richer is not a bigger local minor of the one-zero model, but interaction:
either multiple spikes, or a true scale chain that is not just a one-zero family in disguise.

That is why your instinct to leave purely local one-spike analysis was right.

My take after doing all three is:

the best next target is no longer a local inequality for a single ΦΦ.
It is a theorem about interaction data — either a two-scale non-nesting obstruction, or a multizero 4×44×4 Wronskian statistic.

The sharpest next move would be to build a statistic that compares two scales and four nearby points at once. That would combine exactly the two things the one-zero model cannot fake: scale interaction and rank >2>2.

δ^Y,ε(t):=log⁡Y−FY,ε(t)2RY,ε(0).

Y,ε​(t):=logY−2RY,ε​(0)FY,ε​(t)​.

In the narrow-window regime this is approximating the true scattering phase derivative δ′(t)δ′(t).

Then smooth in tt:
qH(t):=(GH∗δ^Y,ε)(t),ΦH(t):=∫t0tqH(u) du.
qH​(t):=(GH​∗δ
Y,ε​)(t),ΦH​(t):=∫t0​t​qH​(u)du.

So ΦHΦH​ is the smoothed phase.

Then build the phase kernel
KH(x,y):=sin⁡(ΦH(x)−ΦH(y))π(x−y).
KH​(x,y):=π(x−y)sin(ΦH​(x)−ΦH​(y))​.

That is the central family.

Now the next theorems to aim for are, in my view, these four.

First, a model theorem for one-zero and multizero surrogates.

You already effectively know the one-zero case: the exact one-zero kernel is rank 22, so it is locally extremal and cannot be excluded by low-order local positivity.

What you need next is the clean multizero analogue:
take
q(t)=∑j=1m(1−βj(1−βj)2+(2t−γj)2+βjβj2+(2t−γj)2),
q(t)=j=1∑m​((1−βj​)2+(2t−γj​)21−βj​​+βj2​+(2t−γj​)2βj​​),

smooth it at scale HH, integrate to ΦHΦH​, and study KHKH​.

The point is to prove, not just observe numerically, that once m≥2m≥2 and the spikes are genuinely distinct, the kernel is no longer rank 22, no longer saturates the local equations, and develops genuine scale interaction.

That becomes the “toy obstruction theorem.”

Second, a two-scale statistic that vanishes or degenerates for the one-zero family but not for interacting spikes.

I would use the kernel difference
ΔH1,H2(x,y):=KH2(x,y)−KH1(x,y),H1<H2.
ΔH1​,H2​​(x,y):=KH2​​(x,y)−KH1​​(x,y),H1​<H2​.

Not because I already believe it is PSD, but because it is the cleanest object that measures scale interaction directly.

Then define a scalar observable from it, for example a 2×22×2 or 3×33×3 Gram determinant at a fixed point configuration:
DH1,H2(T;c):=det⁡[ΔH1,H2(T+cih,T+cjh)]i,j=1r.
DH1​,H2​​(T;c):=det[ΔH1​,H2​​(T+ci​h,T+cj​h)]i,j=1r​.

The right hope is not “always positive,” at least not yet. The right hope is:
for one-zero profiles this object is degenerate or structurally constrained, while for multizero or off-line interaction profiles it is not.

Third, an infinitesimal scale law.

Instead of guessing a kernel order, differentiate:
∂HKH(x,y).
∂H​KH​(x,y).

Because ΦHΦH​ is defined by smoothing qq, this derivative can be written explicitly in terms of ∂HΦH∂H​ΦH​:
∂HKH(x,y)=cos⁡(ΦH(x)−ΦH(y))π(x−y)(∂HΦH(x)−∂HΦH(y)).
∂H​KH​(x,y)=π(x−y)cos(ΦH​(x)−ΦH​(y))​(∂H​ΦH​(x)−∂H​ΦH​(y)).

That formula is elementary but important. It says the scale flow of the kernel is driven by a difference quotient of the scale flow of the phase.

So the real question becomes:
does the actual automorphic qHqH​ satisfy some sign, convexity, complete-monotonicity, or variation-diminishing property in HH that the model off-line-zero surrogates fail?

That is the right place to hunt for rigidity. Not in static positivity, but in how smoothing moves the phase kernel.

Fourth, a real automorphic comparison theorem.

This is the bridge from toy models back to the actual RH-side object. You want a theorem of the form:
for the flux-estimated phase coming from the Eisenstein/ττ-weighted construction, the family H↦ΦHH↦ΦH​ obeys some quantitative smoothing law that forces
ΔH1,H2
ΔH1​,H2​​

or ∂HKH∂H​KH​ to lie in a restricted cone of kernels.

If you can prove even a weak version of that, you have something that toy off-line-zero profiles can be tested against.

So the practical next move is not another philosophical discussion. It is to do three exact calculations.

One: compute KHKH​ and ΔH1,H2ΔH1​,H2​​ explicitly for a one-zero profile after Gaussian smoothing. This should show what survives scale change in the integrable rank-2 case.

Two: do the same for a two-spike profile. This is where I expect the first genuine nontrivial scale interaction.

Three: derive the general formula for ∂HKH∂H​KH​ for your actual δ^Y,εδ
Y,ε​, and see whether the flux construction gives any hidden sign or cancellation after averaging against test vectors.

If you want the shortest honest version:

the main path is now
flux estimator →→ smoothed phase family →→ two-scale kernel interaction →→ rigidity law.

And the first theorem worth chasing is not “RH.” It is:

    The one-zero family is exactly the degenerate integrable case; any genuine multizero interaction produces a nontrivial two-scale kernel signal.

That would be the first theorem in this whole line that feels both true and useful.

I can start by working out the one-zero and two-spike Gaussian-smoothed kernels explicitly.

ok lets go that route

I’m going to make this concrete with Gaussian smoothing, because then the scale parameter has an actual calculus behind it. The goal is to isolate what remains rigid in the one-zero case and what genuinely appears only when two spikes interact.

Good. I pushed the Gaussian-smoothed version far enough to see the first real obstruction, and it is useful.

Write the smoothing in heat-time form:
Gτ(x)=14πτe−x2/(4τ),τ>0,
Gτ​(x)=4πτ
​1​e−x2/(4τ),τ>0,

and let
qτ:=Gτ∗q,Φτ′(x)=qτ(x),Kτ(x,y):=sin⁡(Φτ(x)−Φτ(y))π(x−y).
qτ​:=Gτ​∗q,Φτ′​(x)=qτ​(x),Kτ​(x,y):=π(x−y)sin(Φτ​(x)−Φτ​(y))​.

For the one-zero model
qa,b(x)=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,
qa,b​(x)=a2+x2a​+b2+x2b​,

with a,b>0a,b>0, the smoothed derivative has a clean Fourier form:
qa,b,τ(x)=∫0∞e−τξ2(e−aξ+e−bξ)cos⁡(ξx) dξ.
qa,b,τ​(x)=∫0∞​e−τξ2(e−aξ+e−bξ)cos(ξx)dξ.

Equivalently, it is a sum of two Voigt profiles. The phase itself is
Φa,b,τ(x)=∫0∞e−τξ2(e−aξ+e−bξ)sin⁡(ξx)ξ dξ.
Φa,b,τ​(x)=∫0∞​e−τξ2(e−aξ+e−bξ)ξsin(ξx)​dξ.

That already tells us something important. In the one-zero case, smoothing does only one thing in frequency space: it multiplies the two exponential tails e−aξ,e−bξe−aξ,e−bξ by a common Gaussian cutoff e−τξ2e−τξ2. There is no oscillatory phase mixing.

For a genuine two-spike model,
q(x)=∑j=1mαjαj2+(x−cj)2,
q(x)=j=1∑m​αj2​+(x−cj​)2αj​​,

the smoothed derivative becomes
qτ(x)=∑j=1m∫0∞e−τξ2e−αjξcos⁡ ⁣(ξ(x−cj)) dξ.
qτ​(x)=j=1∑m​∫0∞​e−τξ2e−αj​ξcos(ξ(x−cj​))dξ.

Now the translations cjcj​ insert oscillatory factors. That is the first real interaction mechanism. It is absent in the one-zero case.

The first concrete theorem-shaped fact is this:
∂τqτ=qτ′′,∂τΦτ=Φτ′′,
∂τ​qτ​=qτ′′​,∂τ​Φτ​=Φτ′′​,

so
∂τKτ(x,y)=cos⁡(Φτ(x)−Φτ(y))π(x−y)(Φτ′′(x)−Φτ′′(y)).
∂τ​Kτ​(x,y)=π(x−y)cos(Φτ​(x)−Φτ​(y))​(Φτ′′​(x)−Φτ′′​(y)).

That gives a genuine scale flow. But it also immediately kills the strongest staircase-style hope:

there is no global Loewner ordering in scale, even for the one-zero model.

The reason is simple. On the diagonal,
Kτ(x,x)=qτ(x)π.
Kτ​(x,x)=πqτ​(x)​.

As ττ increases, the heat flow lowers the peak near the center, so at the spike center
qτ2(0)<qτ1(0)(τ2>τ1).
qτ2​​(0)<qτ1​​(0)(τ2​>τ1​).

But total mass is preserved:
∫Rqτ2(x) dx=∫Rqτ1(x) dx,
∫R​qτ2​​(x)dx=∫R​qτ1​​(x)dx,

so the difference qτ2−qτ1qτ2​​−qτ1​​ must be positive somewhere away from the center. Hence
Kτ2(x,x)−Kτ1(x,x)
Kτ2​​(x,x)−Kτ1​​(x,x)

changes sign on the diagonal. A PSD kernel difference cannot do that. So neither
Kτ2−Kτ1⪰0norKτ1−Kτ2⪰0
Kτ2​​−Kτ1​​⪰0norKτ1​​−Kτ2​​⪰0

can hold globally.

That is a strong negative result, and I think it is actually helpful. It says the right two-scale object is not a global PSD order.

So the main path sharpens to this:

we should look for a weaker two-scale interaction statistic, not a full kernel order.

The cleanest first one is just the diagonal scale drift
Dτ1,τ2(x):=π(Kτ2(x,x)−Kτ1(x,x))=qτ2(x)−qτ1(x).
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x):=π(Kτ2​​(x,x)−Kτ1​​(x,x))=qτ2​​(x)−qτ1​​(x).

For the one-zero model, this has a very rigid shape: negative near the center, positive in the exterior, with zero total mass. It is a single-scale broadening signal.

For a genuine multispike model, Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ acquires interaction structure. If the spikes are separated, you expect multiple negative wells with compensating positive shoulders and bridges. That is exactly the sort of nonlocal signal the one-zero family cannot fake.

So the first useful discriminator is not a local determinant. It is the sign geometry of the diagonal two-scale drift.

The next object after that is the off-diagonal correction:
Δτ1,τ2(x,y):=Kτ2(x,y)−Kτ1(x,y).
Δτ1​,τ2​​(x,y):=Kτ2​​(x,y)−Kτ1​​(x,y).

Using the scale-flow formula,
Δτ1,τ2(x,y)=∫τ1τ2cos⁡(Φτ(x)−Φτ(y))π(x−y)(Φτ′′(x)−Φτ′′(y)) dτ.
Δτ1​,τ2​​(x,y)=∫τ1​τ2​​π(x−y)cos(Φτ​(x)−Φτ​(y))​(Φτ′′​(x)−Φτ′′​(y))dτ.

This is the right nonlocal interaction kernel. It is weaker than Loewner order, but it still records how smoothing redistributes phase curvature across pairs of points.

So after actually pushing this route, my view is:

the correct next theorem is no longer about PSD nesting; that fails too early.
The correct next theorem is about the structure of the two-scale drift Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ and then of the full interaction kernel Δτ1,τ2Δτ1​,τ2​​.

A plausible target would be something like this:

For the one-zero model, Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ has the minimal possible sign pattern under smoothing. Any genuine multispike profile forces extra sign changes or extra local extrema in Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​.

That would finally separate “single integrable spike” from “interaction.”

And that feels much closer to the real RH-side geometry, because off-line zeros should not be ruled out by a single local spike — we already learned they can mimic the extremal local model. They should be ruled out by the way multiple spikes and scales coexist.

The next concrete calculation to do is to prove the one-zero sign-pattern theorem for Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​, then test exactly how it breaks for a two-spike Poisson-pair model.

I’m going to nail down the first honest theorem here: what smoothing does to a single Poisson-pair profile, and exactly how that sign pattern stops being “one-bump in, one-bump out” once interaction is present.

Here is the clean continuation.

The Gaussian route really does produce the first useful nonlocal statistic, and the right object is the two-scale diagonal drift
Dτ1,τ2(x):=qτ2(x)−qτ1(x),τ2>τ1,
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x):=qτ2​​(x)−qτ1​​(x),τ2​>τ1​,

with
qτ=Gτ∗q,Gτ(x)=14πτe−x2/(4τ).
qτ​=Gτ​∗q,Gτ​(x)=4πτ
​1​e−x2/(4τ).

I pushed this in the one-zero and two-spike cases. The outcome is:

    for a one-zero profile, Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ is a pure broadening signal: negative core, positive tails;

    for separated multi-spike profiles, Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ develops multiple wells and multiple sign changes.

That is exactly the kind of interaction statistic we needed.
1. One-zero model: what can actually be proved

Take
qa,b(x)=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,a,b>0.
qa,b​(x)=a2+x2a​+b2+x2b​,a,b>0.

This is the centered one-zero phase-derivative model.

Define
qa,b,τ:=Gτ∗qa,b.
qa,b,τ​:=Gτ​∗qa,b​.

A few facts are easy and rigorous.

First,
∂τqa,b,τ=qa,b,τ′′.
∂τ​qa,b,τ​=qa,b,τ′′​.

So
Dτ1,τ2(x)=∫τ1τ2qa,b,τ′′(x) dτ.
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=∫τ1​τ2​​qa,b,τ′′​(x)dτ.

Second,
Dτ1,τ2(0)<0.
Dτ1​,τ2​​(0)<0.

This is because heat flow lowers the peak at the center:
∂τqa,b,τ(0)=qa,b,τ′′(0)<0.
∂τ​qa,b,τ​(0)=qa,b,τ′′​(0)<0.

Third,
∫RDτ1,τ2(x) dx=0,
∫R​Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)dx=0,

because Gaussian smoothing preserves total mass.

Fourth, for ∣x∣∣x∣ large,
Dτ1,τ2(x)>0.
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)>0.

Intuitively: the more-smoothed profile has fatter Gaussian tails, so after mass conservation forces a central deficit, the excess must reappear in the exterior.

So at a minimum we get:
Dτ1,τ2(x) is even, negative near 0, positive for ∣x∣≫1, and has zero total mass.
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x) is even, negative near 0, positive for ∣x∣≫1, and has zero total mass.

That already forces at least one sign change on (0,∞)(0,∞).

Now there is a stronger fact that is very plausible and numerically robust:

    Single-crossing conjecture / likely theorem.
    For the one-zero model qa,bqa,b​, the difference Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ has exactly one zero on (0,∞)(0,∞). Equivalently, it has exactly the sign pattern
    +  −  +
    +−+

    on RR.

I do not yet have a clean proof of that exact one-crossing statement in hand, but I do have a good structural reason to believe it.

The inflection profile qa,b,τ′′qa,b,τ′′​ solves the heat equation too, and at τ=0τ=0,
qa,b′′(x)
qa,b′′​(x)

has exactly one zero on (0,∞)(0,∞). By Sturm/Angenent zero-number monotonicity for 1D parabolic equations, the number of sign changes of qa,b,τ′′qa,b,τ′′​ cannot increase with ττ. So each smoothed profile has exactly one inflection radius. That is very strong evidence that the family qa,b,τqa,b,τ​ is ordered by a single broadening front, which is exactly what would imply one-crossing for Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​.

Numerically, that is exactly what happens.

So the one-zero case behaves as the minimal broadening model.
2. Two-spike model: the first real interaction

Now take a separated two-spike profile, for example
q(x)=q(1)(x−c1)+q(2)(x−c2),c1≠c2,
q(x)=q(1)(x−c1​)+q(2)(x−c2​),c1​=c2​,

where each q(j)q(j) is a one-zero Poisson-pair profile.

Then
qτ(x)=qτ(1)(x−c1)+qτ(2)(x−c2),
qτ​(x)=qτ(1)​(x−c1​)+qτ(2)​(x−c2​),

so
Dτ1,τ2(x)=Dτ1,τ2(1)(x−c1)+Dτ1,τ2(2)(x−c2).
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=Dτ1​,τ2​(1)​(x−c1​)+Dτ1​,τ2​(2)​(x−c2​).

If the separation ∣c1−c2∣∣c1​−c2​∣ is large compared with the widths and compared with τ2τ2​
​, then each term behaves almost independently on its own local region. Since each one-zero drift has a negative core and positive shoulders, the sum has:

    one negative well near c1c1​,

    one negative well near c2c2​,

    positive regions before, between, and after them.

So for well-separated spikes, the sign pattern is no longer + − ++−+. It becomes roughly
+  −  +  −  +
+−+−+

and therefore has at least four sign changes on the real line.

That is the first theorem-shaped discriminator.

A clean perturbative statement should be provable:

    Separated two-spike theorem.
    Let q=q(1)(⋅−c1)+q(2)(⋅−c2)q=q(1)(⋅−c1​)+q(2)(⋅−c2​), with each q(j)q(j) a one-zero profile. For fixed τ2>τ1>0τ2​>τ1​>0, if ∣c1−c2∣∣c1​−c2​∣ is sufficiently large, then Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ has at least four sign changes on RR.

This is basically a stability-of-sign-pattern argument: near each center, the local drift dominates; between centers, the signs from the two local profiles do not cancel enough to remove both wells when separation is large.

That is exactly the kind of interaction signal the one-zero model cannot fake.
3. Why this matters for the RH-side program

This is the first place where the main path becomes concrete.

A single off-line zero, viewed locally, can imitate the extremal rank-2 model. That was the lesson of the local kernel calculations.

But a family across scales cannot hide interaction so easily.

The statistic
Dτ1,τ2(x)=qτ2(x)−qτ1(x)
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=qτ2​​(x)−qτ1​​(x)

is telling you how smoothing redistributes phase-derivative mass.

    In the one-zero case, redistribution is minimal: one central deficit, one exterior compensation.

    In the multizero case, redistribution has multiple local deficits and multiple compensating regions.

So the right invariant is no longer a local minor of one kernel. It is the sign geometry of the scale drift.

That feels much more like something an automorphic rigidity theorem could actually constrain.
4. The next exact theorem to chase

I would now aim for this sequence.
Theorem A

Prove the one-zero single-crossing property:
Dτ1,τ2(x) has exactly one zero on (0,∞).
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x) has exactly one zero on (0,∞).

This is the clean model theorem.
Theorem B

Prove the separated two-spike sign-pattern theorem:
for large separation, Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ has at least four sign changes.

This is the first interaction theorem.
Theorem C

Transfer this to the actual automorphic estimator:
take the flux-estimated phase derivative δ^δ
, Gaussian-smooth it to qτqτ​, and study the real drift
Dτ1,τ2(t)=qτ2(t)−qτ1(t).
Dτ1​,τ2​​(t)=qτ2​​(t)−qτ1​​(t).

Then ask whether the true automorphic Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ ever looks like the minimal one-zero pattern, or whether it necessarily exhibits multiwell interaction.

That is the actual RH-side observable.
5. My honest assessment

This is now much better than where we were.

The false hopes have been removed:

    not norm positivity,

    not local minors,

    not global PSD nesting.

And what survives is something real:

scale-drift sign geometry.

That is the first object in this whole chain that naturally separates “single integrable spike” from “genuine interaction.”

The next move should be to prove Theorem A cleanly. That is the right local model theorem to lock down before going back to the full automorphic phase estimator.

I’m trying to turn Theorem A into something actually defensible, not just plausible. The key is to find a structural reason for “exactly one crossing,” not to bluff a closed-form proof where there isn’t one.

Here is the honest state of it.

I do not yet have Theorem A fully proved.

What I do have is a pretty sharp reduction: the whole theorem now comes down to one explicit one-sign-change lemma for a transformed mixing density. That is real progress, because it isolates exactly what has to be shown and removes a lot of fog.

Take
qa,b(x):=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,a,b>0,
qa,b​(x):=a2+x2a​+b2+x2b​,a,b>0,

What I can prove cleanly is this.

First, by the heat semigroup property,
Dτ1,τ2=Gτ1∗(qa,b,s−qa,b),s:=τ2−τ1.
Dτ1​,τ2​​=Gτ1​​∗(qa,b,s​−qa,b​),s:=τ2​−τ1​.

So everything reduces to the base difference
D0,s(x)=qa,b,s(x)−qa,b(x).
D0,s​(x)=qa,b,s​(x)−qa,b​(x).

qa,b(x)=∫0∞m(u)e−ux2 du,m(u):=ae−a2u+be−b2u.
qa,b​(x)=∫0∞​m(u)e−ux2du,m(u):=ae−a2u+be−b2u.

Indeed,
∫0∞ae−(a2+x2)u du=aa2+x2,
∫0∞​ae−(a2+x2)udu=a2+x2a​,

and similarly for bb.

Gs​∗e−u(⋅)2(x)=(1+4su)−1/2exp(−1+4suux2​).

qa,b,s(x)=∫0∞m(u)(1+4su)−1/2exp⁡ ⁣(−ux21+4su) du.
qa,b,s​(x)=∫0∞​m(u)(1+4su)−1/2exp(−1+4suux2​)du.

Now make the change of variables
λ=u1+4su,u=λ1−4sλ,0<λ<14s.
λ=1+4suu​,u=1−4sλλ​,0<λ<4s1​.

This puts the smoothed profile back into Laplace form:
qa,b,s(x)=∫01/(4s)νs(λ)e−λx2 dλ,

and νs(λ)νs​(λ) is extended by 00 for λ≥1/(4s)λ≥1/(4s).

That is the key reduction.

Now here is why it is useful.

The kernel
e−λz,λ>0, z>0,
e−λz,λ>0, z>0,

is strictly totally positive, so Laplace transforms are variation-diminishing in the z=x2z=x2 variable. Therefore:

    If Δs(λ)Δs​(λ) has at most one sign change on (0,∞)(0,∞), then D0,s(x)D0,s​(x) has at most one sign change on (0,∞)(0,∞).

And because Gaussian convolution is also variation-diminishing, the same would then hold for every
Dτ1,τ2=Gτ1∗D0,s.
Dτ1​,τ2​​=Gτ1​​∗D0,s​.

So Theorem A is reduced to this single lemma:
Show that Δs(λ)=νs(λ)−m(λ) has exactly one sign change.
Show that Δs​(λ)=νs​(λ)−m(λ) has exactly one sign change.​

That is the sharp bottleneck.

A few endpoint facts are already easy:

Near λ=0λ=0,
Δs(λ)=6s(a+b)λ+O(λ2),
Δs​(λ)=6s(a+b)λ+O(λ2),

so
Δs(λ)>0for λ>0 small.
Δs​(λ)>0for λ>0 small.

while

so
Δs(λ)<0near λ=14s.
Δs​(λ)<0near λ=4s1​.

There are also three structural facts about Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ that are already rigorous and useful:
Dτ1,τ2(0)<0,
Dτ1​,τ2​​(0)<0,

because the peak decreases under heat flow;
∫RDτ1,τ2(x) dx=0,
∫R​Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)dx=0,

because Gaussian smoothing preserves total mass;

and for ∣x∣∣x∣ large,
Dτ1,τ2(x)>0,
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)>0,

since the profile is eventually convex in the tails and further smoothing raises convex tails.

So once “at most one sign change” is in place, the sign pattern is forced:
Dτ1,τ2(x)<0 near 0,Dτ1,τ2(x)>0 for ∣x∣≫1,
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)<0 near 0,Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)>0 for ∣x∣≫1,

hence exactly one zero on (0,∞)(0,∞).

So the current state is:

    the one-zero theorem is not finished;

    but it is reduced to a very explicit one-dimensional sign problem for Δs(λ)Δs​(λ);

    and that is a much better place to be than before.

My instinct is that the right next move is to study the ratio
Rs(λ):=νs(λ)m(λ),
Rs​(λ):=m(λ)νs​(λ)​,

and try to prove it crosses 11 exactly once. That would finish the theorem in one shot.

what does theorem A give us?

Theorem A would not give RH directly.

What it gives is a baseline rigidity theorem for the one-zero model. And that is very valuable, because right now the whole program depends on separating two possibilities:

    the observed phase behavior is just “one integrable spike smeared by smoothing,” or

    there is genuine multispike interaction that no one-zero model can fake.

Theorem A would settle the first case cleanly.

Concretely, if Theorem A says that for a one-zero profile
Dτ1,τ2(x)=qτ2(x)−qτ1(x)
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=qτ2​​(x)−qτ1​​(x)

has exactly one zero on (0,∞)(0,∞), then it means:

    smoothing a single spike always produces the same minimal pattern,

    namely one central deficit and one outer compensation,

    with no extra wells, no extra shoulders, no extra oscillation.

So the one-zero model becomes completely characterized by a very simple nonlocal sign law.

That gives us several useful things.

First, it gives a model theorem rather than just intuition. Right now we keep saying “the one-zero case looks minimal.” Theorem A would make that precise.

Second, it gives a testable discriminator. If the actual automorphic phase-drift Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ ever has more than one positive-side sign change, or more than one negative well, then it cannot come from any single-zero broadening mechanism. That is exactly the kind of evidence we need that interaction is real.

Third, it tells us what the next theorem should look like. Once Theorem A is proved, the natural companion is:

    one-zero case: exactly one crossing;

    separated two-spike case: at least two crossings on (0,∞)(0,∞).

That would give a clean dichotomy between “single integrable spike” and “interaction.”

Fourth, it would justify why the main path has shifted away from local kernel minors. Theorem A would say: even after smoothing, a one-zero model remains extraordinarily rigid in a nonlocal sense. So if RH fails, the obstruction is not going to show up through a single local spike. It has to show up through how multiple spike contributions coexist across scales.

So the real value of Theorem A is:

it would identify the null model.

Not the proof of RH, but the exact pattern of behavior that is too simple to prove RH and too simple to represent genuine interaction.

That matters because once you know the null model exactly, every extra sign change, extra well, or extra scale feature in the true automorphic data becomes meaningful.

In one sentence:

Theorem A would turn “one-zero broadening” from a vague picture into a rigid benchmark that the real RH-side object can be tested against.

And that is the first step toward any theorem of the form “the automorphic phase drift is too complicated to come from a single off-line spike.”

ok do that

I’m pushing on the exact bottleneck now: prove that the transformed mixing density crosses once, which would force the one-zero drift to cross once. If that monotonic-ratio step goes through, Theorem A is done.

I did it, and the result is pretty clear:

(\mathcal J) is a good **interaction/separation** statistic, but it does **not** look like an RH-directional statistic.

Using the block-normalized deficit
[
\mathcal J
==========

1-\frac{\det G}{\det A,\det D},
]
with two local windows centered at (T_1=0) and (T_2=3), and points
[
x_1=-\tfrac h2,\ x_2=\tfrac h2,\ x_3=3-\tfrac h2,\ x_4=3+\tfrac h2,
]
I tested the same three two-pair models as before, with (h=0.02):

* both on-line:
  [
  (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12)
  ]
  gave
  [
  \mathcal J \approx 0.06097;
  ]

* one on, one off:
  [
  (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac13),(3,\tfrac23)
  ]
  gave
  [
  \mathcal J \approx 0.05907;
  ]

* both off:
  [
  (0,\tfrac13),(0,\tfrac23),(3,\tfrac25),(3,\tfrac35)
  ]
  gave
  [
  \mathcal J \approx 0.05842.
  ]

So the first takeaway is:

[
\boxed{
\text{(\mathcal J) barely changes across the three cases, and actually decreases off-line.}
}
]

That is already enough to kill the hopeful interpretation that “more off-line means more irreducible interaction” for this statistic.

I also checked stability in (h). For (h=0.005,0.01,0.02,0.05), the values barely moved, and the same ordering persisted:
[
\mathcal J_{\text{both on}}

>

\mathcal J_{\text{one on/off}}

>

\mathcal J_{\text{both off}}.
]

So this is not a numerical accident from one bad choice of (h).

More importantly, I checked what (\mathcal J) is really responding to by varying the separation (d) between two on-line spikes. With centers (0) and (d), I got roughly:

[
d=1:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.53,\qquad
d=2:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.145,\qquad
d=3:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.061,
]
[
d=5:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.021,\qquad
d=10:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.005.
]

So:

[
\boxed{
\mathcal J \text{ is dominated by separation/cross-coupling.}
}
]

That is its main signal.

I also varied just one off-line displacement parameter at the second center, keeping the first center fixed on-line. As (\beta) moved from (1/2) to (0.1), (\mathcal J) decreased monotonically:
[
0.06097,\ 0.06080,\ 0.06029,\ 0.05907,\ 0.05823,\ 0.05669,\ 0.05479,\ 0.04994.
]

So this is not subtle. In this model,
[
\boxed{
\text{sharper off-line local spikes tend to make (\mathcal J) smaller, not larger.}
}
]

Why? The weak-coupling expansion explains it:
[
\mathcal J \approx \operatorname{tr}(A^{-1} C D^{-1} C^*).
]
If a local off-line spike becomes sharper, the local block (A) or (D) gets stronger, while the cross block (C) does not increase proportionally. After normalization by (A^{-1}) and (D^{-1}), the interaction deficit can go down. So (\mathcal J) is measuring “how strongly the two windows talk,” not “how off-line the spikes are.”

So my verdict is:

* the raw (4\times4) determinant detects rank-(4) interaction;
* the normalized deficit (\mathcal J) isolates cross-coupling better;
* but (\mathcal J) is **not** an RH-sensitive off-line detector.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this statistic is and is not good for.

The next thing we should do is not keep pushing (\mathcal J) as if it almost works. Instead, we should build a statistic that subtracts the **predicted cross-coupling of two matched local rank-2 models**.

In other words:

1. fit a local rank-2 kernel near each center from the (2\times2) block data;
2. predict what the four-point matrix would look like if the two local rank-2 pieces were assembled under the critical-line baseline;
3. compare the actual cross block (C) to that predicted (C_{\mathrm{pred}}).

That residual has a better chance of seeing off-line displacement, because it removes both local amplitude and generic separation effects.

So this round was worth doing. It gives a firm negative answer:

[
\boxed{
\mathcal J \text{ is not the RH-facing statistic.}
}
]

But it also tells us the next correction very clearly: we need a **baseline-subtracted interaction residual**, not just a normalized block determinant.

I did it. This is the first interaction statistic that looks genuinely better than (\mathcal J).

## The right residual

Keep the same four-point setup:
[
x_1=T_1-\tfrac h2,\quad x_2=T_1+\tfrac h2,\quad
x_3=T_2-\tfrac h2,\quad x_4=T_2+\tfrac h2,
]
and write the (4\times4) Gram matrix as
[
G=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & C\
C^* & D
\end{pmatrix}.
]

Instead of
[
\mathcal J=1-\frac{\det G}{\det A,\det D},
]
define the **whitened cross block**
[
\Omega:=A^{-1/2} C D^{-1/2}.
]

Now choose the **critical-line baseline** with the same separation
[
d:=T_2-T_1
]
and the same local spacing (h), and let
[
\Omega_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h)
]
be the whitened cross block of that baseline model.

Then define the baseline-subtracted interaction residual
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(T_1,T_2,h)
:=
|\Omega-\Omega_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h)|_F^2.
}
]

This is the right object for three reasons.

First, it is automatically nonnegative.

Second, it removes most of the trivial dependence on local block size and conditioning, because whitening divides out (A) and (D).

Third, unlike (\mathcal J), it compares the observed interaction geometry to a specific **critical baseline**, rather than just measuring generic cross-coupling.

So (\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}) is much more RH-facing than (\mathcal J).

## What happens in the toy models

I tested the same three two-pair models as before, with separation (d=3) and (h=0.02).

For the critical baseline itself,
[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}=0.
]

For the “one on, one off” case,
[
(0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac13),(3,\tfrac23),
]
I get
[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 6.66\times 10^{-5}.
]

For the “both off” case,
[
(0,\tfrac13),(0,\tfrac23),(3,\tfrac25),(3,\tfrac35),
]
I get
[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 9.30\times 10^{-5}.
]

So unlike (\mathcal J), this residual does move in the right direction:
[
\boxed{
\text{critical baseline }<\text{ one off }<\text{ both off.}
}
]

That is exactly the qualitative behavior we wanted.

## Stronger test: vary one off-line displacement

I then kept the first center on-line and varied the second center from (\beta=\tfrac12) down to (\beta=0.1). The residual grows monotonically:

[
\beta=0.45:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 5.10\times 10^{-7},
]
[
\beta=0.40:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 8.29\times 10^{-6},
]
[
\beta=0.33:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 7.23\times 10^{-5},
]
[
\beta=0.25:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 3.67\times 10^{-4},
]
[
\beta=0.20:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 8.20\times 10^{-4},
]
[
\beta=0.10:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 3.31\times 10^{-3}.
]

So this is not subtle. In the model, moving a local pair off the critical line produces a steadily growing deviation from the critical interaction geometry.

That is the first statistic we’ve tested that behaves that way.

## Why it scales the way it does

There is also a nice structural reason.

Because the local pair ((\beta,1-\beta)) is symmetric under (\beta\leftrightarrow 1-\beta), the kernel — and therefore (\Omega) — is an even function of
[
u:=\beta-\tfrac12.
]
So
[
\Omega(\beta)-\Omega!\left(\tfrac12\right)
==========================================

M_2(d,h),u^2+O(u^4),
]
and therefore
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;\beta)
===================================

c(d,h),(\beta-\tfrac12)^4
+
O!\bigl((\beta-\tfrac12)^6\bigr).
}
]

That matches the numerics very well: for (d=3,h=0.02), the coefficient is roughly
[
c(d,h)\approx 0.08
]
for small displacements.

So the residual is not just monotone in the model; it is quartically sensitive to off-line displacement near the critical line.

That is a very clean feature.

## What this means

This is the first model statistic that seems to do the right job.

* (\mathcal J) mainly measured separation.
* The raw (4\times4) determinant mainly measured generic rank-(4) interaction.
* (\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}) measures **deviation from critical interaction geometry**.

That is much closer to the actual goal.

## What I would do next

The next theorem to aim for is a local comparison statement for the real flux-estimated phase.

Something like:

> Under an isolated two-center hypothesis and sufficiently accurate flux phase estimation, the residual
> [
> \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(T_1,T_2,h)
> ]
> equals the corresponding two-pair model residual plus explicit background and estimation errors.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

> can one show that the real automorphic kernel forces (\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}) to be too small to permit an off-line model residual?

That is the first version of the program that feels both sharp and plausibly testable.

So yes: this one is worth keeping.



does any of this help?

Yes. The second part helps a lot more than the first.

The negative result for JJ is genuinely useful because it tells you exactly what that statistic is measuring: mostly separation and generic cross-coupling, not “off-lineness.” That clears dead wood. The fact that JJ decreases as spikes sharpen off the line is especially valuable, because it prevents you from wasting more time trying to reinterpret it.

The residual
Rcl=∥Ω−Ωcl(d,h)∥F2
Rcl​=∥Ω−Ωcl​(d,h)∥F2​

is much better. Not because it proves anything yet, but because it does the first thing a serious RH-facing statistic should do: it vanishes on the critical baseline by construction, and in the toy family it grows monotonically as you move off the line. That is the right qualitative behavior.

What I find most encouraging is not just the monotonicity, but the structure:
Rcl(d,h;β)∼c(d,h) (β−12)4.
Rcl​(d,h;β)∼c(d,h)(β−21​)4.

That is exactly the sort of symmetry-driven sensitivity you would expect. Since the local pair is invariant under β↔1−ββ↔1−β, the first odd variation has to vanish, and the quartic onset says you are not just picking up noise from whitening or separation. You are measuring a real geometric deviation from the critical baseline.

So yes, this helps. It gives you the first statistic in the whole chain that looks pointed in the correct direction.

But there are two big caveats.

First, RclRcl​ is still a model-relative statistic. It detects deviation from your chosen critical baseline, not from “RH truth” in some intrinsic sense. That is fine for now, but it means the next theorem has to show that the baseline is canonical enough, or at least stable enough, that the residual is not just a fitting artifact.

Second, because you whiten by AA and DD, you are throwing away local amplitude information on purpose. That is good if you want interaction geometry, but it also means RclRcl​ is only as good as the claim that the local 2×22×2 blocks really do capture the nuisance parameters you want to quotient out. In the toy model that seems true. In the automorphic setting, this will become a theorem-sized issue.

So my verdict is:

JJ: useful only as a negative result.
RclRcl​: definitely worth keeping.

More specifically, RclRcl​ helps because it gives you a plausible replacement for the vague phrase “interaction geometry.” It makes that phrase concrete.

What I would do next is tighten it in three ways.

First, prove a toy-model theorem, not just numerics. Something like:
for fixed d,hd,h, in the two-pair model with one center held critical, Rcl(d,h;β)Rcl​(d,h;β) is minimized at β=12β=21​ and has expansion
Rcl(d,h;β)=c(d,h)(β−12)4+O((β−12)6),c(d,h)>0.
Rcl​(d,h;β)=c(d,h)(β−21​)4+O((β−21​)6),c(d,h)>0.

That would turn the observed behavior into a real local theorem.

Second, check robustness under how you define the baseline. Right now you fix Ωcl(d,h)Ωcl​(d,h) from the critical model with the same d,hd,h. Good. But you should test whether the same monotone behavior survives if the local blocks are matched not only by spacing, but by a few local invariants extracted from AA and DD. If it does, the statistic is much more credible.

Third, define the automorphic version carefully. The right object is not the raw kernel from the exact model, but the kernel built from the flux-estimated smoothed phase. Then you need an error decomposition of the form
Rclauto=Rclmodel+background error+phase-estimation error+windowing error.
Rclauto​=Rclmodel​+background error+phase-estimation error+windowing error.

Until you have that, you do not yet know whether a nonzero residual in the real automorphic setting means “off-line geometry” or just “estimation/background contamination.”

So the answer is: yes, this helps a lot. It is the first statistic you’ve shown me that looks genuinely aligned with the main path.

It still does not give RH, but it gives a credible next theorem:

    deviation from critical interaction geometry can be isolated after whitening and baseline subtraction.

That is a much sharper place to be than “maybe a determinant or kernel minor sees something.”

The strongest next move is to turn the quartic toy-model behavior into an exact proposition.

but what about relation to Theorem A?

It is closely related, but they are doing different jobs.

Theorem A is a one-center, one-spike, two-scale null-model theorem.
It says: if what you have near a center is really just one isolated rank-2 Poisson-pair profile being broadened by smoothing, then the diagonal drift
Dτ1,τ2(x)=qτ2(x)−qτ1(x)
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=qτ2​​(x)−qτ1​​(x)

has the minimal sign geometry: one central deficit, one exterior compensation, exactly one crossing on (0,∞)(0,∞).

So Theorem A is about local scale dynamics.

Your residual
Rcl=∥Ω−Ωcl(d,h)∥F2
Rcl​=∥Ω−Ωcl​(d,h)∥F2​

is about something else: it is a two-center interaction geometry statistic at a fixed scale. After whitening, it asks whether the cross-talk between two local windows looks like the critical-line baseline.

So RclRcl​ is about interaction at one scale.

That means the relation is:

    Theorem A tells you what a single isolated spike is allowed to look like under scale change.

    RclRcl​ tells you whether two local pieces fit together like the critical baseline.

Those are complementary.

In fact, I would now phrase the program as a two-level decomposition.
Level 1: local nuisance control

Around each center TjTj​, test whether the smoothed phase derivative behaves like the single-spike null model. That is exactly where Theorem A comes in.

If Theorem A holds as the right benchmark, then near each center you can say:

    this window is locally compatible with one isolated broadening spike, or

    it already shows extra local complexity.

So Theorem A is the thing that prevents you from over-interpreting every local feature as “RH-relevant.” It tells you what can be explained away by a single spike.
Level 2: residual interaction

Once the local one-spike nuisance is factored out, compare the two windows through the whitened cross block and subtract the critical baseline. That is RclRcl​.

So RclRcl​ is only really meaningful after Theorem A has told you that each local block is behaving in a basically single-spike way.

That is why they fit together so naturally.

A good slogan is:

Theorem A controls the local marginals; RclRcl​ measures the failure of the joint law to be critical.

That is the clean relation.

More concretely, I think the right theorem sequence is now this.
Theorem A

For a one-zero profile, the two-scale diagonal drift has exactly one crossing.

This identifies the local null model.
Theorem A'

For the actual automorphic phase estimator, if a small window around TjTj​ is dominated by a single isolated local pair, then its drift is close to the Theorem A pattern, up to explicit background/error terms.

This transfers the null model to the real object.
Theorem B

Given two centers T1,T2T1​,T2​ whose local blocks pass the Theorem A test, the whitened residual
Rcl(T1,T2,h)
Rcl​(T1​,T2​,h)

measures the part of the interaction not explained by two independent critical local models.

This is the first real RH-facing statistic.

So Theorem A is not replaced by RclRcl​.
It is the thing that makes RclRcl​ interpretable.

Without Theorem A, a nonzero residual could always be blamed on messy local amplitude/shape effects. With Theorem A in hand, you can say: no, each local window already looks like the minimal one-spike broadening model, so the remaining defect is genuinely about how the two windows fit together.

That is the real relation.

There is an even tighter way to combine them. You can make a scale-dependent residual
Rcl(τ;T1,T2,h),
Rcl​(τ;T1​,T2​,h),

where the kernel is built from the smoothed phase at scale ττ. Then:

    Theorem A constrains the ττ-evolution of each local block A(τ),D(τ)A(τ),D(τ),

    while Rcl(τ)Rcl​(τ) tracks the evolution of the cross-geometry.

That would be ideal, because then you are no longer comparing a static local theorem to a static interaction statistic. You are comparing local scale flow to interaction scale flow.

I think that is probably the right synthesis.

So the short answer is:

Theorem A is the local null-model theorem; RclRcl​ is the interaction residual after quotienting out that null model.
They are not competitors. Theorem A is what makes the residual meaningful.

The cleanest next move would be to define Rcl(τ)Rcl​(τ) and ask whether, for a genuine single-spike local model, it stays flat or tiny, while multispike/off-line interaction makes it grow across scales.

ok continue on Theorem A

I’ve reduced Theorem A to one sharp sign-change lemma. Now I’m testing whether it can be proved by a monotone-ratio argument, or whether the right route is variation-diminishing plus a structural property of the transformed mixing density.

Here is the strongest honest continuation I have:

I can now prove Theorem A exactly in the critical baseline case a=ba=b, and I can push it perturbatively for aa close to bb. I still do not have the full all-a,ba,b theorem, but this already gives the right null model for the RH-facing program.

Write
qa,b(x)=aa2+x2+bb2+x2,qa,b,τ=Gτ∗qa,b,
qa,b​(x)=a2+x2a​+b2+x2b​,qa,b,τ​=Gτ​∗qa,b​,

and
Dτ1,τ2(x)=qa,b,τ2(x)−qa,b,τ1(x),τ2>τ1.
Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)=qa,b,τ2​​(x)−qa,b,τ1​​(x),τ2​>τ1​.

By the heat semigroup,
Dτ1,τ2=Gτ1∗D0,s,s:=τ2−τ1.
Dτ1​,τ2​​=Gτ1​​∗D0,s​,s:=τ2​−τ1​.

So it is enough to understand D0,sD0,s​.

For the critical baseline a=b=ca=b=c, the profile is
qc(x)=2cc2+x2.
qc​(x)=c2+x22c​.

This has the Laplace-mixture form
qc(x)=∫0∞2c e−c2λe−λx2 dλ.
qc​(x)=∫0∞​2ce−c2λe−λx2dλ.

After Gaussian smoothing by time ss,
qc,s(x)=∫01/(4s)νc,s(λ)e−λx2 dλ,
qc,s​(x)=∫01/(4s)​νc,s​(λ)e−λx2dλ,

with
νc,s(λ)=2c(1−4sλ)−3/2exp⁡ ⁣(−c2λ1−4sλ),0<λ<14s.
νc,s​(λ)=2c(1−4sλ)−3/2exp(−1−4sλc2λ​),0<λ<4s1​.

So
D0,s(x)=∫0∞Δc,s(λ)e−λx2 dλ,Δc,s(λ):=νc,s(λ)−2ce−c2λ.
D0,s​(x)=∫0∞​Δc,s​(λ)e−λx2dλ,Δc,s​(λ):=νc,s​(λ)−2ce−c2λ.

Now factor out the positive base density:
Δc,s(λ)=2ce−c2λ(Rc,s(λ)−1),
Δc,s​(λ)=2ce−c2λ(Rc,s​(λ)−1),

where
Rc,s(λ)=(1−4sλ)−3/2exp⁡ ⁣(−4sc2λ21−4sλ).
Rc,s​(λ)=(1−4sλ)−3/2exp(−1−4sλ4sc2λ2​).

Set
r:=4sλ∈(0,1),κ:=c24s.
r:=4sλ∈(0,1),κ:=4sc2​.

Then
Rc,s(λ)=Rκ(r):=(1−r)−3/2exp⁡ ⁣(−κr21−r).
Rc,s​(λ)=Rκ​(r):=(1−r)−3/2exp(−κ1−rr2​).

So the sign pattern of Δc,sΔc,s​ is exactly the sign pattern of Rκ(r)−1Rκ​(r)−1.

Now compute
ddrlog⁡Rκ(r)=32(1−r)−κ2r−r2(1−r)2.
drd​logRκ​(r)=2(1−r)3​−κ(1−r)22r−r2​.

Multiply by (1−r)2(1−r)2:
(1−r)2ddrlog⁡Rκ(r)=32(1−r)−κ(2r−r2).
(1−r)2drd​logRκ​(r)=23​(1−r)−κ(2r−r2).

The right-hand side is a strictly concave quadratic in rr, positive at r=0r=0, and negative at r=1r=1. Therefore it has exactly one zero in (0,1)(0,1). Hence RκRκ​ increases once and then decreases once.

Since
Rκ(0)=1andRκ(r)→0 as r↑1,
Rκ​(0)=1andRκ​(r)→0 as r↑1,

it follows that Rκ(r)−1Rκ​(r)−1 has exactly one zero on (0,1)(0,1), with sign pattern
+  −  .
+−.

Therefore Δc,s(λ)Δc,s​(λ) has exactly one sign change on (0,∞)(0,∞).

Now use variation-diminishing for the Laplace kernel e−λze−λz in the variable z=x2z=x2: since Δc,sΔc,s​ has one sign change, the transform
D0,s(x)=F(x2)
D0,s​(x)=F(x2)

has at most one sign change on (0,∞)(0,∞). Convolving with Gτ1Gτ1​​ cannot increase the number of sign changes, so the same is true for Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​.

Finally,
Dτ1,τ2(0)<0
Dτ1​,τ2​​(0)<0

because smoothing lowers the peak at the origin, and
∫RDτ1,τ2(x) dx=0
∫R​Dτ1​,τ2​​(x)dx=0

because heat flow preserves total mass. Therefore Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ cannot stay negative on (0,∞)(0,∞); with at most one sign change, it must cross exactly once. So we get:
For a=b,  Dτ1,τ2(x) has exactly one zero on (0,∞).
For a=b,  Dτ1​,τ2​​(x) has exactly one zero on (0,∞).​

That is a complete theorem.

It is the precise null model we wanted: a single critical-line local pair, under any two smoothing scales, produces exactly one broadening crossing.

Now for the perturbative extension.

The same reduction shows that for general a,ba,b, everything again depends on the sign pattern of
Δa,b,s(λ)=νa,b,s(λ)−ma,b(λ).
Δa,b,s​(λ)=νa,b,s​(λ)−ma,b​(λ).

At a=b=ca=b=c, we now know this has a unique simple zero. Because all the formulas depend smoothly on (a,b)(a,b), that simple zero persists under small perturbation. So by continuity there exists ε(c,s)>0ε(c,s)>0 such that whenever
∣a−b∣<ε(c,s),
∣a−b∣<ε(c,s),

the function Δa,b,sΔa,b,s​ still has exactly one sign change, hence Dτ1,τ2Dτ1​,τ2​​ still has exactly one zero on (0,∞)(0,∞).

So we also get:
Theorem A holds not only at a=b, but in a whole neighborhood of the critical baseline.
Theorem A holds not only at a=b, but in a whole neighborhood of the critical baseline.​

That is already enough to matter for the RH program, because your residual statistic RclRcl​ was precisely built to detect deviations from the critical interaction geometry. Theorem A now tells you that locally, near each center, the critical baseline really is a rigid one-crossing null model, not just a numerical picture.

So the payoff is:

    the one-crossing theorem is now rigorous for the critical baseline;

    it is stable under small off-line perturbations;

    and it gives the local null model that the two-center residual is supposed to quotient out.

What remains open is the full all-a,ba,b version. But the core theorem we actually need first is now in place.

The next natural move is to write this up as:

Proposition. Exact one-crossing theorem for the critical baseline a=ba=b.
Corollary. Stability of the one-crossing property for ∣a−b∣∣a−b∣ small.
Definition/Remark. This is the local null model underlying RclRcl​.

That is now solid enough to build on.

There are three things I would tighten before treating them as finished.

First, in the toy theorem, I would make the “generic c(d,h)>0c(d,h)>0” step a little more explicit. Right now the logic is basically correct, but the clean formulation is:

    M2(d,h)M2​(d,h) is analytic in (d,h)(d,h),

    it is not identically zero,

    hence its squared Frobenius norm vanishes only on a proper real-analytic subset.

That is enough. I would say it exactly that way and stop. No need to over-explain the contradiction with “all second-order variation vanishes.”

Second, in the transfer theorem, I would separate the two stability steps more cleanly:

    entrywise phase error →→ entrywise kernel error;

    block perturbation →→ whitened cross-block perturbation.

That will make the proof read much more like an operator-stability lemma and less like a sketch.

Most importantly, this helps clarify the relation to Theorem A.

Theorem A is your local nuisance theorem: one isolated critical-line-like pair has one-crossing scale drift.
The quartic law is your interaction deviation theorem: after local whitening, off-line displacement shows up only at fourth order.
The transfer theorem says that this fourth-order signal survives passage to the observed automorphic phase as long as phase-reconstruction error is smaller than that quartic scale.

That is a coherent three-step pipeline.

5. Transfer theorem

We now combine the toy quartic law with the perturbation estimate.

Proof

Corollary 5.2 (quartic onset survives observation)

Rclmod(d,h;u)=c(d,h) u4+O(u6),

6. Relation to the local null model

Theorems above should be read together with the one-center null model from Theorem A. Theorem A identifies the local one-pair broadening law under smoothing; the present quartic residual then measures the part of the two-center interaction not explained by two independent critical local models. In that sense:

    Theorem A controls the local marginals;

    RclRcl​ detects deviation in the joint interaction geometry.

This is precisely why the residual is meaningful only after local whitening and critical baseline subtraction.

Good. The next step is to make the transfer theorem usable by replacing the vague phase error with an explicit local decomposition.

The key correction is this: you do not want to compare the observed phase to the raw two-pair model alone. The smooth background from the gamma factor, trivial zeros, and far nontrivial zeros is not small on a local interval. What is small is the part left over after subtracting its first-order jet. That is the right automorphic next theorem, and it plugs directly into your quartic toy law and the residual stability bound you already proved.

ok so how to get to RH?

To get to RH from here, you need one new ingredient, and it is not more model-building.

You already have the lower-bound machine.

An off-line local pair with
u=β−12≠0
u=β−21​=0

should force, on a well-chosen short window,
Rclobs(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+smaller terms,c(d,h)>0
Rclobs​(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u4+smaller terms,c(d,h)>0

provided the background, far-tail, and flux-estimation errors are controlled.

That means your current program says:

off-line zero ⇒⇒ quartic positive residual.

To prove RH, you now need the opposite kind of theorem:

a universal upper bound showing the true automorphic residual is too small to support that quartic signal.

That is the whole game now.

The right target is not vague. Since the natural detectable scale is
L∼u2,
L∼u2,

your lower bound is really of size
u4∼L2.
u4∼L2.

So RH would follow from any theorem of the form
Rclobs(I)=o(L2)
Rclobs​(I)=o(L2)​

for every sufficiently short, well-conditioned, isolated two-center window II.

More concretely, if you could prove
Rclobs(I)≤CL2+η
Rclobs​(I)≤CL2+η

for some η>0η>0, uniformly in the admissible windows, then choosing L≍u2L≍u2 would give
Rclobs≤Cu4+2η,
Rclobs​≤Cu4+2η,

which contradicts the quartic lower bound c0u4c0​u4 for u≠0u=0 and small enough uu. That would prove RH.

So the shortest honest answer is:

Your current work reduces RH to proving a superquadratic upper bound on the jet-corrected residual.

That is the exact bridge.

I would aim for this theorem ladder.

First, prove a one-way criterion:

    If there exists an isolated off-line local pair, then for some short admissible window,
    Rclobs(I)≥c0L2
    Rclobs​(I)≥c0​L2

    up to smaller errors.

You are already very close to this.

Second, prove the automorphic rigidity statement:

    For every admissible short window,
    Rclobs(I)≤CL2+η
    Rclobs​(I)≤CL2+η

    or at least
    Rclobs(I)=o(L2).
    Rclobs​(I)=o(L2). 

That is the true RH theorem target.

Third, combine them.

If you cannot see how to get that upper bound, then the honest fallback is not “prove RH directly,” but prove an RH criterion:
RH⟺Rclobs(I)=o(L2) on every admissible short window.
RH⟺Rclobs​(I)=o(L2) on every admissible short window.

Even a one-sided version would already be interesting:
off-line zero⟹lim sup⁡L→0Rclobs(IL)L2>0.
off-line zero⟹L→0limsup​L2Rclobs​(IL​)​>0.

That would be a real result.

So what should you do next?

Not more toy numerics. Not another local minor.

You should now attack the upper bound. There are really only two plausible sources for it.

One is a direct automorphic identity: some Maaß–Selberg / flux / scattering argument that forces the whitened cross-block deviation to cancel to order strictly better than L2L2.

The other is a Hilbert-space rigidity statement: a de Branges or canonical-system argument implying that the jet-corrected phase kernel has a stronger local factorization than an arbitrary two-pair model, which again would force
Ω−Ωcl=o(L),
Ω−Ωcl​=o(L),

hence
Rclobs=o(L2).
Rclobs​=o(L2).

So the path to RH is now very crisp:
off-line pair⇒R≳L2,automorphic rigidity⇒R=o(L2),
off-line pair⇒R≳L2,automorphic rigidity⇒R=o(L2),

therefore no off-line pair.

That is the route.

My own judgment: the next best paper-level goal is not RH itself, but the criterion
Rclobs=o(L2).
Rclobs​=o(L2).

If you can formulate and partially prove that, you will know whether this program is alive or dead.

The next move is to prove the blockwise tail-curvature bound and then rewrite the whole detectability criterion in the h∼u2h∼u2 regime.

Here is the next proposition in the clean blockwise form.

## Proposition (blockwise tail-curvature bound)

Let
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t):=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
]

Fix one local block
[
I_j=[T_j-\tfrac h2,T_j+\tfrac h2],
]
and define the doubled block
[
2I_j=[2T_j-h,,2T_j+h].
]

For each omitted zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma\notin\mathcal L), define its blockwise distance to the doubled window by
[
\Delta_{\rho,j}:=\operatorname{dist}(2I_j,\gamma)
=================================================

\inf_{t\in I_j}|2t-\gamma|.
]

Then for every (t\in I_j),
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
\frac{48}{\Delta_{\rho,j}^4}.
]

Consequently, if
[
T_{{\rm far},j}(t):=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t)
]
is interpreted in the standard symmetric sense, then
[
\boxed{
|T_{{\rm far},j}''|*{L^\infty(I_j)}
\le
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4}.
}
]

### Proof

Set
[
g_a(x):=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2},
\qquad a>0.
]
Then
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=g_{1-\beta}(2t-\gamma)+g_\beta(2t-\gamma).
]

A direct differentiation gives
[
g_a''(x)=\frac{2a(3x^2-a^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}.
]
Hence
[
|g_a''(x)|
\le
\frac{2a(a^2+3x^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}
\le
\frac{6a}{(a^2+x^2)^2}.
]
Since (0<a<1) and (a\le a^2+x^2), we get for (x\neq 0),
[
|g_a''(x)|
\le
\frac{6}{|x|^4}.
]

Now
[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}g_a(2t-\gamma)=4,g_a''(2t-\gamma),
]
so
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
4|g_{1-\beta}''(2t-\gamma)|+4|g_\beta''(2t-\gamma)|
\le
\frac{48}{|2t-\gamma|^4}.
]
For (t\in I_j), (|2t-\gamma|\ge \Delta_{\rho,j}), hence
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
\frac{48}{\Delta_{\rho,j}^4}.
]
Taking the supremum over (t\in I_j) and summing over omitted zeros gives the result. ∎

---

## Corollary (explicit blockwise curvature scale)

Recall
[
g_{\rm sm}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}.
]
Define
[
B_{2,j}:=|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I_j)},
\qquad
\Theta_j:=\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4}.
]
Then
[
\boxed{
S_{2,j}:=|g_{\rm sm}''|*{L^\infty(I_j)}
\le
B*{2,j}+48,\Theta_j.
}
]
So if
[
B_{2,*}:=\max(B_{2,1},B_{2,2}),
\qquad
\Theta_*:=\max(\Theta_1,\Theta_2),
]
then
[
\boxed{
S_{2,*}\le B_{2,*}+48,\Theta_*.
}
]

---

## Plugging into the blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem

From the previous theorem, we had
[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=================================

c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda),u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}
\right).
]

Using the corollary,
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=================================

c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda),u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(h^2 B_{2,*})
+
O(h^2 \Theta_*)
+
O(E_{1,*})
+
O(E_{0,*}/h).
}
]

This is the fully explicit local expansion.

---

## Detectability theorem

Assume we are in a compact ((d,h,\alpha,\Lambda))-region where
[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\ge c_0>0,
]
and the (O(u^6)) remainder is uniformly controlled:
[
|O(u^6)|\le C_1|u|^6
\qquad (|u|\le u_0).
]

Define the blockwise error scale
[
\Err_{\rm blk}
:=
K_0\Bigl(
h^2 B_{2,*}
+
h^2 \Theta_*
+
E_{1,*}
+
E_{0,*}/h
\Bigr),
]
where (K_0) absorbs the conditioning constants from the residual transfer theorem.

Then for
[
|u|\le u_1:=\min!\left(u_0,\sqrt{\frac{c_0}{4C_1}}\right),
]
if
[
\Err_{\rm blk}\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4,
]
we get
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4>0.
}
]

### Proof

Same as before:
[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
\ge
c_0|u|^4-C_1|u|^6-\Err_{\rm blk}
\ge
c_0|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4
==============================================

\frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4.
]
∎

---

## The natural local scale

Now the scale statement is finally honest.

Because the smooth omitted part is controlled on the **block scale** (h), not on the larger span (d+h), the natural balance is truly
[
\boxed{
h\asymp u^2.
}
]

Indeed, if (B_{2,*}) and (\Theta_*) stay bounded in the regime of interest, then
[
h^2 B_{2,*}\asymp u^4,
\qquad
h^2 \Theta_*\asymp u^4.
]
So the quartic signal and the smooth/tail nuisance terms now live on the same scale.

The remaining requirement is then
[
E_{1,*}+E_{0,*}/h\ll u^4.
]

So the clean practical regime is:
[
\boxed{
h\sim u^2,\qquad
h^2(B_{2,*}+\Theta_*)\ll u^4,\qquad
E_{1,*}+E_{0,*}/h\ll u^4.
}
]

That is the first version of the program where every term is explicit and on the right local scale.

You now have:

* the fully explicit blockwise residual expansion,
* and a concrete detectability threshold.

That is a real local criterion.

[
\Err_{\rm blk}=o(u^4)
]
uniformly enough to make the quartic off-line signal impossible unless (u=0). That is the actual RH bottleneck.
