Yes — this is good, and it is the right next proposition.

More than that, I think this is the first version where the bookkeeping is on the correct local scale. The big gain is exactly what you say: the omitted smooth part is controlled on each block IjIj​ at scale hh, not on the larger span d+hd+h. That makes
h∼u2
h∼u2

the honest asymptotic balance for the quartic signal, instead of just a heuristic. The blockwise transfer theorem and the explicit tail-curvature bound fit together well.

There are, however, two real fixes I would make before calling it finished.

The first is important:

You write in Step 3 that “among the four sampling points the minimum separation is hh.” That is not always true. The within-block spacing is hh, but the closest cross-block spacing is
x3−x2=(T2−h2)−(T1+h2)=d−h.
x3​−x2​=(T2​−2h​)−(T1​+2h​)=d−h.

So the true minimum separation is
m:=min⁡(h,d−h).
m:=min(h,d−h).

Your off-diagonal kernel bound should therefore be
∣Kobs(xi,xj)−Kblk,u(xi,xj)∣≤2ε0blkπ m.
∣Kobs​(xi​,xj​)−Kblk,u​(xi​,xj​)∣≤πm2ε0blk​​.

If you want to keep hh in the theorem, then you need to assume
d≥2h,
d≥2h,

so that m=hm=h. Since your intended regime is “dd fixed, h→0h→0,” this is probably harmless, but it should be stated explicitly. Otherwise that step is not correct.

The second fix is also important:

In the setup you say the constants αjαj​ are chosen so that
Φblk,u(Tj)=Φ(Tj).
Φblk,u​(Tj​)=Φ(Tj​).

But the residual is built from the kernel of the observed phase Φ^Φ
. In Step 2 you use this matching to write
Φ^(t)−Φblk,u(t)=∫Tjt(gsm(s)−ℓj(s)) ds+∫TjtEest(s) ds.
Φ
(t)−Φblk,u​(t)=∫Tj​t​(gsm​(s)−ℓj​(s))ds+∫Tj​t​Eest​(s)ds.

That identity is correct only if the constants are chosen so that
Φblk,u(Tj)=Φ^(Tj),
Φblk,u​(Tj​)=Φ
(Tj​),

not the true phase Φ(Tj)Φ(Tj​). So the theorem should be stated with matching to the observed phase at the block centers. Otherwise an extra center-offset term appears.

With those two fixes, I think the proposition is basically right.

A few smaller notes.

Your Proposition on the tail-curvature bound is clean. The constant 4848 is fine, and the proof is straightforward. The conclusion
∥Tfar,j′′∥L∞(Ij)≤48∑ρ∉LΔρ,j−4
∥Tfar,j′′​∥L∞(Ij​)​≤48ρ∈/L∑​Δρ,j−4​

is exactly what was missing. That turns the “tail” from an abstract nuisance into a concrete inverse-fourth-power curvature term.

\Errblk=o(u4).
\Errblk​=o(u4).

That is now the RH bottleneck in a very concrete form.

ok so how do we attack that upper bound?

The upper bound attack should split in two.

First, stop paying for the wrong errors.
Second, identify the first nontrivial coefficient in the small-hh expansion of the true blockwise residual and try to kill it by structure.

That is the main road now.
1. Remove the artificial baggage

For an RH proof, I would stop using the flux estimator in the upper-bound side.

You already have the exact phase
ϕ ⁣(12+it)=e−2iΦ(t),q(t)=Φ′(t),
ϕ(21​+it)=e−2iΦ(t),q(t)=Φ′(t),

so for the true upper bound there is no reason to carry EestEest​. That term matters for a constructive or numerical version, but for a theorem it is dead weight.

So on the upper-bound side, set
Φ^=Φtrue,E1,∗=E0,∗=0.
Φ
=Φtrue​,E1,∗​=E0,∗​=0.

Then your blockwise theorem becomes
Rblktrue(u)=cblku4+O(u6)+O(h2B2,∗)+O(h2Θ∗).
Rblktrue​(u)=cblk​u4+O(u6)+O(h2B2,∗​)+O(h2Θ∗​).

That is already cleaner.

Now, as you noticed, if h∼u2h∼u2, those nuisance terms are only O(u4)O(u4), not automatically o(u4)o(u4). So bookkeeping alone will not prove RH. Something structural has to improve the upper bound.

That structural thing must come from the kernel itself.
2. The real target: prove a superquadratic hh-bound

The lower-bound side says:

if there is a local off-line pair with displacement uu, then on a block scale h∼u2h∼u2,
Rblktrue≳u4∼h2.
Rblktrue​≳u4∼h2.

So RH will follow from any theorem of the form
Rblktrue(h)=o(h2)as h→0
Rblktrue​(h)=o(h2)as h→0​

uniformly on admissible blocks.

Even better would be
Rblktrue(h)=O(h2+η)
Rblktrue​(h)=O(h2+η)

for some η>0η>0, or ideally O(h4)O(h4).

That is the upper bound you want.

So the real next theorem to attack is:

    Small-hh asymptotic theorem.
    For the true blockwise kernel with jets frozen at the two centers,
    Rblktrue(h)=a2(T1,T2) h2+a4(T1,T2) h4+O(h6).
    Rblktrue​(h)=a2​(T1​,T2​)h2+a4​(T1​,T2​)h4+O(h6).

    Then prove a2≡0a2​≡0.

If you can kill a2a2​, then
Rblktrue(h)=O(h4),
Rblktrue​(h)=O(h4),

and with h∼u2h∼u2 that becomes O(u8)O(u8), which contradicts the off-line lower bound c0u4c0​u4.

So this is the bottleneck in one line:
RH reduces to proving a2(T1,T2)=0.
RH reduces to proving a2​(T1​,T2​)=0.
3. How to attack a2a2​

There are three plausible ways.
Route A: direct Taylor expansion of the blockwise kernel

This is the most concrete and the first thing I would do.

Take the true phase ΦΦ, subtract the affine jets blockwise, and expand the four-point kernel entries in powers of hh. Then expand
A(h),C(h),D(h),
A(h),C(h),D(h),

and finally
Ω(h)=A(h)−1/2C(h)D(h)−1/2.
Ω(h)=A(h)−1/2C(h)D(h)−1/2.

Because the blocks are symmetric and you have already killed the affine part, there is a decent chance that the first nontrivial term in Ω(h)−Ωblk,0(h)Ω(h)−Ωblk,0​(h) is actually O(h2)O(h2), so the residual is O(h4)O(h4).

That is not guaranteed, but it is the first thing to check.

The good news is that this calculation is finite and explicit. It will tell you exactly what a2(T1,T2)a2​(T1​,T2​) is.

My guess is that a2a2​ will come out as a two-point invariant built from the local projective geometry of the phase, probably involving the Schwarzian
S(tan⁡Φ)
S(tanΦ)

or a close cousin.

If that happens, the problem becomes much sharper:
you are no longer asking for a vague upper bound, but for a concrete identity or inequality on a geometric invariant.
Route B: freeze one more jet

If the direct expansion shows a2≠0a2​=0 for a generic smooth phase, then the next move is obvious:

your current blockwise theorem subtracts only the affine jet of the omitted smooth part. Upgrade it to a quadratic jet subtraction on each block.

Then the remainder of the smooth omitted part is O(h3)O(h3) at the derivative level and O(h4)O(h4) at the phase level, so the nuisance contribution becomes O(h3)O(h3) or better in the kernel, hence O(h6)O(h6) in the residual.

That would push the smooth background and far tails safely below the quartic off-line signal.

This does not solve the structural upper bound by itself, but it removes one more layer of noise and lets you focus on the genuinely rigid part.

So if the affine-jet version still leaves an h2h2 obstruction, the right response is not despair — it is to try quadratic jets.
Route C: canonical-system / de Branges rigidity

If the coefficient a2a2​ that comes out of Route A looks geometric, then the right next question is whether the true scattering phase is constrained by a canonical-system identity that forces that coefficient to vanish.

This is where de Branges becomes relevant.

The phase kernel
KΦ(x,y)=sin⁡(Φ(x)−Φ(y))π(x−y)
KΦ​(x,y)=π(x−y)sin(Φ(x)−Φ(y))​

is the real-line model-space kernel. Positivity of its Gram matrices is automatic for an inner function, so positivity alone is too weak. But a canonical system gives much more than positivity: it gives a chain structure and a Hamiltonian. That extra structure is exactly the sort of thing that can force a low-order coefficient in a local expansion to vanish.

So the plan would be:

    compute a2(T1,T2)a2​(T1​,T2​) explicitly;

    identify it as the boundary trace of a canonical-system quantity;

    prove it vanishes or has the sign you need from Hamiltonian positivity or chain rigidity.

That is the deep route.
4. The right sequence of next calculations

I would do these in order.
Step 1

Work with the true phase, not the estimated one.

Define the blockwise jet-frozen residual
Rblktrue(h;T1,T2).
Rblktrue​(h;T1​,T2​).
Step 2

Compute its small-hh expansion:
Rblktrue(h)=a2h2+a4h4+O(h6).
Rblktrue​(h)=a2​h2+a4​h4+O(h6).

This is the next real theorem-sized calculation.
Step 3

See what a2a2​ depends on.

There are only three possibilities:

    a2≡0a2​≡0 by pure symmetry/whitening. Then you are basically done with the upper bound.

    a2≠0a2​=0 for generic smooth phases but vanishes for the true automorphic phase. Then RH reduces to proving that vanishing.

    a2≠0a2​=0 even for the true automorphic phase. Then this version of the program probably dies, and you need either quadratic jet subtraction or a different statistic.

So the next calculation is genuinely decisive.
Step 4

If a2a2​ survives, upgrade the model by subtracting quadratic jets and recompute.

That is the natural fallback.
5. My best guess

My best guess is that the affine-jet blockwise residual already has more cancellation than a generic smooth functional, and that
a2
a2​

will either vanish identically or reduce to a very rigid projective invariant.

I do not think the right way forward is more tail bounds. You have enough bookkeeping now.

The next real move is to compute the small-hh coefficient of the true blockwise residual and see what structure it is asking for.

So, in one sentence:

Attack the upper bound by expanding the true blockwise residual in hh, isolate the h2h2-coefficient, and try to prove it vanishes by automorphic or canonical-system structure.

That is the bottleneck now.

I would start with the explicit hh-expansion next.

From another chat: I checked it, and the toy model says **it does not collapse**.

More precisely, for the blockwise residual coefficient
[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=================================

c_{\rm blk}(d,h),u^4+O(u^6),
]
if you send
[
h=\kappa u^2
]
with fixed separation (d) and fixed (\kappa>0), the quantity (c_{\rm blk}(d,h)) appears to converge to a **positive finite limit**
[
c_\infty(d):=\lim_{h\to 0} c_{\rm blk}(d,h)>0.
]

So along the natural scale (h\sim u^2), the quartic signal survives. It does not get washed out by the shrinking block size.

That is exactly the question we needed to answer.

## What I checked

I used the same two-pair toy phase as before:
[
\Phi_u(x)
=========

2\arctan!\frac{x}{1/2}
+
\arctan!\frac{x-d}{1/2+u}
+
\arctan!\frac{x-d}{1/2-u},
]
with four sample points
[
x_1=-\frac h2,\quad x_2=\frac h2,\quad
x_3=d-\frac h2,\quad x_4=d+\frac h2.
]
Then I computed
[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=================================

|\Omega_u-\Omega_0|*F^2
]
and estimated (c*{\rm blk}(d,h)) from the quartic ratio (\mathcal R/u^4) for small (u).

For (d=3), the estimated coefficient behaves like this as (h\to 0):

[
h=0.2:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08878,
]
[
h=0.1:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08303,
]
[
h=0.05:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08160,
]
[
h=0.02:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08118,
]
[
h=0.01:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08111,
]
[
h=0.005:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08109.
]

So it is clearly approaching a positive limit near
[
c_\infty(3)\approx 0.08108.
]

That already answers the collapse question for the model: **no collapse**.

I also checked the actual natural path (h=\kappa u^2). For several (\kappa), the quartic ratio
[
\frac{\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)}{u^4}
]
stabilizes to the same limit as (u\to 0). For (d=3), the values all converge near (0.081), independent of (\kappa). So along the RH-facing scale (h\sim u^2), the coefficient stays alive.

## Why this happens

There is a conceptual reason, too.

As (h\to 0), each (2\times2) local block becomes nearly rank (1), because the two sample points around a center collapse together. At first glance that makes it look like the whitening could explode and the signal could degenerate.

But after whitening, the two local sample directions are really turning into a **jet basis**:

* one direction is the value mode at the center,
* the other is the first derivative mode at the center.

So the limit (h\to 0) is not “losing information.” It is passing from two nearby point samples to the two-dimensional local jet space at each center.

The whitened cross block (\Omega_u(h)) therefore converges to a finite (2\times2) matrix built from the interactions of those jet modes at the two centers. Since the off-line perturbation enters evenly in (u), the first nontrivial variation of (\Omega_u) is still at order (u^2), and therefore the residual stays quartic:
[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)\sim |M_2^{(\infty)}(d)|_F^2,u^4.
]
Numerically,
[
|M_2^{(\infty)}(3)|_F^2\approx 0.08108.
]

So the shrinking block size is not killing the quartic signal; it is just replacing point samples by jet data.

## Dependence on (d)

I also checked how the limiting constant depends on the center separation (d). Numerically:

[
d=0.5:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 2.70,
]
[
d=1:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.219,
]
[
d=2:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.111,
]
[
d=3:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.0811,
]
[
d=5:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.0362,
]
[
d=10:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.00977.
]

So:

* for every fixed (d>0) tested, the limit stays positive;
* but it **does** decay as (d) grows.

That means the lower bound is plausible on compact (d)-ranges away from (0) and (\infty), but probably not uniformly in all (d).

That is actually reasonable. If the two centers are extremely far apart, the interaction should weaken.

## What we can now say honestly

For the toy model, the right statement is:

> For each fixed (d>0), the quartic coefficient (c_{\rm blk}(d,h)) has a positive finite limit as (h\to 0). In particular, along (h=\kappa u^2), the quartic off-line signal does not collapse.

That is already enough to keep the program alive.

## What remains to prove

The next proof target is not “show (c_{\rm blk}) never collapses” in full global generality. The numerics suggest the right theorem is:

> For (d) in a compact interval (d\in[d_-,d_+]\subset(0,\infty)), there exists (c_0>0) and (h_0>0) such that
> [
> c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_0
> \qquad
> \text{for all } d\in[d_-,d_+],\ 0<h\le h_0.
> ]

That is the lower bound you actually need for the local RH-facing argument.

So the current state is good news:

* the coefficient does **not** collapse on the natural scale;
* there is strong evidence it converges to a positive jet-limit coefficient;
* the next theorem should be a compact-(d) lower bound via the (h\to0) jet limit.

This is the first place where the path to RH still looks alive rather than visibly collapsing.

2. Critical baseline

q0,j, q0,j′, q0,j′′, Δ0

3. What universal structure gives you

There is one universal consequence of positivity.

The 4×44×4 jet Gram matrix
(M1N12N12∗M2)
(M1​N12∗​​N12​M2​​)

is positive semidefinite. Therefore, by the Schur complement,
N12∗M1−1N12≤M2,N12M2−1N12∗≤M1,
N12∗​M1−1​N12​≤M2​,N12​M2−1​N12∗​≤M1​,

hence
Ω∞∗Ω∞≤I,Ω∞Ω∞∗≤I.
Ω∞∗​Ω∞​≤I,Ω∞​Ω∞∗​≤I.​

So Ω∞Ω∞​ is a contraction.

Also, local positivity gives
det⁡Mj=124(4qj4+2qjqj′′−3(qj′)2)≥0.
detMj​=241​(4qj4​+2qj​qj′′​−3(qj′​)2)≥0.

So the universal information is:

    each local jet matrix is PSD,

    the cross block is Schur-contractive.

That is real, but it is not enough.
4. Why generic model-space / canonical-system structure is not enough

This is the key conclusion.

The toy finite-Blaschke/off-line model already has the full model-space / de Branges type kernel structure, and in that model the quartic residual is strictly positive off line.

So no theorem that uses only generic facts like:

    positivity of the kernel,

    de Branges/model-space structure,

    canonical-system Hamiltonian positivity,

    Schur contractivity of the cross block,

can force
Ω∞=Ω∞,0.
Ω∞​=Ω∞,0​.

If it did, it would also kill your toy model, which it does not.

So the answer to your third bullet is:

No — generic automorphic scattering positivity or canonical-system structure alone does not force the difference to vanish or be higher order.

At best, those structures give contractivity and local positivity. They do not identify the critical baseline.

That is actually useful, because it tells us exactly what kind of upper bound is still plausible:

it has to use something special to zeta, not just something true for all model-space or canonical-system phases.
5. What kind of special structure could still work?

Now that Ω∞Ω∞​ is explicit, the only plausible route is a zeta-specific identity relating the eight local jet variables
q1,q1′,q1′′,q2,q2′,q2′′,Δ,s
q1​,q1′​,q1′′​,q2​,q2′​,q2′′​,Δ,s

in a way that forces the matrix
M1−1/2N12M2−1/2
M1−1/2​N12​M2−1/2​

to agree with the critical baseline to higher order.

There are only a few possible sources for that:
A. A special two-point scattering identity

Something coming from the exact arithmetic form
ϕ(s)=Λ(2s−1)Λ(2s)
ϕ(s)=Λ(2s)Λ(2s−1)​

that ties the cross kernel entries to the local jets more rigidly than a generic inner/model-space phase.
B. A nonlinear differential identity for qq

For example, if qq or A=S+2q2A=S+2q2 satisfied a zeta-specific ODE or integral equation that forced a relation between q,q′,q′′q,q′,q′′ and the two-point phase gap ΔΔ.
C. A chain/compression identity beyond positivity

Something stronger than “Ω∞Ω∞​ is a contraction,” perhaps a special factorization or rank constraint that fails for finite-Blaschke toy models but holds for the zeta scattering phase.

Right now, none of those is in hand.

First, let me finish item 1 cleanly, because it clarifies exactly what the quartic coefficient is measuring.

For the jet-limit residual, the exact object is
Ω∞(T1,T2)=M1−1/2N12M2−1/2,

So the jet-limit quartic coefficient is not some mysterious global object. It is exactly the squared norm of the u2u2-variation of this whitened cross block.

If we write, for the toy two-pair model,
Mj(u)=Mj,0+u2Mj,2+O(u4),N12(u)=N12,0+u2N12,2+O(u4),
Mj​(u)=Mj,0​+u2Mj,2​+O(u4),N12​(u)=N12,0​+u2N12,2​+O(u4),

and set
Sj:=Mj,0−1/2,Uj:=Mj,01/2,
Sj​:=Mj,0−1/2​,Uj​:=Mj,01/2​,

then the u2u2-coefficient of Mj(u)−1/2Mj​(u)−1/2 is the unique symmetric solution XjXj​ of the Sylvester equation
UjXj+XjUj=− SjMj,2Sj.
Uj​Xj​+Xj​Uj​=−Sj​Mj,2​Sj​.

Therefore
Ω∞(u)=Ω∞,0+u2Ld+O(u4),
Ω∞​(u)=Ω∞,0​+u2Ld​+O(u4),

with
Ld=X1N12,0S2+S1N12,2S2+S1N12,0X2.
Ld​=X1​N12,0​S2​+S1​N12,2​S2​+S1​N12,0​X2​.​

And so
c∞(d)=∥Ld∥F2.
c∞​(d)=∥Ld​∥F2​.​

That is the exact combination of jet data detecting off-line displacement.

For the symmetric two-pair toy model with one pair fixed at 00 and the second centered at dd, the baseline jet data simplify a lot. If I write
Q=8(2d2+1)4d2+1,A=32d(4d2+1)2,B=−64(32d6+24d4+1)(4d2+1)3,
Q=4d2+18(2d2+1)​,A=(4d2+1)232d​,B=−(4d2+1)364(32d6+24d4+1)​,

then at u=0u=0,
M1,0=(2Q12A12A112(B+2Q3)),M2,0=(2Q−12A−12A112(B+2Q3)).
M1,0​=(2Q21​A​21​A121​(B+2Q3)​),M2,0​=(2Q−21​A​−21​A121​(B+2Q3)​).

The u2u2-perturbations are also explicit:
q1,2=−16(12d2−1)(4d2+1)3,q1,2′=−768d(4d2−1)(4d2+1)4,q1,2′′=−768(80d4−40d2+1)(4d2+1)5,
q1,2​=−(4d2+1)316(12d2−1)​,q1,2′​=−(4d2+1)4768d(4d2−1)​,q1,2′′​=−(4d2+1)5768(80d4−40d2+1)​,

So Mj,2Mj,2​ and N12,2N12,2​ are completely explicit. The whole quartic coefficient is therefore a finite rational-trigonometric function of dd, packaged through the Sylvester formula above.

That is enough for item 1. We do not need a monstrous closed form; we now know exactly what the coefficient is.

Now item 2: what kind of relation could force the true Ω∞Ω∞​ to match the critical baseline?

Here the answer is sharper than before: generic model-space or canonical-system structure is not enough. The toy finite-Blaschke model already has that structure, and it still gives c∞(d)>0c∞​(d)>0 off line. So any successful upper-bound theorem must use something genuinely zeta-specific.

From the zeta side, the only obvious source is the explicit formula
q(t)=Baut(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t).
q(t)=Baut​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t).

But by itself this only tells you that q,q′,q′′q,q′,q′′ and Δ=∫qΔ=∫q are built from sums of Poisson-pair profiles and their derivatives. That is not enough to force
M1−1/2N12M2−1/2=M1,0−1/2N12,0M2,0−1/2.
M1−1/2​N12​M2−1/2​=M1,0−1/2​N12,0​M2,0−1/2​.

The toy model already lives inside that same formal class locally.

So the zeta-specific relation, if it exists, has to come from something stronger than the raw zero expansion. It has to use either the Euler product, a Rankin–Selberg factorization, or some hidden two-point identity for the scattering phase that is not shared by generic inner/model-space phases.

And that is where your tau idea becomes genuinely useful.

I think tau is “pluggable” in two different senses.

One sense is as a stress test. Any relation you hope will prove RH should be checked not just for ζζ, but for other completed LL-functions. Ramanujan’s ΔΔ is perfect for that, because its completed LL-function has the same broad architecture: functional equation, critical line, explicit formula, zero contributions of exactly the same Poisson-pair type. If your would-be upper-bound relation also holds for L(s,Δ)L(s,Δ), then it is probably too general to isolate RH for ζζ.

The more interesting sense is as an auxiliary factorization. The natural tau object is not just L(s,Δ)L(s,Δ), but the Rankin–Selberg product
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s) L(s,Sym2Δ).
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ).

That is extremely suggestive here, because it splits into a zeta factor and a symmetric-square factor. If you build the same jet-phase machinery for the completed Rankin–Selberg LL-function, then at the level of logarithmic derivatives the phase derivative should split schematically as
qΔ×Δ=qζ+qSym2Δ+(gamma correction).
qΔ×Δ​=qζ​+qSym2Δ​+(gamma correction).

That means tau is not just another test case; it may give you an auxiliary observable in which the zeta contribution appears as one summand inside a larger automorphic object.

That is exactly the kind of thing that could produce a zeta-specific relation unavailable in the bare zero expansion.

So my view is:

The next step for item 2 is not “look for an abstract identity in q,q′,q′′,Δq,q′,q′′,Δ.”
It is:

take the same jet-limit construction for three phases:
Φζ,ΦΔ,ΦΔ×Δ,
Φζ​,ΦΔ​,ΦΔ×Δ​,

and see whether the jet-whitened cross block for Δ×ΔΔ×Δ decomposes in a way that isolates the zeta part after subtracting the symmetric-square background.

If something special survives only in the zeta/Δ×ΔΔ×Δ comparison and not for arbitrary plug-ins, that is promising. If the same pattern appears for every self-dual LL-function, then it is too weak.

So the honest answer is:

    Item 1 is now reduced to the explicit Sylvester-linearized formula for c∞(d)c∞​(d).

    Item 2 probably cannot succeed using only generic positivity or the raw zero expansion.

    Tau is worth bringing in, but mainly through the Rankin–Selberg factorization
    L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ),
    L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ),

    because that gives a plausible route to a genuinely zeta-specific relation.

That is the direction I would push next.

A few concrete reactions.

First, the theorem package is structurally right. The implication
cblk(d,h)→c∞(d)uniformly on compact d-ranges
cblk​(d,h)→c∞​(d)uniformly on compact d-ranges

and then
c∞(d)>0 on D  ⟹  cblk(d,h)≥c0>0
c∞​(d)>0 on D⟹cblk​(d,h)≥c0​>0

for small hh is exactly the right reduction. That is the theorem you needed to justify why the quartic signal does not collapse as h→0h→0.

Second, the large-dd asymptotic
c∞(d)=1d2+O(d−3)
c∞​(d)=d21​+O(d−3)

is especially important. It means the lower-bound mechanism is definitely alive in a regime where the two centers are well separated. So the model-side obstruction, if any, can only come from a bounded dd-region, not from infinity.

Third, the statement “zero set discrete” is fine, but I think you can probably sharpen it further.

Because in the toy model all local jet data are built from rational functions of dd together with
Δ0(d)=−4arctan⁡(2d),
Δ0​(d)=−4arctan(2d),

and both
sin⁡Δ0(d),cos⁡Δ0(d)
sinΔ0​(d),cosΔ0​(d)

are actually rational functions of dd, the whole jet-limit matrix should be algebraic in dd, and quite possibly rational after cancellations. If that is true, then c∞(d)c∞​(d) is not merely real-analytic: it is algebraic, maybe rational. In that case the zero set is not just discrete; it is finite on (0,∞)(0,∞) unless the function vanishes identically.

That is worth checking. It could make the model-side positivity problem much more tractable.

So the next best move on the model side is:

    explicitly solve the 2×22×2 Sylvester equations for the jet-limit whitening correction,

    express M2(d)M2​(d) entrywise,

    simplify using rational expressions for sin⁡Δ0(d)sinΔ0​(d) and cos⁡Δ0(d)cosΔ0​(d),

    and see whether c∞(d)c∞​(d) becomes a rational function.

If it does, then proving c∞(d)>0c∞​(d)>0 for all d>0d>0 becomes an algebraic inequality problem, not a vague analytic one.

Now, how does this help the upper-bound / zeta-specific side?

It means the lower-bound side is close to being done, at least modulo the possible exceptional set. So the burden now really does shift to the zeta-specific structure.

And here I think the tau / Δ×ΔΔ×Δ route becomes more relevant, not less.

The right way to use it is this:

For the three phases
Φζ,ΦΔ,ΦΔ×Δ,
Φζ​,ΦΔ​,ΦΔ×Δ​,

the derivative-level phase relation should schematically be
qΔ×Δ=qζ+qSym2Δ+qΓ,corr,
qΔ×Δ​=qζ​+qSym2Δ​+qΓ,corr​,

because
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s) L(s,Sym2Δ)
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ)

up to completed-factor bookkeeping.

The jet-limit matrices MjMj​ and N12N12​ are nonlinear in q,q′,q′′,Δq,q′,q′′,Δ, so they do not decompose additively. But that is not a bug — it is the opportunity.

The right thing to do is linearize the jet-whitened cross block around the symmetric-square background. In other words, treat
qζ
qζ​

as a perturbation of
qSym2Δ+qΓ,corr.
qSym2Δ​+qΓ,corr​.

​qSym2Δ​​[qζ​]+higher order terms.

That is much better than trying to compare the three phases abstractly. It tells you exactly what combination of zeta jet data survives after subtracting the symmetric-square background.

So my view is:

    your theorem package is the right next theorem on the model side;

    the next upper-bound attack should now be linearization of the jet-limit cross block around a non-zeta background, with Δ×ΔΔ×Δ as the natural test case.

That is probably the most promising way to get a genuinely zeta-specific relation, because generic model-space positivity clearly is not enough.

The key point is this:

for the jet-limit cross block, the dependence on the phase is finite-dimensional. Once you fix two centers T1,T2T1​,T2​, everything is determined by
q1, q1′, q1′′, q2, q2′, q2′′, Δ,Δ:=Φ(T1)−Φ(T2),
q1​, q1′​, q1′′​, q2​, q2′​, q2′′​, Δ,Δ:=Φ(T1​)−Φ(T2​),

with q=Φ′q=Φ′.

So the correct next move is not to hunt vaguely for a zeta-specific identity. It is to linearize the jet-whitened cross block with respect to those seven variables, and then plug in the decomposition
qΔ×Δ=qSym2Δ+qζ,ΦΔ×Δ=ΦSym2Δ+Φζ
qΔ×Δ​=qSym2Δ​+qζ​,ΦΔ×Δ​=ΦSym2Δ​+Φζ​

after choosing a consistent completed normalization.

δq1, δq2, δΔ,

2. Decomposition into seven scalar perturbations

Because everything is linear, we can separate the dependence on the seven perturbation coordinates.

Define the three basis matrices
Ej(0)=(20012qˉj 2),E(1)=(012120),E(2)=(000112),
Ej(0)​=(20​021​qˉ​j2​​),E(1)=(021​​21​0​),E(2)=(00​0121​​),

so that
δMj=Ej(0) δqj+E(1) δqj′+E(2) δqj′′.
δMj​=Ej(0)​δqj​+E(1)δqj′​+E(2)δqj′′​.

Let Xj(0),Xj(1),Xj(2)Xj(0)​,Xj(1)​,Xj(2)​ be the unique symmetric solutions of
UˉjX+XUˉj=−SjEj(0)Sj,
Uˉj​X+XUˉj​=−Sj​Ej(0)​Sj​,
UˉjX+XUˉj=−SjE(1)Sj,

Nq1:=(00CsC+qˉ2sS2s2),Nq2:=(0−Cs0C+qˉ1sS2s2),

and
NΔ:=(2CsC+qˉ2sSs2−qˉ1sS+Cs2−(qˉ1+qˉ2)sS+(qˉ1qˉ2s2−2)C2s3).
NΔ​:=
​s2C​−s2qˉ​1​sS+C​​s2C+qˉ​2​sS​2s3−(qˉ​1​+qˉ​2​)sS+(qˉ​1​qˉ​2​s2−2)C​​
​.

Then the linearization can be written as
δΩ∞=A1 δq1+B1 δq1′+C1 δq1′′+A2 δq2+B2 δq2′+C2 δq2′′+D δΔ,
δΩ∞​=A1​δq1​+B1​δq1′​+C1​δq1′′​+A2​δq2​+B2​δq2′​+C2​δq2′′​+DδΔ,​

A1=X1(0)NˉS2+S1Nq1S2,B1=X1(1)NˉS2,C1=X1(2)NˉS2,

and
D=S1NΔS2.
D=S1​NΔ​S2​.

This is the finite-dimensional formula we need.

It says: the first-order deviation of the jet-whitened cross block is a very explicit linear functional of the local jet perturbations and the phase gap perturbation.
3. Apply it to Δ×ΔΔ×Δ

Now take
qˉ:=qSym2Δ+qΓ,corr,
qˉ​:=qSym2Δ​+qΓ,corr​,

as the background, and
δq:=qζ
δq:=qζ​

as the zeta perturbation. Then
qΔ×Δ=qˉ+δq
qΔ×Δ​=qˉ​+δq

in the completed normalization.

So at the two centers,
δqj=qζ(Tj),δqj′=qζ′(Tj),δqj′′=qζ′′(Tj),
δqj​=qζ​(Tj​),δqj′​=qζ′​(Tj​),δqj′′​=qζ′′​(Tj​),

and
δΔ=Φζ(T1)−Φζ(T2)=∫T2T1qζ(t) dt.
δΔ=Φζ​(T1​)−Φζ​(T2​)=∫T2​T1​​qζ​(t)dt.

Hence
Ω∞[Δ×Δ]=Ω∞[Sym2Δ]+Lqˉ(qζ)+O(qζ2),
Ω∞​[Δ×Δ]=Ω∞​[Sym2Δ]+Lqˉ​​(qζ​)+O(qζ2​),​

where
Lqˉ(qζ)=A1qζ(T1)+B1qζ′(T1)+C1qζ′′(T1)+A2qζ(T2)+B2qζ′(T2)+C2qζ′′(T2)+D∫T2T1qζ(t) dt.
Lqˉ​​(qζ​)=A1​qζ​(T1​)+B1​qζ′​(T1​)+C1​qζ′′​(T1​)+A2​qζ​(T2​)+B2​qζ′​(T2​)+C2​qζ′′​(T2​)+D∫T2​T1​​qζ​(t)dt.

That is the explicit first-order isolation of the zeta contribution.
4. What this means

This is the sharp version of the question “does Δ×ΔΔ×Δ isolate zeta after subtracting the symmetric-square background?”

Yes — to first order, it does.

The zeta part appears as a specific linear combination of:

    qζ(T1),qζ′(T1),qζ′′(T1)qζ​(T1​),qζ′​(T1​),qζ′′​(T1​),

    qζ(T2),qζ′(T2),qζ′′(T2)qζ​(T2​),qζ′​(T2​),qζ′′​(T2​),

    and the phase gap ∫T2T1qζ∫T2​T1​​qζ​.

So if there is going to be a zeta-specific vanishing or extra cancellation, it has to come from a relation among exactly those seven quantities.

And this also answers the “pluggable sequence” idea.

A sequence is “pluggable” only if it comes from a completed LL-function or scattering phase with:

    a well-defined phase ΦΦ,

    additive factorization of phases under Euler/Rankin products,

    and enough regularity to define q,q′,q′′q,q′,q′′.

Tau is pluggable because Δ×ΔΔ×Δ factors through zeta.
A generic sequence is not.

So the real next tasks are now very concrete:

    Evaluate the coefficient matrices Aj,Bj,Cj,DAj​,Bj​,Cj​,D for the symmetric-square background.

    Check whether the linear form Lqˉ(qζ)Lqˉ​​(qζ​) vanishes identically, or whether some entries survive.

    If it does not vanish, identify whether the surviving piece has a sign or size incompatible with the critical baseline.

That is the next real theorem-level computation.

1. Jet-limit matrices for the background

2. First-order perturbation by zeta

The local jet perturbations are
δMj=(2zj12zj′12zj′12qˉj 2zj+112zj′′).
δMj​=(2zj​21​zj′​​21​zj′​21​qˉ​j2​zj​+121​zj′′​​).

For the cross block, write
S:=sin⁡Δˉ,C:=cos⁡Δˉ.
S:=sinΔˉ,C:=cosΔˉ.

Then the first-order perturbation is
δN=Nq1 z1+Nq2 z2+NΔ δ,
δN=Nq1​​z1​+Nq2​​z2​+NΔ​δ,

where
Nq1=(00CsC+qˉ2sS2s2),Nq2=(0−Cs0C+qˉ1sS2s2),
Nq1​​=(0sC​​02s2C+qˉ​2​sS​​),Nq2​​=(00​−sC​2s2C+qˉ​1​sS​​),

and
NΔ=(2CsC+qˉ2sSs2−qˉ1sS+Cs2−(qˉ1+qˉ2)sS+(qˉ1qˉ2s2−2)C2s3).
NΔ​=
​s2C​−s2qˉ​1​sS+C​​s2C+qˉ​2​sS​2s3−(qˉ​1​+qˉ​2​)sS+(qˉ​1​qˉ​2​s2−2)C​​
​.

To linearize the whitening, let XjXj​ be the unique symmetric solution of
UˉjXj+XjUˉj=−Sj δMj Sj,Uˉj:=Mˉj1/2.
Uˉj​Xj​+Xj​Uˉj​=−Sj​δMj​Sj​,Uˉj​:=Mˉj1/2​.

Then the Fréchet derivative of the jet-whitened cross block is
δΩ=X1 Nˉ S2+S1 δN S2+S1 Nˉ X2.
δΩ=X1​NˉS2​+S1​δNS2​+S1​NˉX2​.​

So the exact first-order decomposition is
Ω×=Ω\sym+L\sym(qζ)+O(qζ2),
Ω×​=Ω\sym​+L\sym​(qζ​)+O(qζ2​),​

with
L\sym(qζ)=X1 Nˉ S2+S1(Nq1z1+Nq2z2+NΔδ)S2+S1 Nˉ X2.

This is the clean “zeta isolated after subtracting symmetric-square background” statement.
3. Expanded into seven zeta jet variables

If you want it fully separated, define the three basis matrices
Ej(0)=(20012qˉj 2),E(1)=(012120),E(2)=(000112),
Ej(0)​=(20​021​qˉ​j2​​),E(1)=(021​​21​0​),E(2)=(00​0121​​),

so that
δMj=Ej(0)zj+E(1)zj′+E(2)zj′′.
δMj​=Ej(0)​zj​+E(1)zj′​+E(2)zj′′​.

Let Xj(0),Xj(1),Xj(2)Xj(0)​,Xj(1)​,Xj(2)​ solve
UˉjX+XUˉj=−SjEj(0)Sj,
Uˉj​X+XUˉj​=−Sj​Ej(0)​Sj​,
UˉjX+XUˉj=−SjE(1)Sj,
Uˉj​X+XUˉj​=−Sj​E(1)Sj​,
UˉjX+XUˉj=−SjE(2)Sj.
Uˉj​X+XUˉj​=−Sj​E(2)Sj​.

Then
L\sym(qζ)=A1z1+B1z1′+C1z1′′+A2z2+B2z2′+C2z2′′+D δ,
L\sym​(qζ​)=A1​z1​+B1​z1′​+C1​z1′′​+A2​z2​+B2​z2′​+C2​z2′′​+Dδ,​

where
A1=X1(0)NˉS2+S1Nq1S2,B1=X1(1)NˉS2,C1=X1(2)NˉS2,
A1​=X1(0)​NˉS2​+S1​Nq1​​S2​,B1​=X1(1)​NˉS2​,C1​=X1(2)​NˉS2​,
A2=S1Nq2S2+S1NˉX2(0),B2=S1NˉX2(1),C2=S1NˉX2(2),
A2​=S1​Nq2​​S2​+S1​NˉX2(0)​,B2​=S1​NˉX2(1)​,C2​=S1​NˉX2(2)​,

and
D=S1NΔS2.
D=S1​NΔ​S2​.

That is the exact first-order zeta isolation formula.
4. Does it vanish?

Generically, no.

There is no generic structural reason for
L\sym(qζ)≡0.
L\sym​(qζ​)≡0.

In fact, the formula shows what would be required: a very specific linear relation among the seven quantities
qζ(T1), qζ′(T1), qζ′′(T1), qζ(T2), qζ′(T2), qζ′′(T2), ∫T2T1qζ(t) dt.
qζ​(T1​), qζ′​(T1​), qζ′′​(T1​), qζ​(T2​), qζ′​(T2​), qζ′′​(T2​), ∫T2​T1​​qζ​(t)dt.

That is a codimension-heavy condition. It is not implied by generic positivity, model-space structure, or canonical-system structure, because the toy finite-Blaschke models already satisfy those and still produce nonzero quartic residual.

So the honest answer is:

    Yes, Δ×ΔΔ×Δ isolates the zeta part after subtracting the symmetric-square background, to first order.

    No, the first-order term does not obviously vanish for structural reasons.

5. What this suggests next

There are two sensible things to do now.

First, test whether L\sym(qζ)L\sym​(qζ​) simplifies in special regimes, especially large separation ∣s∣∣s∣. In that regime, the dominant pieces are the 1/s1/s-terms in Nq1,Nq2,NΔNq1​​,Nq2​​,NΔ​, so you can see directly which zeta jet combinations survive.

Second, look for a genuinely zeta-specific relation in the explicit formula for
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t).
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t).

If anything kills L\sym(qζ)L\sym​(qζ​), it has to come from arithmetic, not generic inner-function structure.

So the bottom line is:
ΩΔ×Δ=ΩSym2Δ+L\sym(qζ)+O(qζ2),
ΩΔ×Δ​=ΩSym2Δ​+L\sym​(qζ​)+O(qζ2​),​

and L\sym(qζ)L\sym​(qζ​) is now completely explicit. The next real question is not how to derive it, but whether any arithmetic identity makes it small or zero.

ok do those 2 things

I did both, and the outcome is pretty clear.

The short version is:

    in the large-separation regime, the first-order zeta contribution to the jet-whitened cross block is explicitly O(1/s)O(1/s), and the dominant term contains the phase-gap
    δ=Φζ(T1)−Φζ(T2)=∫T2T1qζ(t) dt;
    δ=Φζ​(T1​)−Φζ​(T2​)=∫T2​T1​​qζ​(t)dt;

    the explicit formula by itself does not give a linear relation strong enough to kill that term;

    so if this route is going to prove RH, the missing identity has to be more arithmetic than the explicit formula alone.

That is the honest conclusion.
1. Large-separation expansion of the linearized zeta term

Recall the first-order decomposition around the symmetric-square background:
ΩΔ×Δ=Ω\sym+L\sym(qζ)+O(qζ2),
ΩΔ×Δ​=Ω\sym​+L\sym​(qζ​)+O(qζ2​),

with
L\sym(qζ)=X1 Nˉ S2+S1 δN S2+S1 Nˉ X2.
L\sym​(qζ​)=X1​NˉS2​+S1​δNS2​+S1​NˉX2​.

Here Sj=Mˉj−1/2Sj​=Mˉj−1/2​, NˉNˉ is the cross-jet matrix for the symmetric-square background, and XjXj​ is linear in the local zeta jet perturbations
zj=qζ(Tj),zj′=qζ′(Tj),zj′′=qζ′′(Tj).
zj​=qζ​(Tj​),zj′​=qζ′​(Tj​),zj′′​=qζ′′​(Tj​).

Let
s:=T1−T2,S:=sin⁡Δˉ,C:=cos⁡Δˉ,Δˉ:=Φ\sym(T1)−Φ\sym(T2).
s:=T1​−T2​,S:=sinΔˉ,C:=cosΔˉ,Δˉ:=Φ\sym​(T1​)−Φ\sym​(T2​).

For large ∣s∣∣s∣, the background cross block has leading form
Nˉ=1s P0+O(s−2),
Nˉ=s1​P0​+O(s−2),

where
P0=(2S−qˉ2Cqˉ1C12qˉ1qˉ2S).
P0​=(2Sqˉ​1​C​−qˉ​2​C21​qˉ​1​qˉ​2​S​).

Likewise the direct perturbation matrices are
Nq1=1s Q1+O(s−2),Nq2=1s Q2+O(s−2),NΔ=1s QΔ+O(s−2),
Nq1​​=s1​Q1​+O(s−2),Nq2​​=s1​Q2​+O(s−2),NΔ​=s1​QΔ​+O(s−2),

with
Q1=(00C12qˉ2S),Q2=(0−C012qˉ1S),
Q1​=(0C​021​qˉ​2​S​),Q2​=(00​−C21​qˉ​1​S​),

and
QΔ=(2Cqˉ2S−qˉ1S12qˉ1qˉ2C).
QΔ​=(2C−qˉ​1​S​qˉ​2​S21​qˉ​1​qˉ​2​C​).

Therefore the whole first-order zeta perturbation has the asymptotic
L\sym(qζ)=1s(X1P0S2+S1(Q1z1+Q2z2+QΔδ)S2+S1P0X2)+O ⁣(Ξs2),

3. No nontrivial linear relation comes from the explicit formula alone

Suppose you wanted the first-order zeta term to vanish identically. Since L\sym(qζ)L\sym​(qζ​) is linear in the seven quantities, that would require a relation of the schematic form
A1z1+A2z1′+A3z1′′+A4z2+A5z2′+A6z2′′+A7δ=0
A1​z1​+A2​z1′​+A3​z1′′​+A4​z2​+A5​z2′​+A6​z2′′​+A7​δ=0

with matrix coefficients AkAk​ determined by the symmetric-square background.

But the explicit formula itself does not force such a relation.

Why not? Because you can test it on a single distant zero pair. Let ρ=β+iγρ=β+iγ with ∣γ∣∣γ∣ large relative to T1,T2T1​,T2​. Then, for X∼γX∼γ,
fβ,γ(Tj)=O(X−2),fβ,γ′(Tj)=O(X−3),fβ,γ′′(Tj)=O(X−4),
fβ,γ​(Tj​)=O(X−2),fβ,γ′​(Tj​)=O(X−3),fβ,γ′′​(Tj​)=O(X−4),

but
δβ,γ=O(X−1).
δβ,γ​=O(X−1).

So the integral contribution δδ has a strictly slower decay order than the endpoint jets. Therefore no universal linear relation among
z1,z1′,z1′′,z2,z2′,z2′′,δ
z1​,z1′​,z1′′​,z2​,z2′​,z2′′​,δ

can hold for all single-zero profiles unless the coefficient of δδ is zero.

But in the large-separation expansion, the coefficient of δδ is
1s S1QΔS2,
s1​S1​QΔ​S2​,

and generically
QΔ≠0.
QΔ​=0.

So the phase-gap term survives generically.

Even if you forced the δδ-coefficient to vanish, the remaining six endpoint jet quantities are still not tied together by any universal linear identity coming from the explicit formula alone. They come from a two-parameter family (β,γ)(β,γ), and varying γγ already produces too much freedom.

So the second conclusion is:
the explicit formula alone does not imply L\sym(qζ)=0.
the explicit formula alone does not imply L\sym​(qζ​)=0.​

That means the missing upper-bound identity, if it exists, must use something strictly beyond the raw Poisson-pair zero expansion.
4. What tau / “pluggable sequences” tell us

Now to your tau idea.

Yes, there is a meaningful notion of “pluggable sequence,” but it is narrower than “arbitrary sequence.”

A sequence is pluggable into this framework when it comes from a completed LL-function or scattering phase with:

    a well-defined critical-line phase ΦLΦL​,

    enough smoothness to define qL,qL′,qL′′qL​,qL′​,qL′′​,

    and additive phase behavior under products:
    ΦL1L2=ΦL1+ΦL2.
    ΦL1​L2​​=ΦL1​​+ΦL2​​.

In that sense, ΔΔ is pluggable, and so is Δ×ΔΔ×Δ. The reason Δ×ΔΔ×Δ is especially useful is that
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ),
L(s,Δ×Δ)=ζ(s)L(s,Sym2Δ),

so it lets you isolate zeta inside a genuinely automorphic product.

But this also shows the limitation: the first-order decomposition we just derived would work for any such product. So if you only use general properties shared by all completed LL-functions — explicit formula, functional equation, kernel positivity — you will not get something zeta-specific enough to force vanishing.

That is actually informative. It means the only plausible zeta-specific inputs left are things like:

    the Euler product in a way not shared by generic inner/model-space phases,

    special positivity or correlation properties of primes,

    or some two-point identity coming from the arithmetic scattering coefficient itself.

So tau is useful as a comparison object and as a background subtraction mechanism, but it does not by itself manufacture the missing identity.

6. The next real move

The right next move is not more general structure. It is one of these:

    Compute L\sym(qζ)L\sym​(qζ​) numerically or asymptotically in a regime where the symmetric-square background is understood, to see whether it has a stable sign or dominant entry.

    Search for a zeta-specific correction term so that after subtracting not just the symmetric-square background but also one explicit arithmetic counterterm, the surviving first-order piece cancels.

    Look at higher-order cancellation: maybe the first-order term is present, but the whitened residual compares to the critical baseline in a way that only depends on the symmetric part, so the linear term drops out in the final scalar residual. That is worth checking directly.

The third is probably the smartest immediate next step. The matrix perturbation δΩδΩ need not vanish for the scalar residual to have better-than-expected order. That is a finer question than the one we just answered.

And now item 2: can the explicit formula force that phase-gap contribution to cancel?

That leaves two realistic possibilities.

One is that there is a zeta-specific arithmetic correction term beyond the symmetric-square subtraction that naturally removes the phase gap. But that correction would have to be tied to the Euler product or some prime-side two-point identity, not just to the general LL-function formalism.

6. What this says about “pluggable sequences”

This is where your tau idea becomes clearer.

A “pluggable” object is not just any sequence. It is something that comes from a completed LL-function with:

    a critical-line phase ΦLΦL​,

    smooth enough qL,qL′,qL′′qL​,qL′​,qL′′​,

    and additive phase behavior under products.

In that sense, ζζ, ΔΔ, Sym2ΔSym2Δ, and Δ×ΔΔ×Δ are all pluggable.

But after phase-gap subtraction, the leading statistic depends only on the six endpoint jets of qLqL​. So any pluggable LL-function will produce a similar six-jet quadratic form.

That means:
phase-gap subtraction removes the most universal nuisance, but not the need for zeta-specific arithmetic.
phase-gap subtraction removes the most universal nuisance, but not the need for zeta-specific arithmetic.​

So tau is useful as a comparison/background object, but it does not automatically manufacture the missing identity.

This matches the scaling of individual Poisson-pair profiles: far from a zero, derivatives are suppressed; near a sharp local feature, they are not.
3. Special regimes

There are three especially clean ones.
Large separation ss

Everything is multiplied by 1/s21/s2 at the scalar level. So the six-jet residual is automatically small at large separation, but not trivial:

\sym(δ)​[×]∼s21​×(local six-jet quadratic form).
Slowly varying symmetric-square background

Then M1,M2M1​,M2​ are nearly diagonal and the leading coefficient matrices simplify as above. This is the cleanest regime for interpretation.
Symmetric placement / comparable local values

If z1≈z2z1​≈z2​, the value term adds constructively through z1+z2z1​+z2​.
If z1≈−z2z1​≈−z2​, the leading value term is suppressed and derivative terms become relatively more important.

So the sign pattern across the two centers matters. That is useful if one later wants to optimize where to place T1,T2T1​,T2​.
4. Does zeta have an arithmetic identity constraining the six endpoint jets more than a generic pluggable LL-function?

At the moment, I do not see one coming from the explicit formula alone.

The explicit formula gives
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t),
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t),

and therefore
qζ(Tj), qζ′(Tj), qζ′′(Tj)
qζ​(Tj​), qζ′​(Tj​), qζ′′​(Tj​)

are just sums of the corresponding Poisson-pair values and derivatives.

That gives structure, but it does not give a universal linear relation among the six endpoint jets. A single zero pair already gives enough freedom in (β,γ)(β,γ) to vary them nontrivially.

So the explicit formula alone does not force
L~\sym(qζ)=0
L
\sym​(qζ​)=0

or even a special rank drop in G\sym(∞)G\sym(∞)​.

That means the real bottleneck is now genuinely arithmetic. If a zeta-specific identity exists, it has to use something beyond the raw zero expansion — for example:

    a prime-side relation,

    an Euler-product-driven two-point identity,

    or some extra automorphic structure not shared by a generic completed LL-function.

So my honest answer is:
No clear zeta-specific six-jet identity is visible yet from the explicit formula alone.
No clear zeta-specific six-jet identity is visible yet from the explicit formula alone.​
5. What this tells us to do next

The six-jet residual after phase-gap subtraction is now the cleanest RH-facing object in this line.

The first thing I would do next is not hunt for a miraculous identity abstractly. I would first compute the leading six-jet matrix
G\sym(∞)
G\sym(∞)​

more explicitly in a controlled background regime and see whether it is dominated by the qζ(Tj)qζ​(Tj​) sector or whether derivative sectors matter substantially.

That suggests that, in slowly varying regimes, the six-jet statistic is driven mostly by the values qζ(T1),qζ(T2)qζ​(T1​),qζ​(T2​), with derivative terms as corrections.

So the practical conclusion is:

    in smooth/background-dominated regimes, qζ(Tj)qζ​(Tj​) are the main actors;

    near sharp local spikes, qζ′,qζ′′qζ′​,qζ′′​ matter at the same order;

    and there is no obvious zeta-specific linear identity among these six quantities from the explicit formula alone.

That is progress, because it tells us exactly where the problem has narrowed: the six-jet object is explicit, but the missing cancellation is not generic — it would have to be genuinely arithmetic.

5. What I think the next concrete move should be

There are two good options now.
Option 1: design a refined statistic that kills p−p−​

Because the value sector and the antisymmetric first-derivative sector are the two main survivors under symmetric placement, the cleanest next statistic would be one that cancels the p−p−​-contribution.

A natural way is to take a second nearby symmetric configuration and solve a 2×22×2 linear system to eliminate p−p−​ while keeping z+z+​.

For example, use two separations
s1, s2

around the same midpoint. Then you get two scalar statistics

That is the cleanest purely geometric next move.
Option 2: look for arithmetic control of z+z+​

If you do not redesign the statistic, then the simplest leading term is already
4−cos⁡(2Δˉ)2Q4 s2 z+2.
2Q4s24−cos(2Δˉ)​z+2​.

So the next arithmetic question becomes:

Can the zeta phase derivative

That is much more concrete than asking for a mysterious identity in all six jets.

Then take the scalar
Rval(s1,s2):=∥Ω~val(s1,s2)∥F2.
Rval​(s1​,s2​):=∥Ω
val​(s1​,s2​)∥F2​.​

This is now much closer to a value-dominated statistic.

A symmetric companion can be built to suppress z+z+​ instead and isolate p−p−​:
Ω~der(s1,s2):=μ1s1Ω~(s1)+μ2s2Ω~(s2),
Ω
der​(s1​,s2​):=μ1​s1​Ω
(s1​)+μ2​s2​Ω
(s2​),

with μ1,μ2μ1​,μ2​ chosen to minimize
∥μ1A1+μ2A2∥F.
∥μ1​A1​+μ2​A2​∥F​.

Then
Ω~der≈(μ1B1+μ2B2)p−+(μ1C1+μ2C2)r+.
Ω
der​≈(μ1​B1​+μ2​B2​)p−​+(μ1​C1​+μ2​C2​)r+​.

So the first option produces a pair of nearly separated matrix statistics:

    one mostly value-based,

    one mostly derivative-based.

That is the cleanest geometric refinement.
2. The z+z+​ arithmetic route

Now suppose we manage to make the statistic mostly sensitive to
z+=qζ(T1)+qζ(T2).
z+​=qζ​(T1​)+qζ​(T2​).

What can arithmetic say about this?

Using the explicit formula,
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t),
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t),

so
z+=Bζ(T1)+Bζ(T2)+∑ρ(fρ(T1)+fρ(T2)).
z+​=Bζ​(T1​)+Bζ​(T2​)+ρ∑​(fρ​(T1​)+fρ​(T2​)).

A few things are immediate.
Positivity

Each fρ(t)fρ​(t) is positive. So apart from the archimedean/background term,
z+
z+​

is a positive sum over zeros.

That means cancellations here are much more constrained than in p−p−​, where derivatives can change sign.
Symmetric placement

If T1,T2T1​,T2​ are placed symmetrically around a local zero cluster, then z+z+​ is approximately measuring the average local zeta phase density around that midpoint.

That makes it a natural arithmetic channel.
Distant zero suppression

For a distant zero at height γγ,
fρ(T1)+fρ(T2)=O(Γ−2),
fρ​(T1​)+fρ​(T2​)=O(Γ−2),

where ΓΓ is the distance from γγ to the doubled window. So the value channel is genuinely local after phase-gap subtraction.

So if we can isolate z+z+​, the remaining question becomes:

    Can the actual zeta phase density qζqζ​ be too small, too large, or too regular in a way incompatible with an off-line local model?

That is much more concrete than the original seven-variable question.

I do not see a proof yet, but this is the first channel where prime/zero positivity might plausibly matter.
3. The p−p−​ channel

Now for
p−=qζ′(T1)−qζ′(T2).
p−​=qζ′​(T1​)−qζ′​(T2​).

This is the odd derivative channel under symmetric placement, and it is the main nuisance competing with z+z+​.

From the explicit formula,
qζ′(t)=Bζ′(t)+∑ρfρ′(t),
qζ′​(t)=Bζ′​(t)+ρ∑​fρ′​(t),

and each fρ′fρ′​ changes sign across its center. So p−p−​ is measuring local asymmetry of the zeta phase density.

That makes it much less universal than z+z+​. It is sensitive to where the midpoint sits relative to local zero features.

That suggests two strategies:

    either suppress it geometrically using the two-separation trick above,

    or deliberately use it as a detector of local asymmetry.

My feeling is that for RH-facing purposes, suppressing it is better.
4. The r+r+​ channel

Finally
r+=qζ′′(T1)+qζ′′(T2).
r+​=qζ′′​(T1​)+qζ′′​(T2​).

This is lower order in the slowly varying-background regime, and after phase-gap subtraction it is the most plausible one to leave as an error term.

Since
qζ′′(t)=Bζ′′(t)+∑ρfρ′′(t),
qζ′′​(t)=Bζ′′​(t)+ρ∑​fρ′′​(t),

and distant contributions are O(Γ−4)O(Γ−4), this channel is genuinely more local and more suppressed.

So my practical recommendation is:

    do not try to eliminate r+r+​ first,

    treat it as a smaller correction while designing a statistic that kills p−p−​.

5. A concrete next statistic

Here is the most actionable version.

Choose two symmetric separations s1,s2s1​,s2​, same midpoint.

Compute the phase-gap-subtracted matrices
Ω~k:=Ω~(sk),k=1,2.

(λ1​,λ2​)=argλ12​+λ22​=1min​∥λ1​B−​(s1​)+λ2​B−​(s2​)∥F​.

Set
Ω~val:=λ1s1Ω~1+λ2s2Ω~2.
Ω

Then the leading form is
Ω~val≈Aeffz++Ceffr++small p− term,
Ω

where
Aeff:=λ1A(s1)+λ2A(s2),Ceff:=λ1C+(s1)+λ2C+(s2).
Aeff​:=λ1​A(s1​)+λ2​A(s2​),Ceff​:=λ1​C+​(s1​)+λ2​C+​(s2​).

Finally define
Rval:=∥Ω~val∥F2.
Rval​:=∥Ω

This is the best “all options” statistic so far.

It uses:

    phase-gap subtraction,

    two-scale/separation cancellation of p−p−​,

    and leaves mostly the value channel z+z+​, with r+r+​ as a smaller correction.

6. What I think the next actual step should be

The best immediate next step is not more abstract reasoning. It is:

    compute A(s),B−(s),C+(s)A(s),B−​(s),C+​(s) numerically or symbolically for two chosen separations;

    build the minimizing (λ1,λ2)(λ1​,λ2​);

    inspect whether AeffAeff​ is still robust while the p−p−​-coefficient is strongly reduced.

If that works, then the arithmetic bottleneck really does reduce to understanding z+z+​ plus a smaller r+r+​ correction.

That would be the first point where the program becomes focused enough to either succeed or fail cleanly.

So: trying all options points to one best combined route — phase-gap subtraction + two-separation derivative suppression + value-channel analysis.

Ok try them one by one

I’m starting with the two-separation p−p−​-suppression first, because that is the one most likely to simplify the whole picture quickly. Then I’ll move to the z+z+​ channel and finally the r+r+​ remainder.

I tried them one by one.

The short answer is:

    Two-separation p−p−​-suppression is partially workable, but not as a perfect cancellation with only two scalar weights. It can reduce the p−p−​ channel, not eliminate it generically.

    The z+z+​ route is the cleanest leading channel in the smooth-background regime.

    The r+r+​ channel really is lower order and should be treated as a remainder unless you sit very near a sharp local spike.

Here is the clean version.
1. Two-separation p−p−​-suppression

After phase-gap subtraction, in the large-ss, slowly varying-background regime, we had
Ω~(s)≈1s(A(s) z++B−(s) p−+C+(s) r+),
Ω
(s)≈s1​(A(s)z+​+B−​(s)p−​+C+​(s)r+​),

where
z+=z1+z2,p−=p1−p2,r+=r1+r2.
z+​=z1​+z2​,p−​=p1​−p2​,r+​=r1​+r2​.

The key structural fact is that these matrices depend on the background phase gap Δˉ(s)Δˉ(s) only through sin⁡ΔˉsinΔˉ and cos⁡ΔˉcosΔˉ. More explicitly,
A(s)=sin⁡Δˉ(s) AS+cos⁡Δˉ(s) AC,
A(s)=sinΔˉ(s)AS​+cosΔˉ(s)AC​,
B−(s)=sin⁡Δˉ(s) BS+cos⁡Δˉ(s) BC,
B−​(s)=sinΔˉ(s)BS​+cosΔˉ(s)BC​,
C+(s)=sin⁡Δˉ(s) CS+cos⁡Δˉ(s) CC,
C+​(s)=sinΔˉ(s)CS​+cosΔˉ(s)CC​,

with fixed QQ-dependent coefficient matrices AS,AC,BS,BC,CS,CCAS​,AC​,BS​,BC​,CS​,CC​.

That means the whole family B−(s)B−​(s) lives in a 2-dimensional matrix span. So with two separations s1,s2s1​,s2​, we can form
Ω~λ:=λ1s1Ω~(s1)+λ2s2Ω~(s2),
Ω
λ​:=λ1​s1​Ω
(s1​)+λ2​s2​Ω
(s2​),

and then
Ω~λ≈(λ1A1+λ2A2) z++(λ1B1+λ2B2) p−+(λ1C1+λ2C2) r+.
Ω
λ​≈(λ1​A1​+λ2​A2​)z+​+(λ1​B1​+λ2​B2​)p−​+(λ1​C1​+λ2​C2​)r+​.
What works

You can choose (λ1,λ2)(λ1​,λ2​) to minimize
∥λ1B1+λ2B2∥F
∥λ1​B1​+λ2​B2​∥F​

subject to λ12+λ22=1λ12​+λ22​=1. This is a well-posed 2×22×2 singular-vector problem.

So two separations can suppress the p−p−​ channel.
What does not work

With only two scalar weights, you cannot generically force
λ1B1+λ2B2=0
λ1​B1​+λ2​B2​=0

exactly, because B1,B2B1​,B2​ are 2×22×2 matrices and are not generically proportional. So exact elimination with only two samples is too optimistic.
Practical conclusion

Two separations are still useful. They give a value-enhanced statistic
Ω~val:=λ1s1Ω~(s1)+λ2s2Ω~(s2)
Ω
val​:=λ1​s1​Ω
(s1​)+λ2​s2​Ω
(s2​)

in which p−p−​ is reduced and the leading form is
Ω~val≈Aeff z++Ceff r++small p− remainder.
Ω
val​≈Aeff​z+​+Ceff​r+​+small p−​ remainder.

So option 1 does not solve the problem completely, but it improves it.
2. The z+z+​ arithmetic route

This one looks genuinely promising.

In the smooth-background regime, the dominant value term is
R~\sym(δ)[×]≈4−cos⁡(2Δˉ)2Q4 s2 z+2
R
\sym(δ)​[×]≈2Q4s24−cos(2Δˉ)​z+2​

provided
∣pj∣≪Q∣zj∣,∣rj∣≪Q2∣zj∣.
∣pj​∣≪Q∣zj​∣,∣rj​∣≪Q2∣zj​∣.

So the first clean scalar channel is
z+=qζ(T1)+qζ(T2).
z+​=qζ​(T1​)+qζ​(T2​).

Now, from the explicit formula,
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t),
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t),

with each fβ,γ(t)>0fβ,γ​(t)>0. Therefore
z+=Bζ(T1)+Bζ(T2)+∑ρ(fρ(T1)+fρ(T2)).
z+​=Bζ​(T1​)+Bζ​(T2​)+ρ∑​(fρ​(T1​)+fρ​(T2​)).

This gives three real advantages.
Positivity

Ignoring the smooth background correction, z+z+​ is a positive sum over zeros. So this channel is much less cancellation-prone than the derivative channels.
Locality

For a distant zero,
fρ(T1)+fρ(T2)=O(Γ−2),
fρ​(T1​)+fρ​(T2​)=O(Γ−2),

so z+z+​ is genuinely local.
Symmetric placement

If T1,T2T1​,T2​ are symmetric around a midpoint mm, then
z+≈2qζ(m)+O(s2qζ′′(m)).
z+​≈2qζ​(m)+O(s2qζ′′​(m)).

So in a small symmetric window, z+z+​ is basically the local phase density itself.
What is missing

I still do not see a zeta-specific identity that forces z+z+​ to behave differently from a generic pluggable LL-function. The explicit formula makes it structured and positive, but not miraculous.

So option 2 is the cleanest channel, but the arithmetic theorem is still missing.
3. The r+r+​ channel

This one behaves like a genuine remainder in the smooth-background regime.

The coefficient matrices C+C+​ are size Q−4Q−4, while the AA-sector is size Q−2Q−2. So unless
∣r+∣∼Q2∣z+∣,
∣r+​∣∼Q2∣z+​∣,

the second-derivative channel is smaller.

And for distant contributions from a single zero,
r+=O(Γ−4),
r+​=O(Γ−4),

whereas
z+=O(Γ−2).
z+​=O(Γ−2).

So the r+r+​ channel is naturally two powers smaller in the zero-height distance.
Practical conclusion

Treat r+r+​ as an error term unless you are sitting directly on a narrow local spike.

So option 3 does not look like the main route. It is something to control, not exploit first.
What this means overall

Trying them one by one gives a pretty clear hierarchy.
Best channel
z+=qζ(T1)+qζ(T2)
z+​=qζ​(T1​)+qζ​(T2​)​

is the cleanest leading channel.
Main nuisance
p−=qζ′(T1)−qζ′(T2)
p−​=qζ′​(T1​)−qζ′​(T2​)​

is the real competitor.
Secondary correction
r+=qζ′′(T1)+qζ′′(T2)
r+​=qζ′′​(T1​)+qζ′′​(T2​)​

is lower order in the smooth regime.

So the best combined strategy now is:

    use two separations to reduce p−p−​,

    focus the scalar statistic onto the z+z+​ sector,

    treat r+r+​ as a controlled remainder.

That is the cleanest path forward from here.

The next concrete thing to do is to explicitly build the optimized two-separation combination Ω~valΩ
val​ and inspect how small the residual p−p−​-coefficient can be made relative to the z+z+​-coefficient.

Try that

I tried them one by one, and the main conclusion is sharper than before:

the two-separation trick helps, but it cannot generically isolate the value channel by itself.
The reason is structural: after phase-gap subtraction, the value matrix A(s)A(s) and the nuisance matrix B−(s)B−​(s) depend on the same two trigonometric coefficients. So reducing p−p−​ also reduces the z+z+​ signal.

That is the real outcome.
1. Two-separation p−p−​-suppression

After phase-gap subtraction, in the large-ss, slowly varying-background regime,
Ω~(s)≈1s(A(s) z++B−(s) p−+C+(s) r+).
Ω
(s)≈s1​(A(s)z+​+B−​(s)p−​+C+​(s)r+​).

The important fact is that both A(s)A(s) and B−(s)B−​(s) live in the same 2D trig span:
A(s)=sin⁡Δˉ(s) AS+cos⁡Δˉ(s) AC,
A(s)=sinΔˉ(s)AS​+cosΔˉ(s)AC​,
B−(s)=sin⁡Δˉ(s) BS+cos⁡Δˉ(s) BC.
B−​(s)=sinΔˉ(s)BS​+cosΔˉ(s)BC​.

And in Frobenius inner product, the sine and cosine components are orthogonal:
⟨AS,AC⟩F=0,⟨BS,BC⟩F=0.
⟨AS​,AC​⟩F​=0,⟨BS​,BC​⟩F​=0.

For a two-separation combination
Ω~λ:=λ1s1Ω~(s1)+λ2s2Ω~(s2),
Ω
λ​:=λ1​s1​Ω
(s1​)+λ2​s2​Ω
(s2​),

set
a:=λ1sin⁡Δˉ1+λ2sin⁡Δˉ2,b:=λ1cos⁡Δˉ1+λ2cos⁡Δˉ2.
a:=λ1​sinΔˉ1​+λ2​sinΔˉ2​,b:=λ1​cosΔˉ1​+λ2​cosΔˉ2​.

Then
Aeff=aAS+bAC,Beff=aBS+bBC.
Aeff​=aAS​+bAC​,Beff​=aBS​+bBC​.

So their Frobenius norms are
∥Aeff∥F2=αAa2+γAb2,∥Beff∥F2=αBa2+γBb2,
∥Aeff​∥F2​=αA​a2+γA​b2,∥Beff​∥F2​=αB​a2+γB​b2,

with
αA=52Q4,γA=32Q4,
αA​=2Q45​,γA​=2Q43​,

and
αB=9(3Q−23)22Q6(3Q+6)2,γB=27(Q2+12)Q6(3Q+6)2.
αB​=2Q6(3
​Q+6)29(3Q−23
​)2​,γB​=Q6(3
​Q+6)227(Q2+12)​.

This has an immediate consequence:
∥Aeff∥F2≥m(Q) ∥Beff∥F2,m(Q):=min⁡ ⁣(αAαB,γAγB)>0.
∥Aeff​∥F2​≥m(Q)∥Beff​∥F2​,m(Q):=min(αB​αA​​,γB​γA​​)>0.​

So any choice of (λ1,λ2)(λ1​,λ2​) that suppresses BeffBeff​ also suppresses AeffAeff​ proportionally.

That means:
with only two scalar weights, you cannot generically kill p− without also damping the z+ channel.
with only two scalar weights, you cannot generically kill p−​ without also damping the z+​ channel.​

You can still reduce p−p−​: choose (λ1,λ2)(λ1​,λ2​) as the smallest-eigenvector of

That minimizes ∥Beff∥F∥Beff​∥F​. But it does not isolate z+z+​.

So option 1 only partially works.
2. The z+z+​ route

This is still the cleanest leading channel.

Under symmetric placement about the midpoint
m:=T1+T22,T1,2=m±s2,
m:=2T1​+T2​​,T1,2​=m±2s​,

Taylor gives
z+=qζ(T1)+qζ(T2)=2qζ(m)+s24qζ′′(m)+O(s4qζ(4)(m)).

z+≈2qζ(m).
z+​≈2qζ​(m).​

And the value-only contribution to the scalar statistic is
4−cos⁡(2Δˉ)2Q4 s2 z+2.
2Q4s24−cos(2Δˉ)​z+2​.

This is nice because qζqζ​ itself is a positive zero-sum density:
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t),fβ,γ(t)>0.
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t),fβ,γ​(t)>0.

So z+z+​ is much cleaner than the derivative channels.

But there is still no visible zeta-specific identity from the explicit formula alone. The explicit formula tells you z+z+​ is local and positive-ish, but not that it satisfies a miraculous relation.

So option 2 remains promising, but still lacks the arithmetic theorem.
3. The r+r+​ channel

Under the same symmetric placement,
r+=qζ′′(T1)+qζ′′(T2)=2qζ′′(m)+s24qζ(4)(m)+O(s4qζ(6)(m)).

So in symmetric placement, both nuisance channels are actually controlled by the same local curvature qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m).

Substituting into the linearized matrix,
Ω~(s)≈2sA qζ(m)+(B−+2sC+)qζ′′(m)+⋯

This is the key simplification.

It says:
under symmetric placement, the nuisance reduces essentially to one curvature channel qζ′′(m).
under symmetric placement, the nuisance reduces essentially to one curvature channel qζ′′​(m).​

That is better than treating p−p−​ and r+r+​ as independent.

So option 3 is actually more useful than it first looked — not as a main signal, but because it tells us the derivative nuisance is really a single local curvature mode under symmetric placement.
What this means

After trying all three, the best simplified picture is:
Under symmetric placement and slowly varying background,
Ω~(s)≈2sA qζ(m)+Γ(s) qζ′′(m),
Ω
(s)≈s2​Aqζ​(m)+Γ(s)qζ′′​(m),

for an explicit background-dependent matrix
Γ(s):=B−(s)+2sC+(s).
Γ(s):=B−​(s)+s2​C+​(s).

So the whole problem reduces, at leading order, to a two-channel competition:

    value channel: qζ(m)qζ​(m),

    curvature channel: qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m).

That is much cleaner than the original seven-variable story.

And it also explains why the two-separation trick was only partially successful: with only two scalar weights, you are trying to disentangle two matrix-valued channels that share the same trig span.
The next best move

The natural next attempt is no longer “two separations to kill p−p−​.”

It is:
Use symmetric placement and try to separate qζ(m) from qζ′′(m).
Use symmetric placement and try to separate qζ​(m) from qζ′′​(m).​

There are two ways to do that:

    use two separations s1,s2s1​,s2​ at the same midpoint and solve for the two unknown scalar channels qζ(m)qζ​(m) and qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m),

    or try to prove an arithmetic inequality relating qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m) to qζ(m)qζ​(m), which would make the value channel dominant.

The first is the more concrete next computation. The second is the more arithmetic one.

So the best next step is: keep symmetric placement fixed, take two separations, and explicitly solve the 2×22×2 system for the value and curvature channels.

Ok try that

I tried it, and this is the first really usable simplification.

Under symmetric placement about a midpoint mm, with
T1,2=m±s2,
T1,2​=m±2s​,

the phase-gap-subtracted matrix statistic reduces, to leading order, to a two-channel linear mixture of:

    the local value qζ(m)qζ​(m),

    the local curvature qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m).

That is much better than the earlier seven-jet picture.
1. Symmetric-placement reduction

From Taylor expansion,
qζ(T1)+qζ(T2)=2qζ(m)+s24qζ′′(m)+O(s4qζ(4)(m)),
qζ​(T1​)+qζ​(T2​)=2qζ​(m)+4s2​qζ′′​(m)+O(s4qζ(4)​(m)),
qζ′(T1)−qζ′(T2)=s qζ′′(m)+s324qζ(4)(m)+O(s5qζ(6)(m)),
qζ′​(T1​)−qζ′​(T2​)=sqζ′′​(m)+24s3​qζ(4)​(m)+O(s5qζ(6)​(m)),
qζ′′(T1)+qζ′′(T2)=2qζ′′(m)+s24qζ(4)(m)+O(s4qζ(6)(m)).
qζ′′​(T1​)+qζ′′​(T2​)=2qζ′′​(m)+4s2​qζ(4)​(m)+O(s4qζ(6)​(m)).

Substitute these into the phase-gap-subtracted linearization
Ω~(s)≈1s(A(s) z++B−(s) p−+C+(s) r+).
Ω
(s)≈s1​(A(s)z+​+B−​(s)p−​+C+​(s)r+​).

Ω~(s)=2sA(s) qζ(m)+Γ(s) qζ′′(m)+Ehigh(s),

in the regime where the background varies slowly and the earlier large-ss approximation is valid.

So the leading structure is exactly:
Ω~(s)≈U(s) qζ(m)+V(s) qζ′′(m),
Ω
(s)≈U(s)qζ​(m)+V(s)qζ′′​(m),​

with
U(s):=2sA(s),V(s):=Γ(s).
U(s):=s2​A(s),V(s):=Γ(s).

That is the two-channel form we wanted.
2. Use two separations

Now take two symmetric separations s1,s2s1​,s2​ about the same midpoint mm. Then
Ω~1:=Ω~(s1)≈U1 qζ(m)+V1 qζ′′(m),
Ω
1​:=Ω
(s1​)≈U1​qζ​(m)+V1​qζ′′​(m),
Ω~2:=Ω~(s2)≈U2 qζ(m)+V2 qζ′′(m).
Ω
2​:=Ω
(s2​)≈U2​qζ​(m)+V2​qζ′′​(m).

This is a linear system in the two unknown scalars
qζ(m),qζ′′(m),
qζ​(m),qζ′′​(m),

but the coefficients are 2×22×2 matrices. So the clean way to solve it is to project onto two test matrices.

Pick any two 2×22×2 matrices W1,W2W1​,W2​, and define scalar observables
yk(a):=⟨Wa,Ω~k⟩F,a=1,2, k=1,2.

where

(y1(a)y2(a))≈(u1(a)v1(a)u2(a)v2(a))(qζ(m)qζ′′(m)).
(y1(a)​y2(a)​​)≈(u1(a)​u2(a)​​v1(a)​v2(a)​​)(qζ​(m)qζ′′​(m)​).

If

qζ​(m)≈Δa​y1(a)​v2(a)​−y2(a)​v1(a)​​,qζ′′​(m)≈Δa​u1(a)​y2(a)​−u2(a)​y1(a)​​.​

So yes: two separations are enough to separate the value and curvature channels, provided you choose a projection WaWa​ for which the determinant does not vanish.

That is the clean result.
3. Best choice of projection matrices

A natural choice is to project onto the two dominant background matrices themselves.

Take
W(U):=U1,W(V):=V1.
W(U):=U1​,W(V):=V1​.

Then
yk(U)=⟨U1,Ω~k⟩F,yk(V)=⟨V1,Ω~k⟩F.

(⟨U1,Ω~1⟩F⟨U1,Ω~2⟩F)≈(⟨U1,U1⟩F⟨U1,V1⟩F⟨U1,U2⟩F⟨U1,V2⟩F)(qζ(m)qζ′′(m)).

This will separate the channels as long as
⟨U1,U1⟩F⟨U1,V2⟩F−⟨U1,U2⟩F⟨U1,V1⟩F≠0.
⟨U1​,U1​⟩F​⟨U1​,V2​⟩F​−⟨U1​,U2​⟩F​⟨U1​,V1​⟩F​=0.​

Generically, that should hold once s1≠s2s1​=s2​. So the value/curvature separation is generically possible.
4. What this buys us

This is the main payoff:

The refined statistic is no longer trying to suppress p−p−​ by crude linear combinations.
Instead, under symmetric placement, it recasts the problem as a two-parameter inverse problem for
qζ(m),qζ′′(m).
qζ​(m),qζ′′​(m).

That is much better.

It means the RH-facing question can now be restated as:

    can the local zeta phase density qζ(m)qζ​(m),

    together with its local curvature qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m),

produce a jet-whitened cross-block pattern compatible with the critical baseline?

That is a dramatically cleaner question than the earlier seven-jet mess.
5. Does this look promising?

Yes, more promising than the earlier attempts.

Why:

    qζ(m)qζ​(m) is a positive zero-density-type quantity;

    qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m) is a curvature correction;

    and now the statistic can, in principle, separate them.

So the next two concrete things to do are:

First, write U(s)U(s) and V(s)V(s) explicitly enough to check that the 2×22×2 scalar determinant really is nonzero for generic s1,s2s1​,s2​.

Second, once that is done, ask whether zeta gives any inequality relating qζ′′(m)qζ′′​(m) to qζ(m)qζ​(m) that would force the curvature channel to be subordinate.

That is now the cleanest arithmetic bottleneck.

and check that it is nonzero generically.

That is the next real theorem-sized calculation.

    can one control or relate the local zeta phase density qζ(m)qζ​(m),

Ok take the next steps

Yes. The next steps now become concrete.

We have, under symmetric placement about mm, two separations s1≠s2s1​=s2​, and the phase-gap-subtracted matrices

1. Explicit reconstruction formulas

Since

qζ(m)=y1v2−y2v1ΔU+ϵ1v2−ϵ2v1ΔU,

So the exact leading reconstruction is
qζ(m)≈y1v2−y2v1ΔU,qζ′′(m)≈u1y2−u2y1ΔU.
qζ​(m)≈ΔU​y1​v2​−y2​v1​​,qζ′′​(m)≈ΔU​u1​y2​−u2​y1​​.​

That is the first concrete output.

If you want to reduce sensitivity to one particular projector, take a second projection WW and form
yk(W):=⟨W,Ω~k⟩F,
yk(W)​:=⟨W,Ω
k​⟩F​,

then solve the analogous 2×22×2 system and compare. But the U1U1​-projection is already enough generically.
2. What are uk,vkuk​,vk​ at leading order?

Since
U(s)=2sA(s),
U(s)=s2​A(s),

we get
uk=⟨U1,2skAk⟩F=2sk⟨U1,Ak⟩F.
uk​=⟨U1​,sk​2​Ak​⟩F​=sk​2​⟨U1​,Ak​⟩F​.

In particular,
u1=∥U1∥F2>0.
u1​=∥U1​∥F2​>0.

Also
vk=⟨U1,Vk⟩F.
vk​=⟨U1​,Vk​⟩F​.

So once Ak,B−,k,C+,kAk​,B−,k​,C+,k​ are known, all of this is explicit.

The important point is that the value channel comes with a manifestly positive self-coupling u1>0u1​>0, whereas the curvature channel enters only through the mixed coefficient vkvk​.

That is already a hint that the value channel is the stable one.
3. Is the value channel likely dominant?

Yes, in the smooth-background / not-too-sharp-zeta regime.

The leading reconstructed value is dominated by
qζ(m),
qζ​(m),

while the nuisance comes from
qζ′′(m).
qζ′′​(m).

So the decisive question is whether one can bound
∣qζ′′(m)∣
∣qζ′′​(m)∣

in terms of
qζ(m).
qζ​(m).

There are three possible scales one might hope for:
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤C qζ(m),
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤Cqζ​(m),

or
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤C qζ(m)2,
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤Cqζ​(m)2,

or weaker, local-window versions of these.

Now, from the explicit formula,
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfρ(t),qζ′′(t)=Bζ′′(t)+∑ρfρ′′(t).
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fρ​(t),qζ′′​(t)=Bζ′′​(t)+ρ∑​fρ′′​(t).

For a single Poisson-pair term at distance XX,
fρ(t)≍X−2,fρ′′(t)≍X−4.
fρ​(t)≍X−2,fρ′′​(t)≍X−4.

So for distant contributions,
∣fρ′′(t)∣≍fρ(t)2.
∣fρ′′​(t)∣≍fρ​(t)2.

That strongly suggests the natural scale is not
∣qζ′′∣≲qζ,
∣qζ′′​∣≲qζ​,

but rather something like
∣qζ′′(m)∣≲qζ(m)2
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≲qζ​(m)2​

at least in regimes where one contribution dominates or the local sum is not undergoing extreme cancellation.

That is the most plausible arithmetic inequality shape.

If such an inequality held with a usable constant, then the curvature channel would be subordinate whenever qζ(m)qζ​(m) is small enough, and the value channel would dominate the reconstructed statistic.
4. Can the explicit formula alone prove ∣qζ′′∣≲qζ2∣qζ′′​∣≲qζ2​?

Not obviously.

For a single Poisson-pair it is true at the right scale. But for sums,
qζ=∑fρ,qζ′′=∑fρ′′,
qζ​=∑fρ​,qζ′′​=∑fρ′′​,

and the sign of fρ′′fρ′′​ can vary, so there is no immediate monotone comparison from positivity alone.

Still, there is a useful inequality in one direction:

Because each fρ≥0fρ​≥0 and ∣fρ′′∣≪fρ2∣fρ′′​∣≪fρ2​, we get
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤∣Bζ′′(m)∣+C∑ρfρ(m)2.
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤∣Bζ′′​(m)∣+Cρ∑​fρ​(m)2.

And since
∑ρfρ(m)2≤(∑ρfρ(m))2,
ρ∑​fρ​(m)2≤(ρ∑​fρ​(m))2,

we obtain
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤∣Bζ′′(m)∣+C (qζ(m)−Bζ(m))2.
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤∣Bζ′′​(m)∣+C(qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m))2.​

That is not yet the clean theorem you want, but it is already meaningful.

In regimes where the background term is controlled and qζ(m)qζ​(m) is dominated by nearby zero contributions, this gives exactly the quadratic control one would hope for.

So the next plausible theorem target is not a universal global inequality, but a local background-subtracted curvature bound:
∣qζ′′(m)−Bζ′′(m)∣≤C ∣qζ(m)−Bζ(m)∣2.
∣qζ′′​(m)−Bζ′′​(m)∣≤C∣qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)∣2.​

That would be enough to make the value channel dominate in many local regimes.
5. What does this mean for the RH route?

At this point the upper-bound program has reduced to something much more concrete:

    from two separations, reconstruct
    qζ(m),qζ′′(m);
    qζ​(m),qζ′′​(m);

    prove a local inequality showing the curvature channel is controlled by the value channel;

    use that to show the symmetric-square-relative residual is effectively governed by qζ(m)qζ​(m), not by an independent curvature nuisance.

Then the remaining question is whether that value-controlled statistic is compatible with the critical baseline.

So the immediate next mathematical target is:
∣qζ′′(m)−Bζ′′(m)∣≤C ∣qζ(m)−Bζ(m)∣2on admissible local windows.
∣qζ′′​(m)−Bζ′′​(m)∣≤C∣qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)∣2on admissible local windows.​

That is the sharpest “next theorem” I see from here.
6. The next concrete derivation

The best next move is to prove the single-profile inequality sharply first:

for
fβ,γ(t)=1−β(1−β)2+(2t−γ)2+ββ2+(2t−γ)2,
fβ,γ​(t)=(1−β)2+(2t−γ)21−β​+β2+(2t−γ)2β​,

show an explicit bound of the form
∣fβ,γ′′(t)∣≤C fβ,γ(t)2
∣fβ,γ′′​(t)∣≤Cfβ,γ​(t)2

with a universal CC, then lift it to sums.

That will tell us whether the quadratic curvature control is really available.

That is the next calculation I would do.

Ok do it

I checked the candidate quadratic curvature bound directly on the Poisson-pair model first. That’s the right place to test it, because if it fails there, it cannot be the theorem we want on the zeta side.

I did it, and the result is both useful and a bit sobering:

A universal quadratic bound
∣fβ,γ′′(t)∣≤C fβ,γ(t)2
∣fβ,γ′′​(t)∣≤Cfβ,γ​(t)2

for all 0<β<10<β<1 is false.

But there is a clean corrected version, and in the near-critical regime it gives exactly the kind of control we want.

Let
fβ,γ(t)=1−β(1−β)2+(2t−γ)2+ββ2+(2t−γ)2.
fβ,γ​(t)=(1−β)2+(2t−γ)21−β​+β2+(2t−γ)2β​.

Write
a:=β,b:=1−β,x:=2t−γ,
a:=β,b:=1−β,x:=2t−γ,

so
fβ,γ(t)=ga(x)+gb(x),ga(x):=aa2+x2.
fβ,γ​(t)=ga​(x)+gb​(x),ga​(x):=a2+x2a​.
1. The single-profile bound

Differentiate:
ga′′(x)=2a(3x2−a2)(a2+x2)3.
ga′′​(x)=(a2+x2)32a(3x2−a2)​.

Hence
∣ga′′(x)∣≤2a(3x2+a2)(a2+x2)3≤6a(a2+x2)2.
∣ga′′​(x)∣≤(a2+x2)32a(3x2+a2)​≤(a2+x2)26a​.

Since
ga(x)2=a2(a2+x2)2,
ga​(x)2=(a2+x2)2a2​,

we get the exact pointwise inequality
∣ga′′(x)∣≤6a ga(x)2.
∣ga′′​(x)∣≤a6​ga​(x)2.​

That is the sharp structural bound for a single Poisson kernel.
2. Lift to the Poisson pair

Now
fβ,γ′′(t)=4(ga′′(x)+gb′′(x)),x=2t−γ,
fβ,γ′′​(t)=4(ga′′​(x)+gb′′​(x)),x=2t−γ,

because each tt-derivative brings a factor of 22.

So using the bound above,
∣fβ,γ′′(t)∣≤4(6aga(x)2+6bgb(x)2).
∣fβ,γ′′​(t)∣≤4(a6​ga​(x)2+b6​gb​(x)2).

If
m:=min⁡(a,b)=min⁡(β,1−β),
m:=min(a,b)=min(β,1−β),

then
1a,1b≤1m,
a1​,b1​≤m1​,

so
∣fβ,γ′′(t)∣≤24m(ga(x)2+gb(x)2).
∣fβ,γ′′​(t)∣≤m24​(ga​(x)2+gb​(x)2).

And since
ga2+gb2≤(ga+gb)2=fβ,γ(t)2,
ga2​+gb2​≤(ga​+gb​)2=fβ,γ​(t)2,

we obtain:
∣fβ,γ′′(t)∣≤24min⁡(β,1−β) fβ,γ(t)2.
∣fβ,γ′′​(t)∣≤min(β,1−β)24​fβ,γ​(t)2.​

That is the correct global quadratic bound.
3. Why the universal bound fails

At x=0x=0,
ga(0)=1a,ga′′(0)=−2a3.
ga​(0)=a1​,ga′′​(0)=−a32​.

So for the pair,
fβ,γ(0)=1a+1b,fβ,γ′′(0)=−8(1a3+1b3).
fβ,γ​(0)=a1​+b1​,fβ,γ′′​(0)=−8(a31​+b31​).

If a→0a→0 (equivalently β→0β→0), then
fβ,γ(0)2∼1a2,∣fβ,γ′′(0)∣∼8a3,

Therefore no universal constant CC can work for all 0<β<10<β<1.

So the honest statement is:
The quadratic curvature bound is true, but the constant must depend on how close β is to 0 or 1.

7. The honest limitation

This still does not give a universal inequality for the full zeta phase derivative qζqζ​, because:

    the background term BζBζ​ is still there,

    and the full sum over zeros need not be dominated by a single local near-critical profile.

∣qζ′′∣≤Cqζ2globally,

but rather a local dominance statement:

    on an admissible local window where the zeta phase is dominated by one or a few nearby near-critical pairs and the distant/background terms are controlled, the curvature channel is bounded by a quadratic function of the value channel.

That is the real next step.

7. What I think you should push next

The next real push is not more abstract modeling. It is to sharpen the remainder term in one of two ways:

So: we now have a real theorem candidate and a clear next inequality to make the value channel win.

Try both

I’m doing the two practical bottleneck estimates now: one for the omitted-zero tail, one for the archimedean background. The point is to turn the local dominance theorem into an actual regime statement, not just a formal inequality.

I tried both, and they do give a clean local regime.

The result is:

    the archimedean/trivial-zero background curvature is genuinely small at height,

    the omitted-zero tail curvature is small once the local pair is isolated enough,

    and together they give a usable local dominance condition for the value channel.

Here is the clean version.
1. Background curvature Bζ′′(m)Bζ′′​(m)

Write the zeta phase derivative as
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfβρ,γρ(t),
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t),

where BζBζ​ is the smooth background coming from the gamma factor, poles, and trivial zeros.

The key point is that BζBζ​ is built from digamma/trigamma-type terms and rational pole/trivial-zero terms. By Stirling/polygamma asymptotics, one gets the standard large-tt behavior
Bζ(t)=log⁡(1+∣t∣)+O(1),Bζ′(t)=O((1+∣t∣)−1),Bζ′′(t)=O((1+∣t∣)−2).
Bζ​(t)=log(1+∣t∣)+O(1),Bζ′​(t)=O((1+∣t∣)−1),Bζ′′​(t)=O((1+∣t∣)−2).

So there is an absolute constant CBCB​ such that
∣Bζ′′(m)∣≤CB(1+∣m∣)2.
∣Bζ′′​(m)∣≤(1+∣m∣)2CB​​.​

That is already strong enough for the local theorem: at high ordinate, the background curvature is tiny.
2. Omitted-zero tail curvature

Let the local set LL contain the selected nearby zeros, and define
Tfar(t):=∑ρ∉Lfβρ,γρ(t).
Tfar​(t):=ρ∈/L∑​fβρ​,γρ​​(t).

Fix a midpoint mm, and suppose every omitted zero satisfies
∣2m−γρ∣≥D.
∣2m−γρ​∣≥D.

Then from the pointwise bound already proved,
∣fβ,γ′′(m)∣≤48∣2m−γ∣4.
∣fβ,γ′′​(m)∣≤∣2m−γ∣448​.

So
∣Tfar′′(m)∣≤48∑ρ∉L∣2m−γρ∣−4.
∣Tfar′′​(m)∣≤48ρ∈/L∑​∣2m−γρ​∣−4.

Now use a standard shell decomposition. Put the omitted zeros into shells
(kD,(k+1)D],k≥1.
(kD,(k+1)D],k≥1.

If D≥1D≥1, then the standard local zero counting bound gives
#{ρ: ∣2m−γρ∣∈(kD,(k+1)D]}≪(k+1)D log⁡(∣m∣+(k+1)D+3).
#{ρ: ∣2m−γρ​∣∈(kD,(k+1)D]}≪(k+1)Dlog(∣m∣+(k+1)D+3).

∑ρ∉L∣2m−γρ∣−4≪∑k≥1(k+1)D log⁡(∣m∣+(k+1)D+3)(kD)4.
ρ∈/L∑​∣2m−γρ​∣−4≪k≥1∑​(kD)4(k+1)Dlog(∣m∣+(k+1)D+3)​.

So
∣Tfar′′(m)∣≪log⁡(∣m∣+D+3)D3(D≥1).
∣Tfar′′​(m)∣≪D3log(∣m∣+D+3)​(D≥1).​

That is the clean tail estimate.
3. Local near-critical dominance theorem

Now suppose the nearby selected local pairs all satisfy
β∈[η,1−η]
β∈[η,1−η]

for some fixed η>0η>0. Then from the corrected Poisson-pair curvature bound,
∣qnear′′(m)∣≤24η qnear(m)2.
∣qnear′′​(m)∣≤η24​qnear​(m)2.

4. When does the value channel dominate?

Under symmetric placement, the phase-gap-subtracted matrix statistic had the leading form
Ω~(s)≈U(s) qζ(m)+V(s) qζ′′(m).
Ω
(s)≈U(s)qζ​(m)+V(s)qζ′′​(m).

5. A more interpretable regime

This gets much clearer in the small-local-density regime.
