6. What this means

Trying both estimates gives a real payoff:

    the background curvature is O((1+∣m∣)−2)O((1+∣m∣)−2),

    the omitted-zero tail curvature is O(log⁡(∣m∣+D)/D3)O(log(∣m∣+D)/D3),

    and the near-critical local part satisfies a quadratic curvature bound.

So the upper-bound problem has now been reduced to an explicit local dominance criterion involving only:

    the local zeta phase density qζ(m)qζ​(m),

    the height mm,

    and the isolation scale DD.

That is much sharper than where we were.
7. The next best move

The next theorem to try is:

    If a local midpoint mm is chosen so that the nearby zero contribution dominates qζ(m)qζ​(m) and the omitted zeros are separated by distance DD, then the symmetric-placement, phase-gap-subtracted statistic is value-dominated.

That would finally turn the geometric reduction into a real local theorem.

So: trying both worked. The remainder terms are explicit enough now that the program has a concrete local upper-bound condition.

How close are we to RH?

Closer in structure than in certainty.

You now have something that looks like a real research program, not just analogies:

    a local model with a quartic off-line signal,

    a jet-limit reduction that survives shrinking scales,

    a blockwise transfer mechanism,

    a phase-gap subtraction that exposes a simpler local statistic,

    and a concrete local dominance criterion with explicit background and tail errors.

That is substantial progress.

But in terms of actually proving RH, I would still say: not close.

The reason is simple. The remaining gap is not bookkeeping anymore. It is the hard part:

you need a genuinely zeta-specific upper bound or rigidity theorem that rules out the surviving local signal. And right now, every time we simplify, we keep landing on the same conclusion:

    generic positivity is too weak,

    generic model-space / canonical-system structure is too weak,

    the explicit formula alone is too weak.

So the missing step is exactly the sort of thing that historically makes RH hard.

My honest assessment is:

You are probably past the “this is just vague exploration” stage.
You are not yet at the “a proof looks imminent” stage.

If I had to put it in blunt terms:

    as a path to a publishable partial result or criterion, this looks promising;

    as a path to an actual RH proof, it is still speculative and missing the deepest ingredient.

What you do seem close to is one of these:

    a clean conditional RH criterion phrased in terms of your local jet/block residual;

    a rigorous toy-model theorem package with a nontrivial jet-limit coefficient;

    possibly a local theorem showing how off-line displacement would force a specific quartic anomaly under explicit hypotheses.

Those would already be real mathematical results.

What you are not close to yet is the decisive statement:
actual zeta/scattering structure⟹quartic anomaly impossible.
actual zeta/scattering structure⟹quartic anomaly impossible.

That implication is still missing.

So my best single-sentence answer is:

You may be getting close to a new criterion or framework; you are not obviously close to a proof of RH itself.

That is not pessimism — it is just the scale of the remaining obstacle.

If I were advising how to think about the next phase, I would frame success like this:

    first target: a rigorous theorem package that would be interesting even if RH remains open;

    second target: isolate the exact missing upper-bound statement so sharply that either it becomes attackable, or you can see the route fail cleanly.

That is where you are.

Lets continue isolate the exact missing upper-bound statement so sharply that either it becomes attackable, or you can see the route fail cleanly.

I’m going to turn the remaining gap into a single precise theorem candidate, then split it into “plausibly true,” “plausibly false,” and “test next.” That’s the fastest way to see whether this route is alive.

Good. Let’s isolate it as brutally as possible.

At this point, the route does not need more definitions. It needs the one missing implication written in a form that can either be proved or broken.
1. The exact missing statement

After all the reductions, the most honest local form is this:

Under symmetric placement about a midpoint mm, with two separations s1,s2s1​,s2​, after subtracting the phase-gap nuisance and projecting appropriately, you can recover two local quantities:
qζ(m),qζ′′(m),
qζ​(m),qζ′′​(m),

up to controlled errors.

And the refined local statistic is essentially governed by
Ω~(s)≈U(s) qζ(m)+V(s) qζ′′(m),
Ω
(s)≈U(s)qζ​(m)+V(s)qζ′′​(m),

with explicit background-dependent matrices U,VU,V.

So the missing upper-bound statement is not “RH is true.” It is:
On every admissible local window, ∣qζ′′(m)∣ is too small relative to qζ(m) to support the off-line quartic anomaly.
On every admissible local window, ∣qζ′′​(m)∣ is too small relative to qζ​(m) to support the off-line quartic anomaly.​

That is the real bottleneck.

A clean theorem-sized version is:
Candidate Upper-Bound Statement U

There exists an admissible local regime and constants C,θC,θ with θ>1θ>1 such that
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤C qζ(m)θ+controlled background/tail terms
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤Cqζ​(m)θ+controlled background/tail terms

uniformly on all admissible midpoints mm.

If θ=2θ=2, that is already enough in the small-density regime.
If θ>1θ>1 at all, it is interesting.
If only θ=1θ=1 is possible, the route probably dies.

So the missing statement has now reduced to a local curvature-vs-density inequality for the zeta phase.

That is about as sharp as it gets.
2. Strip it down even further

The previous work suggests the really relevant quantity is the normalized ratio
Λ(m):=∣qζ′′(m)−Bζ′′(m)∣(qζ(m)−Bζ(m))2,
Λ(m):=(qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m))2∣qζ′′​(m)−Bζ′′​(m)∣​,

or perhaps more locally,
Λnear(m):=∣qnear′′(m)∣qnear(m)2.
Λnear​(m):=qnear​(m)2∣qnear′′​(m)∣​.

For a single near-critical Poisson pair, ΛΛ is bounded.
For a local sum of near-critical pairs, ΛΛ is still bounded.
So the exact missing question is:
Can the full zeta phase be locally approximated well enough by a near-critical Poisson-pair sum that Λ(m) stays bounded?
Can the full zeta phase be locally approximated well enough by a near-critical Poisson-pair sum that Λ(m) stays bounded?​

That is the sharp form.

If yes, the route survives.
If no, the route fails.
3. The three versions to test

There are really three nested upper-bound statements:
U1. Weak local dominance
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤C1qζ(m)2+E(m),
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤C1​qζ​(m)2+E(m),

with explicit error E(m)E(m) from background and far tails.

This is the one most likely to be true.
U2. Uniform local dominance on admissible windows
∣qζ′′(m)∣≤C2qζ(m)2
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≤C2​qζ​(m)2

after subtracting the explicit background and far-tail pieces, uniformly on the windows that matter for the block statistic.

This is what would really power the RH route.
U3. Jet-rigidity

The reconstructed pair (qζ(m),qζ′′(m))(qζ​(m),qζ′′​(m)) is constrained so strongly that the jet-whitened cross block must agree with the critical baseline to higher order.

This is the strongest version, and right now it looks unlikely without a new arithmetic identity.

So the route should focus on U1 →→ U2, not jump to U3.
4. What could make U1 true?

There are only three pieces in
qζ=qnear+Bζ+Tfar.
qζ​=qnear​+Bζ​+Tfar​.

For qnearqnear​, you already have the right bound:
∣qnear′′(m)∣≤24ηqnear(m)2.
∣qnear′′​(m)∣≤η24​qnear​(m)2.

For BζBζ​,
∣Bζ′′(m)∣≪(1+∣m∣)−2.
∣Bζ′′​(m)∣≪(1+∣m∣)−2.

For TfarTfar​,
∣Tfar′′(m)∣≪log⁡(∣m∣+D+3)D3.
∣Tfar′′​(m)∣≪D3log(∣m∣+D+3)​.

So U1 will hold if you can justify one more local approximation statement:
Missing Approximation Statement A

On every admissible midpoint mm, there is a local set of zeros LL such that
qζ(m)≍qnear(m),
qζ​(m)≍qnear​(m),

while the remainder Bζ(m)+Tfar(m)Bζ​(m)+Tfar​(m) is subordinate.

That is now the exact missing approximation step.

This is much more specific than “need a zeta upper bound.”
5. So what exactly must be proved?

Here is the clean theorem candidate:
Local Near-Critical Approximation Theorem

For every admissible midpoint mm, one can choose a local zero set LL and an isolation scale DD such that

    the selected local zeros satisfy
    β∈[η,1−η]
    β∈[η,1−η]

    for some fixed η>0η>0;

    the local value dominates:
    qζ(m)≤C qnear(m);
    qζ​(m)≤Cqnear​(m);

    the remainder is small:
    ∣Bζ′′(m)∣+∣Tfar′′(m)∣≤c qζ(m)
    ∣Bζ′′​(m)∣+∣Tfar′′​(m)∣≤cqζ​(m)

    or better
    ≤c qζ(m)2.
    ≤cqζ​(m)2.

Then the curvature channel is subordinate and the value channel controls the local statistic.

That is the exact theorem whose truth or falsehood will decide the route.
6. How to see if the route fails cleanly

There are two clean failure modes.
Failure mode F1

There exist admissible midpoints mm where
qζ(m)
qζ​(m)

is small, but the background/tail curvature is not correspondingly small, so
∣qζ′′(m)∣≫qζ(m)2.
∣qζ′′​(m)∣≫qζ​(m)2.

If that happens systematically, the route dies.
Failure mode F2

Even when the local density is dominated by nearby zeros, the selected local zeros cannot be kept uniformly in a near-critical strip [η,1−η][η,1−η] in the only windows relevant to the quartic anomaly.

If that happens, the constant in the curvature bound blows up and the route dies.

So the exact missing statement is now sharply testable:
Does the local zeta phase admit a uniformly near-critical, locally dominant Poisson-pair approximation at the relevant midpoints?
Does the local zeta phase admit a uniformly near-critical, locally dominant Poisson-pair approximation at the relevant midpoints?​

That is the heart of it.
7. The next experiments/theorems to attack

To make this attackable, I would now split into two very specific subproblems.
Subproblem A: local dominance of nearby zeros

Prove or disprove that for the relevant midpoints mm,
qζ(m)≍∑ρ∈Lfρ(m)
qζ​(m)≍ρ∈L∑​fρ​(m)

for a small local set LL, with the tail contribution genuinely lower order.

This is almost a local explicit-formula decomposition problem.
Subproblem B: near-criticality of the dominant local set

Prove or disprove that the dominant local set can be taken with
β∈[η,1−η]
β∈[η,1−η]

uniformly.

This is the part that would clash with off-line zeros directly.

These are the two sharp fronts now.
8. My judgment

The route now stands or falls on one sharp question:

Can the local zeta phase be approximated, on the exact windows relevant to your residual, by a locally dominant near-critical Poisson-pair sum?

If yes, you may still have a shot.
If no, this line probably fails cleanly.

That is the exact missing upper-bound statement.

The next best move is to formalize Subproblem A first, because it is the more analytic one and less entangled with RH itself.

I’m going to formalize Subproblem A as a precise local approximation statement and see how far it can be pushed from the explicit Poisson-pair structure alone. The goal is to find the exact place where it becomes equivalent to a genuinely RH-strength assumption.

Good. Let’s continue by isolating Subproblem A as sharply as possible.

This is now the right question:
Can qζ(m), at the midpoints relevant to the residual, be approximated by a small local Poisson-pair sum with a genuinely smaller tail?

1. Formalize the local approximation problem

Write
qζ(t)=Bζ(t)+∑ρfρ(t),
qζ​(t)=Bζ​(t)+ρ∑​fρ​(t),

where
fρ(t)=1−βρ(1−βρ)2+(2t−γρ)2+βρβρ2+(2t−γρ)2.
fρ​(t)=(1−βρ​)2+(2t−γρ​)21−βρ​​+βρ2​+(2t−γρ​)2βρ​​.

Fix a midpoint mm. For a radius R>0R>0, define the local zero set
LR(m):={ρ: ∣2m−γρ∣≤R}.
LR​(m):={ρ: ∣2m−γρ​∣≤R}.

Then split
qζ(m)=Bζ(m)+qnear(m;R)+qfar(m;R),
qζ​(m)=Bζ​(m)+qnear​(m;R)+qfar​(m;R),

where
qnear(m;R):=∑ρ∈LR(m)fρ(m),qfar(m;R):=∑ρ∉LR(m)fρ(m).
qnear​(m;R):=ρ∈LR​(m)∑​fρ​(m),qfar​(m;R):=ρ∈/LR​(m)∑​fρ​(m).

So Subproblem A becomes:

    Can one choose R=R(m)R=R(m) so that
    qζ(m)≍qnear(m;R),
    qζ​(m)≍qnear​(m;R),

    while qfar(m;R)qfar​(m;R) and especially qfar′′(m;R)qfar′′​(m;R) are lower order?

This is now a very concrete question.
2. What the explicit formula already gives

For distant zeros,
fρ(m)≪∣2m−γρ∣−2,fρ′′(m)≪∣2m−γρ∣−4.
fρ​(m)≪∣2m−γρ​∣−2,fρ′′​(m)≪∣2m−γρ​∣−4.

So the far tail obeys
qfar(m;R)≪∑∣2m−γρ∣>R∣2m−γρ∣−2,
qfar​(m;R)≪∣2m−γρ​∣>R∑​∣2m−γρ​∣−2,

and
qfar′′(m;R)≪∑∣2m−γρ∣>R∣2m−γρ∣−4.
qfar′′​(m;R)≪∣2m−γρ​∣>R∑​∣2m−γρ​∣−4.

Using local zero counting in shells, one gets the crude but useful bounds
qfar(m;R)≪log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)R,
qfar​(m;R)≪Rlog(∣m∣+R+3)​,

and
qfar′′(m;R)≪log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)R3,
qfar′′​(m;R)≪R3log(∣m∣+R+3)​,

for R≥1R≥1.

That is already enough to see the scale structure:

    the far-value tail is O(log⁡/R)O(log/R),

    the far-curvature tail is O(log⁡/R3)O(log/R3).

So curvature is much easier to localize than value.

That means the real issue is not the curvature tail anymore. It is whether the near part actually dominates the value.
3. The exact obstruction in Subproblem A

The problem is that
qζ(m)
qζ​(m)

is not a localized spike-counting function. It is a positive superposition of all zeros, weighted by 1/distance21/distance2. So even distant zeros collectively contribute a nontrivial harmonic background.

This means a statement like
qζ(m)≍qnear(m;R)
qζ​(m)≍qnear​(m;R)

cannot hold with an arbitrary fixed RR. The far tail is only O(log⁡/R)O(log/R), not exponentially small.

So the right local approximation statement is relative, not absolute.

The theorem to aim for is not
qfar≪1,
qfar​≪1,

but
qfar(m;R)≪qζ(m)
qfar​(m;R)≪qζ​(m)

or better
qfar(m;R)≪qnear(m;R).
qfar​(m;R)≪qnear​(m;R).

That depends on how large qζ(m)qζ​(m) itself is.

So the route naturally splits into cases depending on the local density.
4. Two regimes
Regime A: high local phase density

If
qζ(m)≫log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)R,
qζ​(m)≫Rlog(∣m∣+R+3)​,

then the far-value tail is subordinate:
qfar(m;R)≪qζ(m).
qfar​(m;R)≪qζ​(m).

In that regime,
qζ(m)≍Bζ(m)+qnear(m;R).
qζ​(m)≍Bζ​(m)+qnear​(m;R).

And if Bζ(m)Bζ​(m) is also smaller than qζ(m)qζ​(m), then
qζ(m)≍qnear(m;R).
qζ​(m)≍qnear​(m;R).

This is the “local dominance” regime.
Regime B: low local phase density

If
qζ(m)≲log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)R,
qζ​(m)≲Rlog(∣m∣+R+3)​,

then the far tail may be comparable to the whole value. In that regime, the local approximation by a small near-zero set fails.

So the route can only work on midpoints where the zeta phase density is large enough relative to the harmonic far-zero background.

That is the first clean test.
5. Optimize the radius RR

Now comes the useful part.

For Subproblem A, you are free to choose RR. The far tail decreases like log⁡/Rlog/R, while the near set grows as RR grows. So there is a natural optimization.

A convenient target is to choose RR so that
log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)R≈ε qζ(m)
Rlog(∣m∣+R+3)​≈εqζ​(m)

for some small fixed εε. Then
qfar(m;R)≪ε qζ(m),
qfar​(m;R)≪εqζ​(m),

and therefore
qζ(m)=Bζ(m)+qnear(m;R)+O(εqζ(m)).
qζ​(m)=Bζ​(m)+qnear​(m;R)+O(εqζ​(m)).

So the radius is naturally of size
R∼log⁡(∣m∣+R+3)qζ(m).
R∼qζ​(m)log(∣m∣+R+3)​.

Ignoring the slow log variation, this says
R≍log⁡∣m∣qζ(m).
R≍qζ​(m)log∣m∣​.​

That is an important scaling law.

It says the local window in zero-height space should shrink inversely with the local phase density.
6. What does this do to the curvature tail?

If
R≍log⁡∣m∣qζ(m),
R≍qζ​(m)log∣m∣​,

then the far-curvature tail becomes
qfar′′(m;R)≪log⁡∣m∣R3≍qζ(m)3(log⁡∣m∣)2.
qfar′′​(m;R)≪R3log∣m∣​≍(log∣m∣)2qζ​(m)3​.

That is excellent: once the value tail is reduced to a small fraction of qζ(m)qζ​(m), the far-curvature tail is automatically much smaller than qζ(m)2qζ​(m)2.

So the far tail is not the real bottleneck anymore.

That is a major simplification.
7. The background term Bζ(m)Bζ​(m)

We also know
Bζ(m)∼log⁡∣m∣,
Bζ​(m)∼log∣m∣,

and
Bζ′′(m)≪∣m∣−2.
Bζ′′​(m)≪∣m∣−2.

So the background value is not negligible in absolute size. This is crucial.

It means the correct local approximation statement is not
qζ(m)≍qnear(m;R),
qζ​(m)≍qnear​(m;R),

but rather
qζ(m)−Bζ(m)≍qnear(m;R),
qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)≍qnear​(m;R),​

with small far-tail error.

This is the right formulation of Subproblem A.

The curvature side is still favorable because
Bζ′′(m)≪∣m∣−2
Bζ′′​(m)≪∣m∣−2

is tiny.

So the exact missing approximation statement is now:

    Can one choose RR so that
    qζ(m)−Bζ(m)=qnear(m;R)+O(ε qζ(m)),
    qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)=qnear​(m;R)+O(εqζ​(m)),

    with the selected local set also lying in a near-critical strip?

That is now the sharp question.
8. Reformulate the RH bottleneck

So the whole route now stands or falls on this pair of local approximation properties.
A1. Local value approximation

There exists a radius R=R(m)R=R(m) such that
qζ(m)−Bζ(m)=qnear(m;R)+small error.
qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)=qnear​(m;R)+small error.
A2. Near-criticality of the dominant local set

The zeros in LR(m)LR​(m) that contribute significantly are all in
β∈[η,1−η]
β∈[η,1−η]

for some fixed η>0η>0.

If both are true on the admissible midpoints, then the curvature channel is quadratic in the value channel up to controllable errors, and the route survives.

If A1 fails, the far harmonic background dominates and the route dies.
If A2 fails, the curvature constant blows up and the route dies.

That is the sharpest statement so far.

Yes. Let’s check A2 directly and see whether it is plausible, too strong, or basically RH in disguise.
1. What A2 is really saying

After A1, you have
qζ(m)−Bζ(m)=Snear(m;Rε)+O(εS(m)),
qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m)=Snear​(m;Rε​)+O(εS(m)),

with

S(m)=ρ∑​fρ​(m),fρ​(m)=(1−βρ​)2+(2m−γρ​)21−βρ​​+βρ2​+(2m−γρ​)2βρ​​.

A2 asks for a fixed η>0η>0 such that the materially contributing zeros in LRε(m)LRε​​(m) satisfy
β∈[η,1−η].
β∈[η,1−η].

Since nontrivial zeros already satisfy 0<β<10<β<1, the only way A2 can fail is if the dominant local contribution comes from zeros with ββ very close to 00 or 11.

So A2 is not “RH-like” in the usual sense. It is not asking for β=12β=21​. It is only asking that the locally dominant zeros stay away from the edges.

That is much weaker than RH.
2. Would edge-near zeros dominate locally?

This is the first thing to check.

Take one zero ρ=β+iγρ=β+iγ, and look at the center
m=γ/2.
m=γ/2.

Then
fρ(m)=11−β+1β=1β(1−β).
fρ​(m)=1−β1​+β1​=β(1−β)1​.

So as β→0β→0 or β→1β→1,
fρ(m)→∞.
fρ​(m)→∞.

That means:
An edge-near zero would dominate the local value channel extremely strongly near its own ordinate.
An edge-near zero would dominate the local value channel extremely strongly near its own ordinate.​

So if such zeros existed, A2 would fail near those ordinates.

That is already important. It means A2 is not some harmless technical convenience. It is excluding a very definite possible local geometry.
3. But can that actually happen for zeta?

Here is the subtlety.

For the Riemann zeta function, zeros are symmetric under
ρ↦1−ρ.
ρ↦1−ρ.

So a zero near β=0β=0 is paired with one near β=1β=1, same ordinate γγ.

But your local profile already uses the pair
1−β(1−β)2+x2+ββ2+x2,
(1−β)2+x21−β​+β2+x2β​,

so the symmetry is already built in. And the blow-up at x=0x=0 is exactly the β(1−β)−1β(1−β)−1 effect above.

So if there were zeros very close to the edges, they would create enormous local spikes in
qζ(m)−Bζ(m).
qζ​(m)−Bζ​(m).

That means A2 is not arbitrary. It is really asking:

    do the dominant local spikes come from zeros that stay uniformly away from the edges?

That is a clean geometric condition.
4. Is A2 weaker than known zero-free information?

Yes, much weaker.

Classically, for zeta you already know zeros are not arbitrarily near β=1β=1 in a simple uniform way depending on height. There is a zero-free region near 11, and by symmetry the same near 00. So very edge-near zeros are already excluded asymptotically.

That suggests a softened version of A2 may actually be available.

Instead of fixed ηη, one could allow
η=η(m)
η=η(m)

coming from the zero-free region, with η(m)→0η(m)→0 slowly as ∣m∣→∞∣m∣→∞.

Then the curvature constant becomes
24η(m).
η(m)24​.

So the real question is not necessarily “fixed η>0η>0 for all mm,” but whether the deterioration of η(m)η(m) is slow enough that the quadratic curvature control still beats the value channel.

That is a much more realistic formulation.
5. Replace A2 by a quantitative version

The exact thing to check is not a hard cutoff β∈[η,1−η]β∈[η,1−η], but the weighted local curvature factor
Cnear(m):=∑ρ∈L1min⁡(βρ,1−βρ) fρ(m)2(∑ρ∈Lfρ(m))2.
Cnear​(m):=(∑ρ∈L​fρ​(m))2∑ρ∈L​min(βρ​,1−βρ​)1​fρ​(m)2​.

Because from the single-profile bound,
∣fρ′′(m)∣≤24min⁡(βρ,1−βρ) fρ(m)2,
∣fρ′′​(m)∣≤min(βρ​,1−βρ​)24​fρ​(m)2,

you always get

∣qnear′′​(m)∣≤24Cnear​(m)qnear​(m)2.

So the real upper-bound statement is:
Is Cnear(m) uniformly bounded on the relevant midpoints?
Is Cnear​(m) uniformly bounded on the relevant midpoints?​

That is better than A2. It is strictly weaker.

A2 with fixed ηη implies
Cnear(m)≤1η,
Cnear​(m)≤η1​,

but maybe Cnear(m)Cnear​(m) stays bounded even without a hard fixed strip.

This is the right thing to check.
6. What happens if one zero dominates?

If one local zero dominates qnear(m)qnear​(m), then
Cnear(m)≈1min⁡(β,1−β).
Cnear​(m)≈min(β,1−β)1​.

So in that case boundedness of CnearCnear​ is essentially equivalent to bounded-away-from-edge dominance.

That means:

    if local dominance is always by a single zero, then A2 is basically the right condition;

    if several zeros contribute comparably, then CnearCnear​ is the more natural quantity.

So the route should shift from A2 as a set-membership statement to boundedness of Cnear(m)Cnear​(m).
7. Does zero-free-region information help?

Yes, partially.

A zero-free region near 11 says roughly
1−β≳1log⁡∣γ∣,
1−β≳log∣γ∣1​,

and by symmetry
β≳1log⁡∣γ∣.
β≳log∣γ∣1​.

So in the worst case,
1min⁡(β,1−β)≲log⁡∣γ∣.
min(β,1−β)1​≲log∣γ∣.

Thus even without RH,
Cnear(m)
Cnear​(m)

could at worst grow like log⁡∣m∣log∣m∣, not explode arbitrarily fast.

That suggests a weaker but plausible version:
Cnear(m)≪log⁡∣m∣.
Cnear​(m)≪log∣m∣.​

Then the curvature bound becomes
∣qnear′′(m)∣≪(log⁡∣m∣) qnear(m)2.
∣qnear′′​(m)∣≪(log∣m∣)qnear​(m)2.

This is worse than a fixed constant, but still structured. Whether it is enough depends on the size of the value channel in the block statistic.

So this check says something important:

A2 with fixed ηη is probably too blunt.
But a logarithmically degrading version may be realistic.
8. What does this do to the route?

It splits the route in two possible ways.
Strong version

Prove Cnear(m)Cnear​(m) uniformly bounded.

That would be powerful, but it smells close to deep zero-location control.
Weaker version

Prove
Cnear(m)≪log⁡∣m∣
Cnear​(m)≪log∣m∣

or another slowly growing bound, and then check whether the block-statistic scaling can absorb it.

This is much more plausible.

So the exact thing to test next is not A2 as originally phrased. It is:
How large can Cnear(m) be on the admissible midpoints?
How large can Cnear​(m) be on the admissible midpoints?​

That is the sharp quantitative bottleneck.

over the zeros that actually dominate the local near part.

2. What zero-free region information gives

By the classical zero-free region, any nontrivial zero satisfies
min⁡(β,1−β)≳1log⁡(∣γ∣+3)

up to absolute constants.

1min⁡(β,1−β)≪log⁡(∣m∣+R+3).

Therefore
Cnear(m)≪log⁡(∣m∣+R+3).
Cnear​(m)≪log(∣m∣+R+3).​

This is immediate, since the weight 1/min⁡(β,1−β)1/min(β,1−β) can be bounded by the same logarithm for every zero in the local set, and then it factors out of the numerator.

So even without RH, the curvature factor grows at worst logarithmically.

That is much better than the fixed-ηη dream, and it is probably the right quantitative statement.

∣qnear′′(m)∣≪log⁡(∣m∣+R+3) qnear(m)2.

3. Does that logarithmic loss kill the route?

Not automatically.

It is much more believable than fixed-strip A2, because the only price paid is a logarithm.
6. What does this mean geometrically?

It means the route is naturally adapted to small local phase density.

is automatically smaller than the linear value term qζ(m)qζ​(m), even with the logarithmic loss.

So the route is not trying to control arbitrary high-density local configurations. It is strongest in sparse local regimes.

That is a useful clarification.

There are three reasons sparse midpoints should exist.
A. Between local zero clusters

Since each fρfρ​ decays quadratically away from its center, if mm sits in a region not especially close to any ordinate γρ/2γρ​/2, then S(m)S(m) should be modest. So sparse windows should exist between sharper local zero features.
B. Positivity helps choose them

Because all fρ≥0fρ​≥0, there is no delicate cancellation issue. To make S(m)S(m) small, you just place mm away from the strongest nearby contributors. That is much easier than controlling an oscillatory sum.
C. Local anomaly windows need not sit at peak centers

Your quartic anomaly is a local block effect. There is no obvious reason it must be tested exactly at the midpoint of maximal zero density. If the geometry is stable enough, one may be able to slide the midpoint slightly into a sparser location while preserving the local off-line signature.

So I think the sparse-midpoint condition is plausible.
3. But can the anomaly always be localized there?

This is the real unresolved part.

There are two possibilities.
Favorable scenario

The off-line quartic anomaly is a robust local geometric feature of a neighborhood, not just of one maximizer. Then you can shift to a nearby midpoint where S(m)S(m) is smaller, and the background-subtracted statistic is in the value-dominated regime.

If that is true, the route stays alive.
Bad scenario

The anomaly only becomes visible precisely where the local zero contribution is large, i.e. where S(m)S(m) is not small. Then the curvature channel remains competitive, and this version of the upper-bound route weakens badly.

If that is true, the route may fail.

FYI note: At u=0, the toy model has two double poles, one at 0+i/2 and one at d+i/2. The corresponding local jet spaces are two-dimensional: at the first center they are spanned by g_1(x)=(x+i/2)^{-1} and g_2(x)=(x+i/2)^{-2}, and at the second center by h_1(x)=(x-d+i/2)^{-1} and h_2(x)=(x-d+i/2)^{-2}. When the second double pole is symmetrically split by u, the natural even/odd local channels expand as
\eta_0(u)=h_1-u^2 h_3+O(u^4),\qquad
\eta_1(u)=h_2-u^2 h_4+O(u^4),
so the first nontrivial deformation appears at order u^2 and is carried by the genuinely new directions h_3 and h_4.

The key step is then to compute the baseline-whitened interaction matrix between the fixed first jet space and the moving second jet space. In the Cauchy/Hardy model, all pairings can be written explicitly using the confluent Cauchy formula
\langle g_m,h_n\rangle
=
i\,(-1)^{n-1}\binom{m+n-2}{m-1}(d+i)^{-(m+n-1)}.
From this one gets the raw cross matrix B(u)=B_0+u^2B_2+O(u^4), the moving Gram matrix G(u)=G_0+u^2H_2+O(u^4), and hence the order-u^2 coefficient in the whitened frame:
M(d)=B_2-\frac12\,B_0\,G_0^{-1}H_2.
A direct calculation gives an explicit 2\times2 matrix M(d), and its determinant simplifies to
\det M(d)
=
-\frac{i\,(d(1-i)+1)\,(d(1-i)+i)}{(d+i)^8}.
For every real d>0, neither factor in the numerator vanishes, so \det M(d)\neq 0. Therefore M(d)\neq 0 for all d>0.

Finally, the jet-limit quartic coefficient is the squared Frobenius norm of the corresponding order-u^2 coefficient, up to fixed invertible baseline changes of basis. Since invertible changes cannot turn a nonzero matrix into zero, M(d)\neq 0 implies
c_\infty(d)>0 \qquad \text{for every } d>0.
So the exceptional set
Z=\{d>0:c_\infty(d)=0\}
is empty. In short: the second-order split of the second double pole always produces a genuinely nontrivial interaction with the first jet space, and that forces the quartic coefficient to stay strictly positive for all separations d>0.

This is very good.

If the calculation is correct, it removes one of the last soft spots on the model side:
Z=∅,c∞(d)>0 for all d>0.
Z=∅,c∞​(d)>0 for all d>0.

That is a real upgrade over the earlier “discrete exceptional set” result.

What you’ve done is exactly the right kind of argument: not numerics, not asymptotics, but a structural jet-space computation in the confluent Cauchy model.

The logic is clean:

at u=0u=0, each local block is a 2D jet space coming from a double pole;
the symmetric split introduces a first nontrivial deformation at order u2u2;
that deformation lands in genuinely new directions h3,h4h3​,h4​;
the whitened interaction coefficient is an explicit 2×22×2 matrix M(d)M(d);
and your determinant formula shows M(d)≠0M(d)=0 for every real d>0d>0.

Since the quartic jet-limit coefficient is the squared Frobenius norm of that order-u2u2 coefficient up to invertible baseline changes of basis, nonvanishing of M(d)M(d) really does force
c∞(d)>0.

So yes: if the determinant computation is right, the exceptional set is gone.

where BζBζ​ is the explicit archimedean/trivial-zero background and SS is the positive zero contribution.

We then showed the right scale for comparing these two channels is microscopic:
s∼(log⁡∣m∣)−2.
s∼(log∣m∣)−2.

At that scale, the two-separation reconstruction remains analytically invertible, so the geometric part of the reduction does not collapse.

The main zeta-side theorem we got is a local curvature bound for the zero contribution:
∣S′′(m)∣≪L(m) S(m)2,L(m):=log⁡ ⁣(3+∣m∣+1S(m)).
∣S′′(m)∣≪L(m)S(m)2,L(m):=log(3+∣m∣+S(m)1​).

This comes from three ingredients:
the single Poisson-pair curvature bound,
the zero-free-region control on 1/min⁡(β,1−β)1/min(β,1−β),
and a near/far decomposition with the far tail optimized at the natural radius.

That is the central zeta-side result. It says the curvature channel is only quadratic in the local zero-density channel, up to a mild logarithmic loss.

As a consequence, at the microscopic scale the local zeta statistic becomes value-dominated rather than curvature-dominated. In other words, after all the renormalizations,
Ω^(s;m)≈U(s) S(m)
Ω
(s;m)≈U(s)S(m)

with the curvature term lower order in the regime that matters.

So the zeta-side conclusion is:

    the local statistic can be reduced to a background-subtracted zero-density channel S(m)S(m) plus a controlled curvature correction;

    the curvature correction is bounded by L(m)S(m)2L(m)S(m)2;

    the reconstruction machinery is still valid at the only scale where this comparison is favorable.

What remains unresolved is the final structural step: whether a value-dominated local zeta statistic can or cannot realize the same quartic jet-limit anomaly that the off-line model forces.

So the zeta-side result is not a proof of RH, but it is a sharp upper-bound framework:
we reduced the analytic difficulty to a single local density-vs-curvature inequality, proved that inequality with a logarithmic loss, and thereby isolated the last remaining issue as a structural incompatibility question rather than a tail-estimate problem.

Ok how do we prove it?

To prove it, I think you need to stop looking for one giant miracle and break the last step into three smaller statements, in order.

Right now the bottleneck is:
Ω^(s;m)=U(s) S(m)+O ⁣(L(m)S(m)2)
Ω
(s;m)=U(s)S(m)+O(L(m)S(m)2)

versus an off-line toy anomaly that is quartic and genuinely jet-structural.

So the proof route should be:
1. Prove a rigidity theorem for the value-only statistic

First ignore the quadratic remainder and study the map
S(m)⟼U(s) S(m).
S(m)⟼U(s)S(m).

This is rank-one in the scalar S(m)S(m): it can only move along the one-dimensional cone generated by the fixed matrix U(s)U(s).

By contrast, the off-line toy anomaly comes from a genuine u2u2-deformation of the jet-whitened cross block, and on the model side you now know that deformation is nonzero for every d>0d>0. So the first theorem to prove is:

    Value-rigidity theorem.
    A purely value-driven local statistic
    Ω^val(s;m)=U(s) S(m)
    Ω
    val​(s;m)=U(s)S(m)

    cannot realize the same second-order jet deformation as the off-line model.

This is the cleanest place to attack, because it is linear algebra, not zeta analysis.

Concretely, the toy quartic anomaly is governed by a nonzero 2×22×2 matrix M(d)M(d). Your value-driven zeta statistic lives in the span of U(s)U(s), which is one-dimensional in the scalar variable S(m)S(m). So the most natural thing to prove is:
M(d)∉span⁡{U(s):s>0}
M(d)∈/span{U(s):s>0}

for the admissible range of ss.

If you can prove that, then a purely value-driven zeta statistic is already structurally incompatible with the toy anomaly.

That is the first thing I would try to prove.
2. Show the quadratic remainder cannot repair the mismatch

Suppose Step 1 works, so the linear term U(s)S(m)U(s)S(m) cannot match the toy anomaly.

Then the only possible rescue would be the quadratic correction
E(s;m):=O ⁣(L(m)S(m)2).
E(s;m):=O(L(m)S(m)2).

So the next theorem should be quantitative:

    Quadratic smallness theorem.
    On the microscopic scale s∼(log⁡∣m∣)−2s∼(log∣m∣)−2, the quadratic correction is too small, in operator norm or Frobenius norm, to move U(s)S(m)U(s)S(m) onto the toy anomaly manifold.

In other words, you want something like
dist⁡(U(s)S(m),Mtoy)≫L(m)S(m)2,
dist(U(s)S(m),Mtoy​)≫L(m)S(m)2,

where MtoyMtoy​ is the family of off-line jet anomalies.

If that holds, the quadratic term cannot save the zeta side.

This is the exact place where the logarithmic curvature estimate gets used. You already have the upper bound. What remains is a transversality statement: the toy anomaly points in a direction that the value channel plus quadratic correction cannot reach.

So Step 2 is basically a perturbative stability argument around Step 1.
3. Prove the toy anomaly is genuinely two-dimensional

This is probably already implicit in your Cauchy/Hardy jet calculation, but I would isolate it as its own lemma.

You want to show that the model anomaly is not just “nonzero,” but that it carries a structural feature a value-only statistic cannot fake. The cleanest candidates are:

    nonzero determinant,

    nonzero traceless part,

    failure to commute with the baseline,

    or non-collinearity with U(s)U(s).

From what you wrote earlier, the determinant computation is especially strong:
det⁡M(d)≠0for all d>0.
detM(d)=0for all d>0.

A rank-one value statistic U(s)S(m)U(s)S(m) will not generically have that property in the same normalized frame. So the strongest proof route may be:

    show the zeta value channel produces matrices in a restricted class CvalCval​,

    show the toy anomaly matrix M(d)M(d) lies outside CvalCval​ for all d>0d>0,

    then show the quadratic correction stays inside a small neighborhood of CvalCval​.

That would give a contradiction cleanly.

I would aim for these three statements.
Theorem A: value-channel image is restricted

For microscopic ss,
Ω^val(s;m)=U(s)S(m)
Ω
val​(s;m)=U(s)S(m)

ranges over a one-parameter family of matrices with a specific invariant relation, for example

∣F(M(d))∣≥c(d)>0.

Yes — we can make that completely explicit, and it sharpens the final comparison a lot.

From
\Phi_K(M(d))=\frac{2\bigl(i(d^2-1)-d\bigr)}{(d+i)^4},
\qquad
\Phi_E(M(d))=\frac{d(3d^2+2id+5)}{(d+i)^5},
their absolute values are

\boxed{
|\Phi_K(M(d))|
=
\frac{2\sqrt{d^4-d^2+1}}{(d^2+1)^2}
}
and
\boxed{
|\Phi_E(M(d))|
=
\frac{d\sqrt{9d^4+34d^2+25}}{(d^2+1)^{5/2}}.
}

These are exact.

For \Phi_K, there is a very nice two-sided comparison. Since
d^4-d^2+1 \ge \frac{(d^2+1)^2}{4}
because
4(d^4-d^2+1)-(d^2+1)^2=3(d^2-1)^2\ge 0,
and also
d^4-d^2+1\le (d^2+1)^2,
we get
\boxed{
\frac{1}{d^2+1}\le |\Phi_K(M(d))|\le \frac{2}{d^2+1}.
}

So \Phi_K never gets small faster than d^{-2}, and in particular it is bounded below uniformly on every compact d-interval.

For \Phi_E, the exact formula already shows the right scale. Since
\sqrt{9d^4+34d^2+25}\ge 3d^2+5
and also
\sqrt{9d^4+34d^2+25}\le 3d^2+2d+5,
we get
\boxed{
\frac{d(3d^2+5)}{(d^2+1)^{5/2}}
\le
|\Phi_E(M(d))|
\le
\frac{d(3d^2+2d+5)}{(d^2+1)^{5/2}}.
}

So again, it is positive for every d>0, but unlike \Phi_K, it does go to 0 as d\to 0.

The asymptotics are:

as d\to 0,
|\Phi_K(M(d))|=2+O(d^2),
\qquad
|\Phi_E(M(d))|=5d+O(d^3),

and as d\to\infty,
|\Phi_K(M(d))|=\frac{2}{d^2}+O(d^{-4}),
\qquad
|\Phi_E(M(d))|=\frac{3}{d^2}+O(d^{-4}).

So the model-side transverse size is very explicit:
	•	\Phi_K stays order 1 near d=0 and decays like 2/d^2 for large d,
	•	\Phi_E is linear near d=0 and decays like 3/d^2 for large d.

This means the right uniform statement is not “bounded below for all d>0” — impossible for \Phi_E because of the 5d behavior near 0 — but:

on any compact interval d\in[d_-,d_+]\subset(0,\infty), both |\Phi_K(M(d))| and |\Phi_E(M(d))| admit positive lower bounds.

And that is exactly what you need for the zeta comparison on a fixed local-separation regime.

Is this a new zero free region result?

I’m checking this as a literature-positioning question, not just from our internal logic. The key distinction is whether it would count as an actual zero-free region theorem, or only as a conditional exclusion inside your local framework.

Not in its current form.

What you have now is better described as a conditional local exclusion principle inside your jet-statistic framework, not an unconditional new zero-free region for ζ(s)ζ(s).

The distinction matters.

A classical zero-free region is a theorem of the form
β≤1−η(γ)
β≤1−η(γ)

for every nontrivial zero β+iγβ+iγ, with η(γ)η(γ) explicit or asymptotic. The standard regions for ζζ are near ℜs=1ℜs=1, like de la Vallée Poussin / Korobov–Vinogradov type regions, and modern work still treats those as the benchmark notion of a “zero-free region.”

What your argument currently gives is closer to this:

    build a very specific local statistic from the scattering/jet setup;

    prove an upper bound on its transverse zeta-side component,
    ∣ΦK(Ω^ζ(s;m))∣≪L(m)S(m)2Q4+tail/error;
    ∣ΦK​(Ω
    ζ​(s;m))∣≪Q4L(m)S(m)2​+tail/error;

    compare that to the toy off-line model, where the same transverse channel is of size
    u2 ∣ΦK(M(d))∣+O(u4),u=β−12;
    u2∣ΦK​(M(d))∣+O(u4),u=β−21​;

    conclude that sufficiently large off-line displacement is incompatible with the zeta-side bound.

That is a real statement in principle, but it is not yet a standard zero-free region theorem, because it still depends on your local reconstruction framework, the value-dominance reduction, and the comparison between the toy anomaly and the actual zeta statistic. It does not yet say directly, unconditionally, “every zero satisfies ∣β−12∣≤…∣β−21​∣≤….”

More bluntly: at the moment it looks like
framework + proved bounds + one remaining structural incompatibility step⟹local exclusion of off-line pairs.
framework + proved bounds + one remaining structural incompatibility step⟹local exclusion of off-line pairs.

Until that last incompatibility step is proved in a way that applies to actual zeros, it is not a new zero-free region result.

Even if you do finish that step, what you are likeliest to get first is not a classical region near ℜs=1ℜs=1, but something more like a central exclusion band:
∣β−12∣ cannot exceed some height-dependent threshold
∣β−21​∣ cannot exceed some height-dependent threshold

for zeros that trigger your local anomaly. That would be unusual and potentially very interesting, but it would still need to be phrased and proved as a direct theorem about actual zeros before I’d call it a new zero-free region.

So my honest answer is:

    Right now: no, not yet a new zero-free region result.

    What it currently is: a promising, fairly sharp conditional exclusion mechanism.

    What it could become: a nonclassical zero-exclusion theorem near the critical line, if you can finish the final incompatibility step and state it directly for zeros.

If you want, the next useful thing is to formulate the strongest unconditional theorem you can already state from the current framework, without overselling it.

Is there any way to find this: a much stronger zeta-side transverse upper bound than O(LS^2/Q^4), ideally exact vanishing or something 

Yes, but not by squeezing harder on the same estimate.

If a much stronger transverse bound exists, it will almost certainly come from a structural cancellation, not from improving the crude inequalities you already used.

Right now your bound
∣ΦK(Ω^ζ)∣≪L S2Q4
∣ΦK​(Ω
ζ​)∣≪Q4LS2​

comes from three steps that all throw away structure:

    replace the true local zero contribution by an absolute-value curvature bound,

    replace sums of squares by square of sums,

    ignore the special form of ΦKΦK​, which kills the value channel exactly.

So the only realistic ways to get something much stronger are to exploit something that those steps destroyed.

There are four plausible attack routes.
1. Prove that ΦKΦK​ kills not only the value channel, but also part of the curvature channel

This is the most promising one.

Right now you have
Ω^(s;m)≈U(s)S(m)+V(s)S′′(m),ΦK(U(s)S(m))=0,
Ω
(s;m)≈U(s)S(m)+V(s)S′′(m),ΦK​(U(s)S(m))=0,

so
ΦK(Ω^(s;m))≈ΦK(V(s)) S′′(m).
ΦK​(Ω
(s;m))≈ΦK​(V(s))S′′(m).

And you computed
ΦK(V(s))=(−9Q+63)sin⁡Δˉ(s)Q3(3Q+6).
ΦK​(V(s))=Q3(3
​Q+6)(−9Q+63
​)sinΔˉ(s)​.

At microscopic scale s=κ/Q2s=κ/Q2, this is only O(Q−4)O(Q−4), which is why the current bound is what it is.

So the first serious question is:

Can you modify the statistic so that the new transverse functional kills both U(s)U(s) and the leading part of V(s)V(s)?

In other words, instead of one test functional ΦKΦK​, find a functional Φ∗Φ∗​ or a two-scale combination such that
Φ∗(U(s))=0,Φ∗(Vlead(s))=0.
Φ∗​(U(s))=0,Φ∗​(Vlead​(s))=0.

Then the transverse zeta signal would start one order later, possibly at something like
O ⁣(L S2Q5)or smaller.
O(Q5LS2​)or smaller.

This is the cleanest path to a stronger bound.

Concretely, because U(s)U(s) and the leading part of V(s)V(s) live in a very low-dimensional matrix span, you should try to build Φ∗Φ∗​ by using two or three separations and solving a linear annihilation problem. This is exactly analogous to how you eliminated nuisances earlier, but now done at the scalar-functional level.

So the first thing I would try is:

    compute the leading microscopic expansions of U(s)U(s) and V(s)V(s),

    identify the 2D or 3D subspace they generate,

    choose a functional orthogonal to that whole subspace.

If that subspace has codimension at least 1, you get a stronger transverse bound automatically.
2. Replace the crude bound on S′′S′′ by a signed cancellation identity

Your present estimate
∣S′′(m)∣≪L(m)S(m)2
∣S′′(m)∣≪L(m)S(m)2

comes from absolute values. That is robust, but wasteful.

A much stronger bound would come from showing that the specific linear combination entering ΦK(V(s))S′′(m)ΦK​(V(s))S′′(m) is not controlled by ∣S′′∣∣S′′∣, but by a signed sum with major cancellation.

The template would be:
ΦK(Ω^ζ)=∑ρcρ(m,s),
ΦK​(Ω
ζ​)=ρ∑​cρ​(m,s),

where the coefficients cρcρ​ are oscillatory or alternating enough that the sum is much smaller than the sum of absolute values.

This would be genuinely stronger than the LS2LS2 bound.

The problem is that nothing you’ve used so far forces such cancellation. So to get it, you would need a zeta-specific identity — probably from the Euler product, explicit formula on the prime side, or a relation between the scattering phase and a prime-weighted kernel.

This is harder, but it is the only route to anything like exact vanishing.
3. Build the statistic from a second derivative in ss, not from a fixed ss

This is a very plausible trick.

Right now ΦK(Ω^(s;m))ΦK​(Ω

∂sΩ^(s;m)orΩ^(s1;m)−Ω^(s2;m)
∂s​Ω

(s1​;m)−Ω

chosen to cancel the leading VV-term.

You already used two separations to reconstruct channels. Here, use them to eliminate the leading transverse zeta curvature term.

That is, look for coefficients a1,a2a1​,a2​ such that
a1 ΦK(V(s1))+a2 ΦK(V(s2))=0.
a1​ΦK​(V(s1​))+a2​ΦK​(V(s2​))=0.

a1​ΦK​(Ω

This is probably the most practical next experiment. It does not need any new arithmetic input, just better use of the geometry you already uncovered.
4. Try to prove exact vanishing from symmetry — but only after renormalizing more aggressively

Exact vanishing is unlikely for the current statistic. But it might happen for a more fully normalized one.

Right now you subtract:

    phase gap,

    background value.

Maybe the true transverse obstruction only appears after also subtracting:

    the local curvature jet predicted by the background-subtracted zero density,

    or the symmetric-square linear response.

In other words, the current statistic may still contain a “universal second-order” piece that has nothing to do with off-line anomalies. If you remove that too, exact vanishing on the zeta side becomes at least thinkable.

The right model is:
Ω^ren=Ω^−U(s)S(m)−Vuniv(s) C(m),

ΦK(Ω^ren)=0
ΦK​(Ω
ren​)=0

or is super-small.

This is more speculative, but if exact vanishing exists anywhere, it will be after one more renormalization like this.
My honest ranking

Most promising:

    annihilate the leading VV-term using a better transverse functional or a two/three-separation combination,

    use two separations to cancel the leading transverse scalar coefficient,

    search for a signed prime-side identity,

    hope for exact vanishing after deeper renormalization.

Least promising is just trying to improve constants in
∣S′′∣≪LS2.
∣S′′∣≪LS2.

That will not buy enough.

Is there an analogy to speriod prolate waves here?

Yes — I think there is a real analogy, though not an exact identification.

The cleanest way to say it is:

your statistic is starting to look like a concentration / leakage problem between a preferred finite-dimensional local jet space and its orthogonal complement.
That is very much the same kind of geometry that appears in prolate spheroidal wave theory.

Here is the analogy.

In the Slepian–Landau–Pollak / prolate picture, you have two incompatible notions of localization:
time concentration and band limitation. The interesting objects are the vectors that are as concentrated as possible in both senses. The key operator is a compressed projector, and the important quantities are its eigenvalues and the leakage into transverse directions.

In your setup, the two competing notions are not time and frequency, but something like:

    local jet concentration near a midpoint or pair of centers,

    global admissibility / automorphic phase structure coming from the zeta-side kernel.

Your jet-whitened cross block is a kind of compressed interaction operator between two local jet spaces. The toy off-line anomaly produces a deformation that leaks into a transverse direction. On the zeta side, after all the renormalizations, you are trying to show that the true object is almost confined to a very small structural subspace, with only small transverse leakage.

That is strongly reminiscent of prolate theory.

More concretely, the analogies are:

1. Local jet spaces ↔↔ time-limited subspaces.
Your 2D local jet spaces at each center play the role of finite-dimensional “localized” spaces.

2. Whitened cross block ↔↔ compressed concentration operator.
The matrix
Ω∞=M1−1/2N12M2−1/2
Ω∞​=M1−1/2​N12​M2−1/2​

is like a normalized compressed operator measuring how much one localized space talks to another.

3. Transverse obstruction ↔↔ spectral leakage.
The functionals ΦK,ΦEΦK​,ΦE​ are measuring components outside the preferred value-plane. That is exactly like measuring leakage outside the optimally concentrated subspace.

4. Microscopic scale tuning ↔↔ choosing the Shannon number regime.
Your choice
s∼(log⁡∣m∣)−2
s∼(log∣m∣)−2

is analogous to tuning the window/bandwidth scale so that the main concentration effect is visible and the nuisance terms are subordinate.

So yes: there is a genuine prolate-style analogy.

But there is also an important difference.

Classical prolate theory is linear spectral theory of a compact self-adjoint concentration operator. Your problem is more nonlinear and more local:

    you are not just diagonalizing one fixed operator;

    you are renormalizing, subtracting nuisance jets, and comparing families of compressed operators;

    and the decisive obstruction is not just an eigenvalue gap, but whether the zeta-side object can enter a transverse direction that the toy anomaly forces.

So the analogy is strongest at the level of operator geometry, not at the level of exact formulas.

If you wanted to import the analogy productively, I would look in two directions.

First, define the preferred zeta-side “admissible” subspace as the plane
P=span⁡{J,D},
P=span{J,D},

and think of the toy anomaly as a vector trying to leak out of PP. Then ΦK,ΦEΦK​,ΦE​ are the analog of concentration-loss coordinates.

Second, try to build a best-concentrated transverse test functional, the analog of a prolate eigenfunction: not just any ΦΦ killing the value channel, but the one that maximizes toy-side transverse response relative to zeta-side nuisance leakage.

That would be a very Slepian-like move.

So my answer is:

yes, there is a real prolate-wave analogy: compressed local spaces, optimal concentration, and transverse leakage.
It is not a literal PSWF problem, but it may be the right mental model for the last step.

Is mine maybe more promising?

Maybe — but not for the reason “it looks closer to RH than prolate theory.”

It may be more promising because it is much more tailored to the actual obstruction you care about.

Prolate theory is powerful when the real issue is optimal concentration under two competing linear constraints. Your setup has some of that geometry, but the hard core of your problem is more specific:

    an off-line toy anomaly forces a particular transverse jet deformation;

    the zeta side, after renormalization, seems constrained to a much smaller structural class;

    the remaining question is whether the zeta object can leak into that transverse direction strongly enough.

That is a more bespoke problem than a generic concentration problem. So a framework built from the exact jet spaces, exact whitening, exact transverse functionals ΦK,ΦEΦK​,ΦE​, and exact local scale s∼(log⁡∣m∣)−2s∼(log∣m∣)−2 has a real chance of being more relevant than importing a broad classical theory.

So yes: your approach may be more promising for this specific question, because it is adapted to the geometry of the anomaly rather than to a general abstract analogy.

But there is also a danger.

Custom frameworks often feel more promising because they are closer to the phenomenon — but they can also hide that the missing step is equivalent to a very hard arithmetic statement in disguise. And that is exactly where you are now. You have largely succeeded in stripping away the geometric fog. What remains is not “need a better analogy,” but a very concrete structural incompatibility theorem.

That is actually a good sign. It means the framework has done its job.

So my honest answer is:

    more promising than prolate theory as a loose analogy: yes;

    more promising than classical methods for actually proving RH: impossible to say yet;

    more promising than most speculative RH ideas because it has produced a theorem spine and a sharp bottleneck: yes, I think so.

What makes it serious is not that it feels deep. It is that it has narrowed the problem to a small number of explicit, falsifiable statements. That is rare and valuable.

If I were judging it as a research program, I’d say:
it is promising enough to keep pushing hard, but only if you keep demanding exact theorem statements and exact failure modes the way you’ve been doing.

4. What this buys

Conceptually, this is huge.

It means the zeta-side transverse channel looks like a low-order analytic function of the microscopic parameter κκ, while the toy anomaly looks like a κκ-independent constant at leading order.

That is exactly the situation where finite-difference annihilation works.

So in principle:

    1 separation: zeta O(Q−4)O(Q−4), toy survives.

    2 separations: cancel first zeta moment, improve by one order, toy survives.

    3 separations: cancel two zeta moments, improve by another order, toy survives.

    and so on.

This suggests a general strategy:
use an NN-separation moment-canceling combination to kill the first N−1N−1 zeta-side microscopic orders, while preserving the toy constant term through
∑j=1Naj≠0.
j=1∑N​aj​=0.

That is very promising.
5. The exact condition for the general NN-separation trick

Pick distinct κ1,…,κNκ1​,…,κN​, and choose coefficients ajaj​ such that
∑j=1Najκjr=0(r=1,…,N−1).
j=1∑N​aj​κjr​=0(r=1,…,N−1).

Then any zeta-side expansion beginning as a polynomial in κκ up to degree N−1N−1 is annihilated to that order.

The toy signal survives provided
∑j=1Naj≠0.
j=1∑N​aj​=0.

And for distinct κjκj​, the nullspace of the Vandermonde moment matrix has dimension 11, so such coefficients exist uniquely up to scale. Generically, their sum is nonzero.

So the whole game is now:
How many microscopic orders of the zeta-side transverse expansion can be canceled before some genuinely non-polynomial or toy-coupled term appears?
How many microscopic orders of the zeta-side transverse expansion can be canceled before some genuinely non-polynomial or toy-coupled term appears?​

That is the next real question.
6. My honest read

This is the first place where the route starts to look stronger than a one-shot estimate.

If the zeta-side transverse scalar really has a full asymptotic expansion in κκ, and the toy side remains constant in κκ at leading order, then this moment-canceling scheme could, in principle, drive the zeta-side upper bound arbitrarily low while keeping the toy obstruction alive.

That would be a major breakthrough in the logic of the framework.

The risk is that at some order, the zeta-side expansion stops being a clean polynomial-in-κκ object or the remainder blows up too badly. But structurally, the mechanism is real.

5. The real obstacles

Now the honest part. There are two serious obstacles before you can claim “iterate arbitrarily, therefore RH.”
Obstacle A: full asymptotic structure

You need the microscopic zeta-side transverse expansion to really continue in the expected odd-power pattern to arbitrarily high order, with coefficients controlled uniformly enough in mm.

For the first few orders, yes. To all orders, not yet proved.

This is probably true if the local renormalized statistic is analytic in Δˉ=κ/QΔˉ=κ/Q, because oddness would come from the sine-type structure. But that needs to be written carefully.
Obstacle B: growth of the combining coefficients

As NN grows, the nullvector ajaj​ of the odd-Vandermonde system can become badly conditioned. Even if the formal cancellation works, the coefficients may blow up rapidly with NN.

If the size of
∑j∣aj∣
j∑​∣aj​∣

or the norm of aa grows too fast, it can wipe out the Q−2N−2Q−2N−2 gain.

So the true next theorem is not just “a nullvector exists,” but:

    can one choose the κjκj​ so that the moment-killing coefficients grow at most moderately with NN?

That is now the crucial technical question.
6. The right way to test it

The smartest next test is not infinite NN. It is to compute the coefficient growth for a structured family of κjκj​.

Two natural choices:
Choice 1: symmetric arithmetic progression
κj=j,j=1,…,N.
κj​=j,j=1,…,N.
Choice 2: symmetric geometric or Chebyshev-type nodes

Pick κjκj​ to control Vandermonde conditioning, analogously to interpolation theory.

Then compute or estimate the unique nullvector aa satisfying
∑jajκj2r+1=0,r=0,…,N−2,
j∑​aj​κj2r+1​=0,r=0,…,N−2,

and normalize it so that
∑jaj=1.
j∑​aj​=1.

If the norm of aa grows only exponentially or polynomially in NN, the scheme may still be workable, because the Q−2N−2Q−2N−2 gain is extremely strong.

If the coefficients grow super-exponentially, the route may stall.

So the real next check is coefficient growth, not moment existence.

At the formal level, yes: the odd-moment cancellation iterates.

That is real.

The decisive next question is:
Can the odd-moment-canceling coefficients be chosen with controlled growth as N→∞?

If no, then the iteration is only a finite-order improvement scheme, not a route to RH.

So the next thing I would do is explicit coefficient growth analysis for the odd-Vandermonde nullvector. That is now the sharp technical bottleneck.

1. Why the expansion should be odd

This part is basically structural.

The statistic is built from symmetric placement
m−s2,m+s2,
m−2s​,m+2s​,

so swapping s↦−ss↦−s just exchanges the two local points. After the phase-gap subtraction and background-value subtraction, the remaining local object is analytic in ss, and the functional ΦKΦK​ kills the even value-plane. That is why the first few computed terms came out as
κ, κ3, κ5,…
κ, κ3, κ5,…

only.

So the natural theorem is:

    For each admissible midpoint mm, the renormalized transverse scalar
    Hm(s)=ΦK(Ω^ζ(s;m))
    Hm​(s)=ΦK​(Ω
    ζ​(s;m))

    is an odd analytic function of ss near s=0s=0.

Hm​(s)=r≥0∑​b2r+1​(m)s2r+1.

1. Matrix-level symmetry under s↦−ss↦−s

Under symmetric placement,

x−​(−s)=x+​(s),x+​(−s)=x−​(s).

At the raw 2×22×2 local-block level, this is implemented by the swap matrix
P=(0110).
P=(01​10​).

So before whitening and subtraction, every two-point matrix X(s)X(s) built from the symmetric pair satisfies
X(−s)=P X(s) P.
X(−s)=PX(s)P.

That is just the statement “changing the sign of ss exchanges the two points.”
2. What this does after jet normalization

The jet-normalized statistics are obtained from the two-point data by a fixed local linear change of basis of midpoint/difference type. In the notation we used earlier, this is the passage to the jet basis. Under point-swap, the value direction is even and the derivative direction is odd.

That means that in jet coordinates, the swap matrix becomes
J0:=(100−1).
J0​:=(10​0−1​).

So after the jet-basis change, every local 2×22×2 block satisfies
X~(−s)=J0 X~(s) J0.

(−s)=(a(s)−c(s)​−b(s)d(s)​).

So the diagonal entries are even in ss, and the off-diagonal entries are odd in ss.

That is the core structural fact.
3. Why the subtractions preserve this parity split

Now check the two renormalizations.

The phase-gap subtraction is built from a scalar phase difference between the two symmetric points. That phase difference changes sign under swapping the points, so the correction term it induces is compatible with the same parity rule: it contributes to the odd off-diagonal sector, not the even value-plane sector.

The background-value subtraction is
U(s) Bζ(m).
U(s)Bζ​(m).

Here Bζ(m)Bζ​(m) depends only on the midpoint mm, not on the sign of ss. Also U(s)U(s) lies in the value-plane PP, and in jet coordinates its contribution is even under s↦−ss↦−s. So subtracting it removes part of the even sector and does not affect oddness of the transverse channel.

7. What controls the radius

There are really only two plausible obstructions.

One is a singularity of the local zeta/scattering phase itself near the midpoint. Since we are working with the critical-line phase of completed ξξ-type data, the natural obstruction scale should be tied to local zero spacing, which is of order Q−1Q−1. That is why the target radius Q−1Q−1 is plausible.

The other is a degeneracy in the whitening or normalization matrices. But earlier we already checked that the microscopic two-separation reconstruction stays invertible, albeit with polynomial conditioning. That suggests the normalization does not collapse before the natural Q−1Q−1 scale.

    oddness is now structurally clear,

1. What could cause a singularity?

There are really only two sources.

First, the underlying phase data can become singular when the complexified symmetric points
m±s2
m±2s​

approach a zero or pole of the meromorphic object behind the scattering phase.

Second, the normalization/whitening matrices could become singular if the local Gram blocks degenerate.

We already have good evidence the second one is not the true obstruction: the microscopic reconstruction stayed invertible, only polynomially ill-conditioned. So the genuine radius should be governed by the first source: distance to the nearest singularity of the complexified local phase data.

So the theorem we want is really a nearest-singularity lower bound.
2. What is the natural scale of the nearest singularity?

Near height mm, the natural zero spacing scale is
≍1log⁡∣m∣=1Q.
≍log∣m∣1​=Q1​.

So the problem becomes:
Can the renormalized local statistic be arranged so that its nearest singularity is at distance ≫Q−1 from s=0?
Can the renormalized local statistic be arranged so that its nearest singularity is at distance ≫Q−1 from s=0?​
3. Why renormalization matters

Without renormalization, the local object would inherit poles directly from nearby zeros, and the radius would be exactly controlled by the nearest one.

But your statistic is already heavily renormalized:

    phase-gap subtraction,

    background-value subtraction,

    jet whitening,

    then projection by ΦKΦK​.

That means many “obvious” local singular pieces may cancel before they ever reach Hm(s)Hm​(s).

So the analyticity radius of HmHm​ may be larger than the naive nearest-zero distance of the raw phase itself.

This is why the radius question is genuinely nontrivial and worth checking carefully.
4. The right local model for the radius

Take the local zero contribution near mm:
S(t)=∑ρfρ(t).
S(t)=ρ∑​fρ​(t).

Complexify tt. A single pair contribution has singularities at
2t−γ=±iβand2t−γ=±i(1−β).
2t−γ=±iβand2t−γ=±i(1−β).

12(2m−γ)2+β2or12(2m−γ)2+(1−β)2.
21​(2m−γ)2+β2
​or21​(2m−γ)2+(1−β)2
​.

At t=mt=m, if γγ is near 2m2m, this is controlled by ββ or 1−β1−β, which is O(1)O(1), not O(Q−1)O(Q−1). So the complex singularity from a single local Poisson pair is actually not microscopically close.

This is encouraging.

It suggests the raw Poisson-pair model itself has plenty of analyticity room in ss, at least locally.

So the likely obstruction is not the poles of individual Poisson kernels. It is the accumulation of nearby zeros in the full zeta-side object.

But since the local zero spacing is Q−1Q−1, a radius of order Q−1Q−1 still looks realistic.
5. What the whitening can do

Now consider the local Gram blocks and their whitening.

At s=0s=0, the point pair collapses to a jet space, not a singular object. The microscopic expansions already showed that the whitened objects have finite jet limits. So the normalization is removing the raw s−1s−1-type blowups associated with collapsing point samples.

That means the whitening is not introducing a new microscopic singularity at s=0s=0. Instead, it is regularizing the collision.

This is very good news. It means s=0s=0 is a regular point of the renormalized statistic, exactly as the odd Taylor expansion picture suggests.

So the analytic radius is determined by what happens away from 00, not by the collision itself.
6. The exact lemma to prove

The right next lemma is this.

    Lemma (local renormalized analyticity radius).
    For each admissible midpoint mm, there exists rm>0rm​>0 such that the renormalized matrix
    Ω^ζ(s;m)
    Ω
    ζ​(s;m)

    is analytic for ∣s∣<rm∣s∣<rm​. Moreover,
    rm≳Q−1,Q≍log⁡∣m∣.
    rm​≳Q−1,Q≍log∣m∣. 

Once that is proved, oddness gives
Hm(s)=ΦK(Ω^ζ(s;m))
Hm​(s)=ΦK​(Ω
ζ​(s;m))

odd analytic in the same disk, and then the entire NN-point cancellation machine becomes legitimate.

So this lemma is now the main technical gate.
7. How I would try to prove it

I would not attack the full renormalized matrix at once. I would factor the proof into three layers.
Layer A: raw local kernel analyticity

Show that the underlying local kernel entries built from the phase/scattering data are analytic in ss for
∣s∣<c/Q.
∣s∣<c/Q.

This should come from the local zero-spacing scale and the absence of singularities closer than that in the complexified symmetric configuration.
Layer B: local Gram analyticity and invertibility

Show that the local Gram blocks remain analytic and invertible in the same disk. Since the jet-limit at s=0s=0 is nonsingular, this should hold on a small enough disk by continuity.
Layer C: subtraction terms preserve analyticity

Show that the phase-gap subtraction and background-value subtraction are analytic in the same disk.

Then Ω^ζΩ
ζ​ is analytic there.

This is a much more modular attack than trying to reason about the whole thing in one shot.
8. What may still fail

The real danger is not oddness anymore. It is that the analytic radius in ss might shrink faster than Q−1Q−1 for some pathological midpoints because the full zeta object feels nearby zeros more strongly than the Poisson-pair heuristic suggests.

If that happens, the all-order cancellation scheme might only be available to bounded finite order, not uniformly in NN.

So the decisive test is:
Does the renormalized local matrix stay analytic on the natural local-spacing scale Q−1?
Does the renormalized local matrix stay analytic on the natural local-spacing scale Q−1?​

If yes, the odd-moment machine is real.
If no, the framework may still give finite-order exclusion results but probably not RH.
9. My honest assessment

Oddness now looks essentially done.

The analyticity radius is the first place where the route again feels genuinely delicate. But it is also a very cleanly posed local question now, not a vague global RH mystery.

So the next exact mathematical target is:

    prove regularity at s=0s=0,

    prove analyticity for ∣s∣<c/Q∣s∣<c/Q,

    then write the odd analytic expansion and plug it into the NN-point cancellation coefficients.

That is the right continuation.

2. The full zero sum should be analytic in the same disk

Now the real issue is not one term but the sum over zeros.

For ∣s∣<c/Q∣s∣<c/Q, each term remains away from its poles by a definite fraction of its distance-to-pole scale, so the terms and their ss-derivatives satisfy the same kind of shell bounds we were already using on the real line, just with slightly inflated constants.

The crucial point is that in such a disk, the denominators are bounded below by
(2m−γ)2+c2/Q2
(2m−γ)2+c2/Q2

up to constants, so the series over zeros still looks like a weighted

That is the first genuine analytic lemma.
3. The local Gram blocks stay invertible there

The next issue is the whitening.

At s=0s=0, the symmetric two-point block collapses to the jet-limit Gram matrix, and earlier calculations showed that this limit is finite and nondegenerate.

So let Gm(s)Gm​(s) denote the relevant local Gram block in jet coordinates. Then:

    Gm(s)Gm​(s) is analytic in ∣s∣<c/Q∣s∣<c/Q,

    Gm(0)Gm​(0) is invertible,

    therefore, by continuity, Gm(s)Gm​(s) remains invertible on a smaller disk
    ∣s∣<c1/Q
    ∣s∣<c1​/Q

    for some absolute c1>0c1​>0, as long as the admissible midpoint class keeps Gm(0)Gm​(0) uniformly away from singularity.

This is the second real lemma.

Once that holds, the analytic inverse
Gm(s)−1/2
Gm​(s)−1/2

exists in the same disk, so the whitening preserves analyticity.
4. The subtraction terms are analytic too

The phase-gap subtraction depends on the phase difference between the symmetric points. Since the phase derivative is analytic in the same local disk, the integrated phase-gap term is also analytic there.

The background-value subtraction
U(s)Bζ(m)
U(s)Bζ​(m)

is even simpler: Bζ(m)Bζ​(m) is just a scalar depending on mm, and U(s)U(s) is built from the same analytic local jet data, so it is analytic in ss.

So all renormalization pieces preserve the same c/Qc/Q-disk.

Putting these together gives the theorem sketch:
Ω^ζ(s;m) is analytic for ∣s∣<c1/Q.
Ω
ζ​(s;m) is analytic for ∣s∣<c1​/Q.​

And from the earlier parity argument,
Hm(s)=ΦK(Ω^ζ(s;m)) is odd analytic for ∣s∣<c1/Q.
Hm​(s)=ΦK​(Ω
ζ​(s;m)) is odd analytic for ∣s∣<c1​/Q.​

That is the exact statement needed for the all-order odd-moment cancellation machine.

6. So where is the real risk now?

Not oddness.
Not coefficient growth.
Not the existence of the cancellation coefficients.

The real risk is whether the full renormalized local matrix really admits a uniform c/Qc/Q-disk with the needed boundary bound. That is the only serious technical point left in the all-order scheme.

But importantly, this is now a very specific local analytic statement, not a vague global RH mystery.

1. Oddness is basically done

This part is structural.

Under symmetric placement,
m−s2,m+s2,
m−2s​,m+2s​,

the sign change s↦−ss↦−s swaps the two points. In jet coordinates, that is conjugation by
J0=(100−1).

So oddness is not the bottleneck.
2. The right way to attack analyticity

Do not start from the full global ξ′/ξξ′/ξ. Start from the local renormalized matrix entries.

There are three layers:
Layer A: raw local kernel entries

Each raw entry is built from the phase/scattering kernel evaluated at the symmetric pair. For fixed mm, these are meromorphic in ss. Their singularities come from the complexified symmetric points hitting poles of the underlying local meromorphic data.
Layer B: jet normalization / whitening

At s=0s=0, the two-point configuration collapses to a jet limit, but earlier calculations already showed this limit is finite and nondegenerate. So the apparent collision singularity is removable after passing to the jet frame.
Layer C: renormalization terms

Phase-gap subtraction and background-value subtraction are built from the same local meromorphic data, so they preserve analyticity wherever the raw local pieces are analytic.

So the actual issue is not “is HmHm​ analytic?” but:
Are there singularities of the raw local meromorphic data within distance ≍Q−1 of s=0?
Are there singularities of the raw local meromorphic data within distance ≍Q−1 of s=0?​

with coefficient bounds

∣c2r+1​(m)∣≪ρ0​​L(m)S(m)2ρ0−(2r+1)​.​

This makes the odd-moment cancellation machine rigorous.
Proposition 5. NN-point odd-moment cancellation

Take nodes

Final comparison

Combining the two sides gives

So the entire framework now reduces to just one final gap:
Make the analyticity-radius and boundary-size propositions fully rigorous, with uniform constants.
Make the analyticity-radius and boundary-size propositions fully rigorous, with uniform constants.​

Everything after that is algebra.

The exact next move is to write Proposition 1 carefully and prove it with constants, then propagate those constants through Propositions 2–4. Once that is done, the whole conditional package becomes a real theorem chain.

Can we fix that gap?

Maybe, but this is now the place where honesty matters most: this gap looks fixable in principle, but it is exactly the sort of technical gap that can still hide a fatal loss.

So I would not say “yes, we can fix it.” I would say:
The gap has been reduced to a concrete local analytic package that looks attackable.
The gap has been reduced to a concrete local analytic package that looks attackable.​

That is a strong statement. It is not the same as having fixed it.

Here is how I would judge it.
What looks genuinely fixable

The gap is no longer some mysterious RH-strength global input. It is now a chain of local facts:

    denominator comparability on the circle ∣s∣=ρ0/Q∣s∣=ρ0​/Q,

    transport of the near/far estimates to that circle,

    uniform invertibility of the jet Gram block on that same disk,

    Cauchy bounds for the odd analytic expansion.

Items 1 and 2 look like hard analysis, but standard hard analysis.
Item 3 looks like local linear algebra and continuity.
Item 4 is then automatic.

That is why I think the package is plausibly fixable.
What could still go wrong

There are three real risks.

First, the complexified near/far comparability may lose more than a constant. If the complex denominators are only comparable with a factor that secretly grows with QQ, then the whole Q−3Q−3 boundary bound weakens, and the NN-point machine may stop being strong enough.

Second, the admissible midpoint class may not be uniform enough. The phrase “uniform in mm” can hide all kinds of trouble. A small bad subset of midpoints with poor local geometry could be enough to break the global contradiction.

Third, even if the radius is c/Qc/Q, the boundary norm may be too large in exactly the wrong way. The all-order scheme needs not just analyticity, but analyticity with the right scale. If the boundary is Q−2Q−2 instead of Q−3Q−3, or if the constants in ΘN(ρ0)ΘN​(ρ0​) are worse than expected, the exponential win may disappear.

So the gap is not cosmetic. It is the kind of gap that can decide the whole project.
The right way to try to fix it

I would not attack the whole package at once. I would try to prove the following four lemmas in this order.
Lemma 1

For each local Poisson-pair denominator,
∣(x+s)2+a2∣≍x2+a2(∣s∣=ρ0/Q),
∣(x+s)2+a2∣≍x2+a2(∣s∣=ρ0​/Q),

with constants depending only on ρ0ρ0​.

This one should be fully provable now.
Lemma 2

The same comparability lifts to the full near/far decomposition, giving
∣Cm(s)∣≪ρ0L(m)S(m)2(∣s∣=ρ0/Q).
∣Cm​(s)∣≪ρ0​​L(m)S(m)2(∣s∣=ρ0​/Q).

This is the most important analytic lemma.
Lemma 3

The jet Gram block Gm(s)Gm​(s) stays uniformly invertible on ∣s∣<ρ0/Q∣s∣<ρ0​/Q.

This looks favorable because the s=0s=0 limit is strongly positive.
Lemma 4

Combine Lemmas 2 and 3 with the explicit ΦK(V(s))ΦK​(V(s)) formula to get
∣Hm(s)∣≪ρ0L(m)S(m)2Q3(∣s∣=ρ0/Q).
∣Hm​(s)∣≪ρ0​​Q3L(m)S(m)2​(∣s∣=ρ0​/Q).

Then the rest is Cauchy and algebra.

That is the smallest realistic “fix the gap” program.
My best assessment

So: can we fix the gap?

Possibly yes.
And more importantly, we now know exactly what “fixing it” means.

If I had to put probabilities on it, I’d say:

    proving a local c/Qc/Q analyticity radius with some bound: plausible;

    getting exactly the Q−3Q−3 boundary size with uniform constants: plausible but delicate;

    having the whole package survive without a hidden logarithmic or polynomial loss that ruins the NN-point machine: uncertain.

That last part is why I would not say the gap is fixed.
What I would do next, concretely

I would now stop all higher-level discussion and write Lemma 1 carefully with constants, then Lemma 2 carefully with constants. If those two go through cleanly, then the gap is genuinely shrinking. If either one picks up the wrong QQ-dependence, we will know immediately that this route is weaker than it looks.

So yes, it may be fixable — and the good news is that the only honest way to find out is now very concrete.

(xρ2+aρ2)−1.
(xρ2​+aρ2​)−1.

By Lemma 1, on the complex circle these denominators are comparable, up to ρ0ρ0​-dependent constants, to the real ones. So the same derivation goes through with a larger constant. ∎
Lemma 3. Complexified near/far transport

Fix R≥1R≥1, and split the zeros into
∣2m−γρ∣≤Rand∣2m−γρ∣>R.
∣2m−γρ​∣≤Rand∣2m−γρ​∣>R.

Then on ∣s∣=ρ0/Q∣s∣=ρ0​/Q,
∣Cnear(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0LR(m) S(m)2,LR(m):=log⁡(3+∣m∣+R),
∣Cnear​(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0​​LR​(m)S(m)2,LR​(m):=log(3+∣m∣+R),

and
∣Cfar(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0LR(m)R3.
∣Cfar​(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0​​R3LR​(m)​.
Proof

For the near part, Lemma 2 gives
∣fρ(2)(m;s)∣≪ρ01min⁡(βρ,1−βρ) fρ(m)2.
∣fρ(2)​(m;s)∣≪ρ0​​min(βρ​,1−βρ​)1​fρ​(m)2.

By the zero-free region,
1min⁡(βρ,1−βρ)≪log⁡(3+∣γρ∣).
min(βρ​,1−βρ​)1​≪log(3+∣γρ​∣).

On the near set, ∣γρ∣≤2∣m∣+R∣γρ​∣≤2∣m∣+R, so
1min⁡(βρ,1−βρ)≪LR(m).
min(βρ​,1−βρ​)1​≪LR​(m).

Summing and using ∑fρ(m)2≤(∑fρ(m))2∑fρ​(m)2≤(∑fρ​(m))2,
∣Cnear(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0LR(m) S(m)2.
∣Cnear​(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0​​LR​(m)S(m)2.

For the far part, Lemma 1 transports the real estimate
∣fρ′′(m)∣≪∣2m−γρ∣−4
∣fρ′′​(m)∣≪∣2m−γρ​∣−4

to the complex circle:
∣fρ(2)(m;s)∣≪ρ0∣2m−γρ∣−4.
∣fρ(2)​(m;s)∣≪ρ0​​∣2m−γρ​∣−4.

Summing over shells with standard zero counting yields
∣Cfar(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0LR(m)R3.
∣Cfar​(m,s;R)∣≪ρ0​​R3LR​(m)​.

∎
Corollary 4. Complexified curvature bound

Choose
R≍1S(m)log⁡ ⁣(3+∣m∣+1S(m)).
R≍S(m)1​log(3+∣m∣+S(m)1​).

Then
∣Cm(s)∣≪ρ0L(m) S(m)2(∣s∣=ρ0/Q).
∣Cm​(s)∣≪ρ0​​L(m)S(m)2(∣s∣=ρ0​/Q).
Proof

With that choice of RR, the far term is
≪S(m)3L(m)2,
≪L(m)2S(m)3​,

which is lower order than L(m)S(m)2L(m)S(m)2. Combine with Lemma 3. ∎
2. What this gives immediately

If the renormalized local block is holomorphic on ∣s∣<ρ0/Q∣s∣<ρ0​/Q, then the boundary estimate follows at once.

Indeed, since ΦKΦK​ kills the value channel exactly,
Hm(s)=ΦK(Ω^ζ(s;m))
Hm​(s)=ΦK​(Ω
ζ​(s;m))

only sees the curvature channel. Using the explicit formula
ΦK(V(s))=(−9Q+63)sin⁡Δˉ(s)Q3(3Q+6),
ΦK​(V(s))=Q3(3
​Q+6)(−9Q+63
​)sinΔˉ(s)​,

and ∣Δˉ(s)∣≪ρ01∣Δˉ(s)∣≪ρ0​​1 on ∣s∣=ρ0/Q∣s∣=ρ0​/Q, we get
∣ΦK(V(s))∣≪ρ0Q−3.
∣ΦK​(V(s))∣≪ρ0​​Q−3.

Together with Corollary 4,
∣Hm(s)∣≪ρ0L(m) S(m)2Q3(∣s∣=ρ0/Q),

provided HmHm​ is indeed holomorphic there.
